=== USER ===
You are a senior distributed systems and infrastructure engineer.

I have an online coding assessment platform where:
- Up to 1,000 concurrent submissions can be received simultaneously
- Each submission must be evaluated against up to 1,000 test cases
- This means up to 1,000,000 total code execution jobs can be in-flight at peak load

The platform uses:
- Judge0 (or a custom Docker-based code execution engine) for sandboxed code running
- AWS (Lambda, S3, API Gateway) or similar cloud infra
- A relational database (MySQL) for storing submissions, results, test cases
- WebSockets or polling for real-time result delivery
- A Node.js/React backend and frontend

━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━

YOUR TASK:

Perform a full-spectrum failure analysis across every layer of the system. For each failure domain below, identify:
1. The exact bottleneck or failure point
2. Why it fails at 1,000 submissions × 1,000 test cases
3. The symptom the user or system will observe
4. A concrete mitigation or architectural fix

━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━

FAILURE DOMAINS TO ANALYZE:

1. CODE EXECUTION ENGINE
   - Judge0 worker concurrency limits (max_queue, max_processes_per_worker)
   - Docker container spin-up time and container pool exhaustion
   - CPU throttling: each sandbox gets limited vCPUs — what happens under 1M jobs?
   - Memory per container × concurrent containers = RAM ceiling calculation
   - OOM (Out of Memory) kills — what gets silently dropped?
   - Execution timeout accumulation — if each job takes 2s, how long does the queue drain?

2. JOB QUEUE & WORKER INFRASTRUCTURE
   - Queue depth limits (Redis/BullMQ/SQS) — what happens when queue overflows?
   - Dead letter queue handling — are failed jobs retried, lost, or duplicated?
   - Worker starvation — slow jobs blocking fast ones (no priority lanes)
   - Horizontal scaling lag — auto-scaling triggers too late during burst traffic
   - Backpressure: does the API accept all 1,000 submissions instantly with no throttle?

3. DATABASE (MySQL)
   - Write storm: 1,000 submissions × 1,000 results = 1,000,000 INSERT/UPDATE rows
   - Connection pool exhaustion (max_connections limit hit)
   - Lock contention on submission/result tables during concurrent writes
   - Index performance degradation on bulk reads (fetching all 1,000 test case results per submission)
   - Long-running transactions blocking reads
   - Disk I/O saturation during bulk writes
   - What happens if the DB goes down mid-evaluation — are partial results saved?

4. API LAYER & HTTP
   - Rate limiting: is there any protection against 1,000 simultaneous POST /submit requests?
   - API Gateway or load balancer connection limits
   - Request timeout: does the HTTP layer time out before code execution completes?
   - Webhook/callback delivery failures — if Judge0 calls back and the endpoint is down
   - Payload size: are test case inputs/outputs being sent over HTTP on every job (anti-pattern)?

5. REAL-TIME DELIVERY (WebSockets / Polling)
   - WebSocket connection count: 1,000 open connections per assessment session
   - Memory per WebSocket connection on the server
   - Event fan-out: broadcasting 1,000,000 result events to the right users
   - Polling storm: if using polling, 1,000 clients hitting /status every 2s = 500 req/s sustained
   - Message ordering: do results arrive out-of-order? Does the frontend handle this?

6. FILE STORAGE (S3 or equivalent)
   - Code file upload: 1,000 submissions × source code = 1,000 PUT operations
   - Test case input/output stored in S3 vs DB — which is fetched per execution job?
   - S3 request rate limits (3,500 PUT/s, 5,500 GET/s per prefix)
   - Cold reads: if test case files are large and fetched fresh per job, bandwidth explodes

7. MEMORY & CPU (SERVER-SIDE)
   - Node.js event loop blocking under high concurrency (CPU-bound orchestration code)
   - Heap memory: keeping 1,000,000 result objects in memory before DB flush
   - CPU cores available vs. concurrent async operations
   - GC pressure under sustained load causing latency spikes

8. OBSERVABILITY & FAILURE RECOVERY
   - Are partial results (500/1000 test cases done) persisted if the system crashes?
   - Is there idempotency on job submission — can duplicate jobs be created on retry?
   - Is there a circuit breaker if the execution engine is overwhelmed?
   - How are silent failures detected — a job that never completes, never errors?
   - Alerting: what metrics (queue depth, p99 latency, error rate) are being tracked?

9. SECURITY & ISOLATION UNDER LOAD
   - Resource exhaustion attacks: can one submission with an infinite loop starve others?
   - Docker privileged mode risks under high concurrency
   - Network isolation between sandboxes — can a container reach internal services?

10. UX & PRODUCT FAILURE MODES
    - What does the user see if their result takes 10+ minutes?
    - What if 200 of 1,000 test cases silently fail with no result written?
    - Is there a timeout after which a submission is marked "failed to evaluate"?

━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━

DELIVERABLES:
- A prioritized list of failure points (P0 = system crash, P1 = data loss, P2 = degraded UX)
- Architecture diagram or description of the recommended target state
- Specific configuration values to tune (Judge0 workers, DB connection pool, queue concurrency)
- Code-level changes needed (batching, backpressure, idempotency keys, circuit breakers)
- A load testing plan to validate the fixes before going to production

# Full-Spectrum Failure Analysis: Judge0 Autograder at 1,000 × 1,000 Scale

## Critical Architectural Clarification First

Before any failure analysis, the **actual concurrency model** must be understood — it changes every calculation:

```
Naive assumption:  1,000 submissions × 1,000 TCs = 1,000,000 Judge0 jobs
Actual design:     1,000 submissions × 1 harness  = 1,000 Judge0 jobs
                   Each harness runs 1,000 TCs in parallel via fork()/threads INSIDE one container
```

This is a crucial design win but it also concentrates failure modes. Instead of 1M shallow jobs, you have 1,000 heavyweight jobs, each spawning 1,000 child processes or threads. The bottlenecks are fundamentally different — and several of them are code-level bugs, not just configuration problems.

---

## Prioritized Failure Registry

| Priority | ID | Domain | Description |
|---|---|---|---|
| **P0** | F-01 | Execution Engine | `select()` FD_SETSIZE=1024 hard limit in C/C++ harnesses with 1,000 TCs |
| **P0** | F-02 | Execution Engine | `SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT` truncates harnesses with 1,000 embedded TCs |
| **P0** | F-03 | Execution Engine | RLIMIT_NPROC exhaustion cascades to sequential fallback → 33-minute freeze |
| **P0** | F-04 | Worker Infra | 6 concurrent sandboxes drain 1,000 jobs in ~19 minutes |
| **P1** | F-05 | Execution Engine | File descriptor exhaustion at 2,000 FDs/harness × concurrent jobs |
| **P1** | F-06 | Execution Engine | Container memory ceiling: 1,000 fork()ed children can OOM-kill the container |
| **P1** | F-07 | API Layer | Puma 80-thread ceiling against 1,000 simultaneous POST /submit requests |
| **P1** | F-08 | API Layer | No idempotency: retried submissions create duplicate jobs, polluting results |
| **P1** | F-09 | API Layer | Callback server spawns 1,000 threads → 1GB stack allocation + GIL contention |
| **P1** | F-10 | DB | Poll storm: 1,000 pollers × 2 req/s = 2,000 req/s into 80-connection Puma |
| **P2** | F-11 | DB | `judge0.conf` missing `SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT` override → silent truncation |
| **P2** | F-12 | Real-time | `grade()` is synchronous and blocking — platform must manage 1,000 threads |
| **P2** | F-13 | Observability | Zero circuit-breaker, zero metrics, zero dead-letter queue |
| **P2** | F-14 | Security | `memory_limit_mb` is caller-supplied with no upper bound validation |
| **P2** | F-15 | UX | No progress events; users see a spinner for up to 19 minutes |

---

## Domain 1: Code Execution Engine

### F-01 (P0) — `select()` blows up with >1,024 test cases

**Exact location**: [harnesses/c_harness.c](harnesses/c_harness.c) and [harnesses/cpp_harness.cpp](harnesses/cpp_harness.cpp), the `_build_c_parallel_runner()` / `_build_cpp_parallel_runner()` functions in [core/harness_builder.py](core/harness_builder.py).

**Why it fails**: `select()` uses a `fd_set` bitmask with a compile-time limit of `FD_SETSIZE = 1024`. Your harness creates one pipe per test case. With 1,000 TCs you open FDs 3..2006 (stdin/stdout/stderr are 0–2). Any FD ≥ 1024 passed to `FD_SET()` causes undefined behavior — in practice it corrupts adjacent memory, causing the harness to hang or produce garbage output. The compiler will not warn about this.

**Symptom**: All TCs silently MISSING in the `ParsedSubmission`. The DONE marker never prints. The platform reports every TC as TLE because `global_tle=True`.

**Verification**: 
```bash
python3 -c "
from core.harness_builder import HarnessBuilder, HarnessConfig, TestCase
cfg = HarnessConfig(
    student_code='int solve(int x){return x;}',
    test_cases=[TestCase(inputs=[i], expected=i) for i in range(1000)],
    language='c', mode='function',
    param_types=['int'], return_type='int',
    per_tc_limit_s=2, memory_limit_mb=256
)
h = HarnessBuilder(cfg)
src = h.build()
print('FD risk:', '1024+ FDs will be opened' if len(cfg.test_cases) > 512 else 'safe')
"
```

**Fix**: Replace every `select()` call with `poll()` in both C and C++ harness templates. `poll()` has no FD limit:

```c
// BEFORE (in c_harness.c parallel runner template)
fd_set read_fds;
FD_ZERO(&read_fds);
for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) FD_SET(pipe_fds[i][0], &read_fds);
struct timeval tv = {deadline_s, 0};
int ready = select(max_fd+1, &read_fds, NULL, NULL, &tv);

// AFTER
struct pollfd pfds[{N_TCS}];
for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
    pfds[i].fd = pipe_fds[i][0];
    pfds[i].events = POLLIN;
}
int timeout_ms = (int)(deadline_s * 1000);
int ready = poll(pfds, n, timeout_ms);
// Check pfds[i].revents & (POLLIN | POLLHUP) per FD
```

This same change is needed in [harnesses/cpp_harness.cpp](harnesses/cpp_harness.cpp).

---

### F-02 (P0) — Harness source code exceeds Judge0's `SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT`

**Exact location**: [core/judge0_client.py](core/judge0_client.py) `submit_and_wait()`, and [judge0.conf](judge0.conf).

**Why it fails**: Judge0 defaults `SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT` to 65,536 bytes (64KB). A harness embedding 1,000 test cases is not 64KB:

```
Python harness baseline:       ~8KB
+ 1,000 test cases at 50 bytes avg:  +50KB
+ JSON escaping overhead:       +5KB
Total:                          ~63KB  ← borderline
With large string inputs (200 bytes): ~220KB → hard failure
```

When the limit is exceeded, Judge0 returns HTTP 422 or silently truncates the source code before compiling. The compilation either fails or the harness runs with incomplete test cases. No error is propagated to the autograder — it looks like a compile error.

**Current `judge0.conf` is missing this setting entirely** — it uses the default, which may be 64KB in some Judge0 builds.

**Symptom**: `GradingResult.judge0_raw.status_str == "Compilation Error"` or partial TC execution with TCs beyond the truncation point silently missing.

**Fix — two-part**:

1. Add to [judge0.conf](judge0.conf):
```
SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT=524288   # 512KB — enough for 1,000 TCs with large I/O
```

2. Add a pre-flight guard in [core/judge0_client.py](core/judge0_client.py):
```python
SOURCE_CODE_LIMIT_BYTES = 512 * 1024  # must match judge0.conf

def submit_and_wait(self, source_code: str, ...):
    encoded = base64.b64encode(source_code.encode()).decode()
    raw_bytes = len(source_code.encode('utf-8'))
    if raw_bytes > SOURCE_CODE_LIMIT_BYTES:
        raise ValueError(
            f"Harness size {raw_bytes:,} bytes exceeds SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT "
            f"({SOURCE_CODE_LIMIT_BYTES:,}). Reduce test case count or input size."
        )
    ...
```

---

### F-03 (P0) — RLIMIT_NPROC exhaustion cascades to sequential fallback

**Exact location**: [harnesses/python_harness.py](harnesses/python_harness.py) `_can_fork_n()` and `_run_all()`.

**Why it fails**: `RLIMIT_NPROC` is a **per-UID limit** on the total number of processes. It is not per-process — it counts all processes for that user system-wide. Judge0's isolate runs all workers under the same UID.

With `MAX_RUNNERS=2` and 3 worker replicas (6 concurrent sandboxes), at peak:
- 6 harnesses × 1,000 fork()s = 6,000 child processes needed simultaneously
- Linux default per-user process limit: typically 1,024–4,096 in a container
- Python `_can_fork_n(1000)` checks available slots and falls back to sequential if insufficient

Sequential mode execution time:
```
1,000 TCs × 2s per TC = 2,000 seconds = 33 minutes per submission
```

**Symptom**: `GradingResult.judge0_raw.time_taken_s` hits the Judge0 global limit. All TCs beyond ~15 (in 30s) are MISSING and marked TLE. The `warning` field in each TCResult says "sequential fallback."

**Fix**:

1. Set appropriate `RLIMIT_NPROC` in the isolate/cgroup configuration, or in Docker:
```yaml
# docker-compose.yml — workers service
workers:
  ulimits:
    nproc: 65536    # per-container limit
```

2. In [harnesses/python_harness.py](harnesses/python_harness.py), the fallback warning currently suppresses the root cause. Add explicit limit logging:
```python
def _can_fork_n(n: int) -> tuple[bool, int]:
    try:
        soft, hard = resource.getrlimit(resource.RLIMIT_NPROC)
        try:
            current = len(os.listdir('/proc/self/task'))
        except OSError:
            current = 10
        available = soft - current - 2
        return available >= n, available
    except Exception:
        return True, -1
```

3. Enforce the limit in judge0.conf: `MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER` should account for 1,000 children:
```
MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER=1100   # 1,000 children + harness overhead
```

---

### F-04 (P0) — Worker throughput is catastrophically insufficient

**Exact location**: [docker-compose.yml](docker-compose.yml), judge0.conf `MAX_RUNNERS=2`.

**Calculation**:
```
Dev config:   3 worker replicas × MAX_RUNNERS=2 = 6 concurrent sandboxes
Execution time per job: per_tc_limit_s(2) + overhead(5) = 7 seconds
Throughput:   6 sandboxes / 7s = 0.86 submissions/second
Time to drain 1,000 submissions: 1,000 / 0.86 = 1,163 seconds = 19.4 minutes

Prod config (16 core): 12 replicas × 2 = 24 sandboxes
Time to drain: 1,000 / (24/7) = 292 seconds = 4.9 minutes
```

For a timed coding assessment, 4.9–19.4 minutes to return results is a product-level failure.

**Required throughput for acceptable latency** (target: p95 result in <60 seconds):
```
1,000 submissions, p95 done in 60s
Required throughput: 950 submissions / 60s = 15.8 sub/sec
Required sandboxes: 15.8 sub/sec × 7s execution = 111 concurrent sandboxes
With MAX_RUNNERS=2: 56 worker replicas
With MAX_RUNNERS=4 (if CPU allows): 28 worker replicas
```

**Fix — scaling configuration** for [docker-compose.yml](docker-compose.yml):
```yaml
workers:
  deploy:
    replicas: 50        # for AWS ECS or Docker Swarm
  environment:
    - MAX_RUNNERS=2     # keep at 2; more causes CPU thrash per worker
```

Manual scaling command for burst:
```bash
docker compose up --scale workers=50 -d
```

On AWS ECS, configure auto-scaling on the `workers` task:
```json
{
  "ScalingPolicy": "step",
  "MetricName": "redis_queue_depth",
  "Thresholds": [
    {"Lower": 10,  "ScaleOut": 10},
    {"Lower": 100, "ScaleOut": 25},
    {"Lower": 500, "ScaleOut": 50}
  ]
}
```

---

### F-05 (P1) — File descriptor exhaustion

**Calculation**:
```
Python/C harness: 2 FDs per TC (pipe read/write ends)
1,000 TCs → 2,000 FDs per harness process
6 concurrent harnesses → 12,000 FDs in the Judge0 worker process
Linux default open file limit in containers: often 1,024
```

**Symptom**: `pipe()` returns `EMFILE` → harness aborts mid-fork → first K TCs have results, TCs K+1..1000 are MISSING.

**Fix**: Set container FD limits:
```yaml
# docker-compose.yml — workers service
workers:
  ulimits:
    nofile:
      soft: 65536
      hard: 65536
```

Also add a pre-flight check in each harness template:
```c
// c_harness.c: before fork loop
struct rlimit rl;
getrlimit(RLIMIT_NOFILE, &rl);
if (rl.rlim_cur < (unsigned long)(n * 2 + 20)) {
    // emit all TCs as ERROR with detail "fd limit"
    for (int i = 1; i <= n; i++) {
        printf("%sSTART_%d\n{\"status\":\"ERROR\",\"detail\":\"fd_limit\"}\n%sEND_%d\n",
               DELIM, i, DELIM, i);
    }
    printf("%sDONE\n", DELIM);
    return 1;
}
```

---

### F-06 (P1) — Container OOM kills the entire harness

**Calculation**:
```
Each child: RLIMIT_AS = memory_limit_mb (e.g., 256MB)
RLIMIT_AS limits virtual address space, NOT RSS
Linux copy-on-write: children share parent's code pages
Dirty pages (heap allocations): fully counted per child

Worst case (student allocates near limit):
256MB dirty × 1,000 children = 256GB RSS → impossible on real hardware
Realistic case (avg 10MB dirty per child):
10MB × 1,000 = 10GB per harness × 6 concurrent = 60GB needed
```

If the Docker container has no memory limit set, the OOM killer fires at the cgroup/system level and kills the entire worker process, not just one child. All in-flight jobs on that worker are lost with no results written.

**Symptom**: Worker process exits with OOM signal. Judge0 internal DB record transitions from "Processing" → stuck, eventually timed out. Autograder TimeoutError after `max_polls × poll_interval_s = 30s`.

**Fix — three layers**:

1. Set per-container memory limits in [docker-compose.yml](docker-compose.yml):
```yaml
workers:
  mem_limit: "4g"          # hard limit per worker replica
  mem_reservation: "2g"    # soft guarantee
```

2. Reduce maximum children per harness via a `MAX_PARALLEL_TCS` cap:
```python
# In harness_builder.py HarnessBuilder.build()
MAX_PARALLEL_TCS = 250  # run in batches of 250, 4 rounds for 1,000 TCs
```

3. For C/C++ harnesses, add a container-level memory check before forking:
```c
long total_vm = (long)memory_limit_mb * 1024 * 1024 * n;
struct sysinfo si;
sysinfo(&si);
long available = (long)si.freeram * si.mem_unit;
if (total_vm > available * 2) {  // 2× safety margin
    // batch execution instead of full parallel
}
```

---

## Domain 2: Job Queue & Worker Infrastructure

### Current queue math

```
MAX_QUEUE_SIZE = 10,000 jobs   → 1,000 submissions fit (10%)
Harness size per job: ~50–200KB
1,000 jobs × 100KB = 100MB in Redis
Redis maxmemory = 512MB → safe margin
```

The queue depth itself is not the bottleneck. The throughput is.

### Burst acceptance — no backpressure at submission layer

The autograder accepts all 1,000 submissions instantly and queues them. There is no back-pressure mechanism that slows intake when workers are saturated.

**Correct pattern** — add a submission rate limiter in the platform layer (not the autograder, which is a library):
```python
# Platform API endpoint pseudocode
from asyncio import Semaphore

SUBMISSION_CONCURRENCY = 100  # max in-flight grades at once
_sem = Semaphore(SUBMISSION_CONCURRENCY)

async def submit_endpoint(submission_data):
    async with _sem:
        result = await asyncio.get_event_loop().run_in_executor(
            thread_pool, autograder.grade, submission
        )
    return result
```

### Dead letter queue — jobs disappear on timeout

**Exact location**: [core/judge0_client.py](core/judge0_client.py) `submit_and_wait()`.

When `max_polls` is exhausted, a `TimeoutError` is raised. The submission exists in Judge0's DB but the platform has no record of it. The worker may complete the job 5 minutes later, but no one is listening.

**Fix**: Store submission tokens before polling, enable delayed retrieval:
```python
# In autograder.py grade()
token = self.judge0.submit_only(harness_code, language, ...)
# persist token to platform DB here
result = self.judge0.wait_for_token(token, timeout=300)
```

Add a `submit_only()` method to [core/judge0_client.py](core/judge0_client.py):
```python
def submit_only(self, source_code: str, language: str, ...) -> str:
    """Submit without waiting. Returns Judge0 token for later retrieval."""
    payload = self._build_payload(source_code, language, ...)
    resp = self._post_with_retry("/submissions?base64_encoded=true", payload, timeout=120)
    return resp["token"]
```

---

## Domain 3: Database (PostgreSQL)

### Actual write volume at 1,000 submissions

```
Per submission: 1 INSERT (submission) + ~3 UPDATEs (state transitions) + 1 UPDATE (result)
Total: 1,000 × 5 = 5,000 DB operations (not 1,000,000)
```

The single-harness design reduces DB write load by 1,000×. This domain is **not a bottleneck** at this scale given the current configuration — with one caveat:

### F-10 (P1) — Polling storm overwhelms Puma connections

**Calculation**:
```
Poll mode: 1,000 autograder instances × 1 poll every 0.5s
= 2,000 GET /submissions/{token} requests/second
Puma: 4 processes × 20 threads = 80 concurrent connections
Queue at Puma: 2,000 - 80 = 1,920 requests/second backed up
Each request waits for a Puma thread to free
With 100ms DB query time: each thread handles 10 req/s
80 threads × 10 req/s = 800 req/s capacity → 2,000 req/s demand
```

**Symptom**: GET /submissions returns HTTP 503 or times out. Autograder's exponential backoff kicks in (0.5s, 1s, 2s). Effective poll rate drops to one poll every 2s per submission → still 500 req/s → still saturated.

**Fix**: Switch to callback mode in all production deployments. Callbacks eliminate polling entirely:

```python
# Platform autograder instantiation
autograder = Autograder(
    judge0_config=Judge0Config(base_url=JUDGE0_URL),
    callback_host="0.0.0.0",    # listen on all interfaces
    callback_port=9090
)
```

With callbacks: 1,000 submissions generate 1,000 webhook POSTs **total** (not 2,000/second sustained). Puma load drops by ~99%.

---

## Domain 4: API Layer & HTTP

### F-07 (P1) — Puma saturates at 80 connections against 1,000 simultaneous requests

**Exact location**: [docker-compose.yml](docker-compose.yml) `RAILS_MAX_THREADS=20`, `WEB_CONCURRENCY=4`.

**What happens**:
1. 1,000 simultaneous POST /submit requests arrive
2. OS TCP listen backlog: typically 128 by default
3. Connections 129–1,000 are either queued in SYN state or immediately RST
4. Of the 128 accepted: Puma queues them for its 80 threads
5. Each POST blocks a thread for the full DB INSERT duration (~5ms) 
6. Throughput: 80 threads × (1s / 5ms) = 16,000 req/s capacity → 1,000 simultaneous is fine... BUT
7. The 1,000 TPC backlog at the TCP level means the first connection is served immediately, the 128th waits ~8ms, and connections 129–1,000 time out

**Fix — increase listen backlog and Puma capacity**:
```yaml
# docker-compose.yml
server:
  environment:
    - WEB_CONCURRENCY=8        # 8 Puma processes
    - RAILS_MAX_THREADS=25     # 25 threads each = 200 connections total
  sysctls:
    net.core.somaxconn: 4096   # OS listen backlog
    net.ipv4.tcp_max_syn_backlog: 4096
```

Or better, add an nginx reverse proxy in front of Puma:
```yaml
nginx:
  image: nginx:alpine
  ports: ["2358:80"]
  volumes: ["./nginx.conf:/etc/nginx/nginx.conf"]
```
```nginx
# nginx.conf
upstream judge0 {
    server server:2358;
    keepalive 200;
}
server {
    listen 80 backlog=4096;
    location / {
        proxy_pass http://judge0;
        proxy_connect_timeout 5s;
        proxy_read_timeout 180s;
    }
}
```

### F-08 (P1) — No idempotency on submission retries

**Exact location**: [core/judge0_client.py](core/judge0_client.py) `submit_and_wait()` — the POST to `/submissions` has no idempotency key.

**Scenario**: Client times out on POST (after 120s under load). Client retries. Two harnesses now exist for the same submission. Both complete. Which result does the platform use?

**Fix**: Add an idempotency header using the platform's submission ID:
```python
# In judge0_client.py submit_and_wait() / submit_only()
def submit_and_wait(self, source_code: str, language: str, ..., 
                    idempotency_key: str = None):
    headers = {"Content-Type": "application/json"}
    if self.config.api_key:
        headers["X-Auth-Token"] = self.config.api_key
    if idempotency_key:
        headers["X-Idempotency-Key"] = idempotency_key  # Judge0 1.13 supports this
    ...
```

Judge0 1.13.0 does not natively support idempotency keys. The fix is to implement it at the platform layer: **store the Judge0 token before blocking**. If the network call succeeds but the client times out before receiving the response body, the platform can query the token it already stored.

---

### F-09 (P1) — Callback server spawns 1,000 threads simultaneously

**Exact location**: [core/judge0_client.py](core/judge0_client.py) `CallbackServer` using `ThreadingHTTPServer`.

**Python's `ThreadingMixIn`** creates one OS thread per incoming connection. 1,000 simultaneous webhook deliveries = 1,000 threads:
```
1,000 threads × ~1MB stack (Python default) = 1GB RAM just for callback stacks
Python GIL: only 1 thread runs Python at a time
Thread creation: ~5–10ms each → 5–10 seconds to create all 1,000 threads
```

Meanwhile, the `_deliver()` method acquires `self._lock` for every delivery — a global lock on the results dict. This becomes a bottleneck.

**Fix**: Replace `ThreadingHTTPServer` with a non-blocking async server:
```python
# In judge0_client.py
import asyncio
from aiohttp import web

class CallbackServer:
    def __init__(self):
        self._results: dict[str, dict] = {}
        self._events: dict[str, asyncio.Event] = {}
        self._lock = asyncio.Lock()  # asyncio lock, not threading
        self._app = web.Application()
        self._app.router.add_put('/callback/{token}', self._handle)

    async def _handle(self, request: web.Request) -> web.Response:
        token = request.match_info['token']
        payload = await request.json()
        async with self._lock:
            self._results[token] = payload
            if token in self._events:
                self._events[token].set()
        return web.Response(status=200)
```

Or, simpler fix that avoids rewriting: use `ThreadPoolExecutor` with a bounded pool:
```python
from socketserver import ThreadingMixIn
from http.server import HTTPServer
from concurrent.futures import ThreadPoolExecutor

class BoundedThreadingHTTPServer(ThreadingMixIn, HTTPServer):
    _pool = ThreadPoolExecutor(max_workers=50)  # cap at 50 threads
    
    def process_request(self, request, client_address):
        self._pool.submit(self.process_request_thread, request, client_address)
```

---

## Domain 5: Real-Time Delivery

### F-12 (P2) — `grade()` is synchronous and blocking

**Exact location**: [autograder.py](autograder.py) `Autograder.grade()`.

The entire chain — security check, harness build, Judge0 submit, poll/wait, parse — runs synchronously. For a platform handling 1,000 simultaneous submissions, each call to `grade()` blocks its thread for 7–30 seconds.

```python
# Platform must do this (if using threads):
from concurrent.futures import ThreadPoolExecutor
executor = ThreadPoolExecutor(max_workers=1000)  # 1GB+ memory just for stacks
futures = [executor.submit(autograder.grade, sub) for sub in submissions]
```

1,000 OS threads is feasible but wasteful. The right architecture:

**Fix**: Add async wrapper to [autograder.py](autograder.py):
```python
import asyncio
from functools import partial

class Autograder:
    ...
    async def grade_async(self, submission: Submission) -> GradingResult:
        """Non-blocking version for asyncio platforms."""
        loop = asyncio.get_event_loop()
        return await loop.run_in_executor(
            self._thread_pool,  # shared bounded pool
            partial(self.grade, submission)
        )
```

And in the platform's WebSocket handler:
```python
# Platform WebSocket endpoint
@websocket_route("/ws/submit")
async def handle_submission(ws, submission_data):
    submission = Submission(**submission_data)
    result = await autograder.grade_async(submission)
    await ws.send_json(result.summary())
```

### Polling storm from frontend clients

If the platform uses HTTP polling (not WebSocket) and 1,000 students poll `/status` every 2 seconds:
```
1,000 clients × 0.5 req/s = 500 req/s on platform API
Duration: ~7 minutes until all results arrive
Total requests: 500 req/s × 420s = 210,000 requests
```

**Fix**: Use Server-Sent Events (SSE) instead of polling:
```javascript
// Frontend
const es = new EventSource(`/api/submission/${id}/stream`);
es.onmessage = (e) => updateUI(JSON.parse(e.data));
```
```python
# Backend SSE endpoint
async def stream_result(request):
    async def generate():
        result = await result_future
        yield f"data: {result.json()}\n\n"
    return StreamingResponse(generate(), media_type="text/event-stream")
```

---

## Domain 6: File Storage

### Harness payload size is the real S3 equivalent concern

There is no S3 in the current architecture — test cases are embedded directly in harness source code. This is the correct design (avoids S3 cold-read latency per job), but creates the size constraint described in F-02.

**For very large test cases** (e.g., 10KB input files), the harness approach breaks down:
```
1,000 TCs × 10KB each = 10MB harness
SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT = 512KB → won't fit
```

**Architectural fix for large I/O test cases**: Pass test cases via stdin to the harness, not embedded in source code:
```
Harness source: template only (< 10KB, always)
stdin to Judge0: JSON array of all test cases (up to 10MB)
Judge0 stdin_limit: set to 10MB in judge0.conf
```

```python
# In harness_builder.py
def build_with_stdin(self) -> tuple[str, str]:
    """Returns (harness_source, stdin_data) for large TC sets."""
    harness = self._build_template_only()   # no embedded TCs
    stdin = json.dumps([tc.__dict__ for tc in self.config.test_cases])
    return harness, stdin
```

```python
# In judge0_client.py submit_and_wait()
payload = {
    "source_code": base64.b64encode(source_code.encode()).decode(),
    "stdin": base64.b64encode(stdin_data.encode()).decode() if stdin_data else None,
    ...
}
```

---

## Domain 7: Memory & CPU (Server-Side)

### Node.js event loop — if the platform backend is Node.js

The autograder is Python, but the platform backend is Node.js. With 1,000 simultaneous HTTP submissions, Node's event loop handles them efficiently — but if any submission handler does synchronous CPU work (JSON parsing of 1,000 TC results, etc.), it blocks the loop.

**Rule**: Any `JSON.parse()` of a response > 100KB, or any synchronous iteration over 1,000 results, should be offloaded to a Worker Thread:
```javascript
const { Worker, isMainThread, workerData, parentPort } = require('worker_threads');

// In main thread
const worker = new Worker('./result-parser.js', { workerData: rawOutput });
worker.on('message', (parsed) => sendToClient(parsed));
```

### Python memory under 1,000 concurrent `grade()` calls

```
Per Submission object (1,000 TCs):
  - test_cases list: 1,000 × ~500 bytes = 500KB
  - harness source code: ~100KB
  - GradingResult: 1,000 TCResult × ~300 bytes = 300KB
Total per call: ~900KB

1,000 concurrent calls: ~900MB peak
Python GC: triggered at 700MB → pause during GC
```

This is manageable but requires the platform to not accumulate all GradingResults in memory before writing to DB. Process and discard each result immediately after writing.

---

## Domain 8: Observability & Failure Recovery

### F-13 (P2) — No circuit breaker, no metrics, no dead-letter queue

**Current state**: Zero observability infrastructure in the codebase.

**What fails silently today**:
1. A job submitted with an incorrect `callback_url` (firewall blocks it) → waits `global_limit + 30s` → TimeoutError → platform retries → duplicate job
2. Redis fills to `MAX_QUEUE_SIZE=10,000` → Judge0 returns HTTP 503 → autograder raises an exception with no retry → submission lost
3. A worker crashes mid-execution → job stays in "Processing" state forever → autograder polls until `max_polls=60` → TimeoutError

**Minimum viable observability**:

```python
# Add to judge0_client.py
import time
import logging

class Metrics:
    """Drop-in metrics collector. Replace with Prometheus/Datadog in production."""
    _counts: dict[str, int] = {}
    _latencies: dict[str, list[float]] = {}
    
    @classmethod
    def increment(cls, key: str) -> None:
        cls._counts[key] = cls._counts.get(key, 0) + 1
    
    @classmethod
    def record_latency(cls, key: str, seconds: float) -> None:
        cls._latencies.setdefault(key, []).append(seconds)
    
    @classmethod
    def report(cls) -> dict:
        report = {}
        for k, v in cls._latencies.items():
            s = sorted(v)
            n = len(s)
            report[k] = {"p50": s[n//2], "p95": s[int(n*0.95)], "p99": s[int(n*0.99)], "count": n}
        report["counts"] = cls._counts
        return report

# In submit_and_wait():
t0 = time.monotonic()
try:
    result = self._poll(token) or self._wait_callback(token, global_limit_s)
    Metrics.increment("judge0.submit.success")
    Metrics.record_latency("judge0.submit.latency", time.monotonic() - t0)
    return result
except TimeoutError:
    Metrics.increment("judge0.submit.timeout")
    raise
```

**Circuit breaker** to prevent cascade failures:
```python
# Add to judge0_client.py
class CircuitBreaker:
    def __init__(self, failure_threshold=5, recovery_timeout=30):
        self._failures = 0
        self._threshold = failure_threshold
        self._open_until = 0.0
    
    def call(self, fn, *args, **kwargs):
        if time.monotonic() < self._open_until:
            raise RuntimeError("Circuit open: Judge0 is unavailable")
        try:
            result = fn(*args, **kwargs)
            self._failures = 0
            return result
        except (TimeoutError, ConnectionError) as e:
            self._failures += 1
            if self._failures >= self._threshold:
                self._open_until = time.monotonic() + self._recovery_timeout
                logging.critical("Circuit breaker OPEN — Judge0 unreachable")
            raise
```

**Alerting thresholds** to configure in your monitoring system:
| Metric | Warning | Critical |
|---|---|---|
| Redis queue depth | > 500 | > 2,000 |
| Worker error rate | > 1% | > 5% |
| Submission p95 latency | > 30s | > 120s |
| TimeoutError rate | > 0.1% | > 1% |
| Active sandboxes / max | > 80% | > 95% |

---

## Domain 9: Security & Isolation Under Load

### F-14 (P2) — Caller-controlled `memory_limit_mb` has no upper bound

**Exact location**: [autograder.py](autograder.py) `Submission` dataclass. The `memory_limit_mb` field has no validation.

A malicious or misconfigured caller sets `memory_limit_mb=16384` (16GB). The harness sets `RLIMIT_AS=16GB` per child. With 1,000 children, the process can attempt to map 16TB of virtual address space. While pages aren't immediately faulted in, a `malloc(16GB)` call will succeed (virtual allocation), and any write to that allocation creates dirty pages. In a container without `mem_limit`, this crashes the worker.

**Fix**: Add validation in [autograder.py](autograder.py):
```python
MAX_MEMORY_LIMIT_MB = 1024   # 1GB hard cap
MAX_PER_TC_LIMIT_S = 10      # 10 second hard cap
MAX_TEST_CASES = 1000        # cap enforced explicitly

@dataclass
class Submission:
    ...
    def __post_init__(self):
        if self.memory_limit_mb > MAX_MEMORY_LIMIT_MB:
            raise ValueError(f"memory_limit_mb {self.memory_limit_mb} exceeds maximum {MAX_MEMORY_LIMIT_MB}")
        if self.per_tc_limit_s > MAX_PER_TC_LIMIT_S:
            raise ValueError(f"per_tc_limit_s {self.per_tc_limit_s} exceeds maximum {MAX_PER_TC_LIMIT_S}")
        if len(self.test_cases) > MAX_TEST_CASES:
            raise ValueError(f"test case count {len(self.test_cases)} exceeds maximum {MAX_TEST_CASES}")
```

### Sandbox isolation is sound, but network isolation needs verification

The current C/C++ child processes do `setrlimit(RLIMIT_NPROC, 1)` to prevent student code from forking. But the following syscalls are **not** restricted at the harness level:
- `open()` with `O_RDONLY` — student can read `/proc/self/maps`, `/etc/passwd`
- `mmap()` with `MAP_SHARED` — shared memory between processes
- Raw socket syscalls via `syscall(SYS_socket, ...)` bypassing the `socket()` wrapper

These are mitigated by Judge0's `isolate` sandbox (which uses seccomp-bpf to restrict syscalls). Verify your Judge0 configuration includes:
```
# judge0.conf
ALLOW_ENABLE_NETWORK=false    # critical: disables network inside sandbox
```

---

## Domain 10: UX & Product Failure Modes

### Timeout after which a submission is marked failed

Currently there is no such timeout in the autograder. `grade()` raises `TimeoutError` after 30 seconds. The platform must catch this and write a failed state:

```python
# Platform-level wrapper
async def grade_with_deadline(autograder, submission, platform_db, deadline_s=300):
    try:
        result = await asyncio.wait_for(
            autograder.grade_async(submission),
            timeout=deadline_s
        )
        await platform_db.save_result(submission.student_id, result)
    except asyncio.TimeoutError:
        await platform_db.save_failed(
            submission.student_id,
            reason="evaluation_timeout",
            partial_results=None
        )
        # Notify student via WebSocket
        await ws_notify(submission.student_id, {
            "status": "failed",
            "message": "Evaluation timed out. Your submission has been received but could not be graded within the time limit."
        })
```

### Progress events

The current architecture delivers results atomically (all or nothing). For large TC sets, add intermediate progress delivery in the harness output protocol:

```
@@TC_RESULT__abc123__START_1
{"status": "PASS", ...}
@@TC_RESULT__abc123__END_1
@@TC_RESULT__abc123__PROGRESS_1_OF_1000     ← add this
```

Then stream partial results via SSE as the platform reads Judge0 stdout chunks.

## Recommended Target Architecture

```
                         ┌─────────────────────────────────┐
                         │         Load Balancer            │
                         │  (nginx, backlog=4096, keepalive)│
                         └──────────────┬──────────────────┘
                                        │
                    ┌───────────────────▼──────────────────┐
                    │         Platform API (Node.js)        │
                    │  Rate Limiter: 100 concurrent/exam    │
                    │  SSE/WebSocket for result streaming   │
                    │  Idempotency key generation           │
                    └──────┬────────────────────────────────┘
                           │ async grade_async()
                    ┌──────▼─────────────────────────────────┐
                    │      Autograder (Python, thread pool)   │
                    │  - SecurityChecker                      │
                    │  - HarnessBuilder (poll() not select()) │
                    │  - Judge0Client (callback mode)         │
                    │  - CircuitBreaker                       │
                    └──────┬─────────────────────────────────┘
                           │ POST /submissions (idempotency key)
         ┌─────────────────▼──────────────────────────────┐
         │         Judge0 API (Puma, 8 proc × 25 threads) │
         │         + nginx frontend (backlog=4096)         │
         └──┬─────────────────────────────────────────────┘
            │                              ▲
    ┌───────▼──────┐              ┌────────┴────────┐
    │  Redis Queue │              │  Callback Server│
    │  maxmem=2GB  │              │  (aiohttp, async│
    │  noeviction  │              │   no thread/conn│
    └───────┬──────┘              └─────────────────┘
            │                              ▲
    ┌───────▼──────────────────────────────┴─────┐
    │  Worker Pool (50 replicas, MAX_RUNNERS=2)   │
    │  ulimits: nofile=65536, nproc=65536         │
    │  mem_limit=4GB per replica                  │
    │  Harness: poll() not select()               │
    │  Max 250 parallel TCs per harness           │
    └────────────────────────────────────────────┘
            │
    ┌───────▼──────┐
    │  PostgreSQL  │
    │  max_conn=200│
    │  (no change) │
    └──────────────┘
```

## Specific Configuration Values

### [judge0.conf](judge0.conf) changes
```ini
# Current                          # Recommended
MAX_QUEUE_SIZE=10000               MAX_QUEUE_SIZE=10000        # no change, fine
MAX_RUNNERS=2                      MAX_RUNNERS=2               # no change, 2 is optimal
                                   SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT=524288  # ADD: 512KB
                                   MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER=1100  # ADD: 1000 children + overhead
                                   ALLOW_ENABLE_NETWORK=false     # ADD: security
                                   STDIN_SIZE_LIMIT=10485760      # ADD: 10MB for large TCs
```

### [docker-compose.yml](docker-compose.yml) changes
```yaml
server:
  environment:
    - WEB_CONCURRENCY=8      # was 4
    - RAILS_MAX_THREADS=25   # was 20 → 200 total connections
  sysctls:
    net.core.somaxconn: 4096
    net.ipv4.tcp_max_syn_backlog: 4096

workers:
  deploy:
    replicas: 50             # was 3 → target: 50 for 1,000 subs in <60s
  mem_limit: "4g"            # ADD: prevent OOM cascades
  ulimits:
    nofile:
      soft: 65536            # ADD: prevent FD exhaustion
      hard: 65536
    nproc: 65536             # ADD: prevent fork() exhaustion

redis:
  command: >
    redis-server
    --appendonly yes
    --maxmemory 2gb          # was 512mb → 1,000 × 100KB = 100MB + headroom
    --maxmemory-policy noeviction
    --requirepass judge0redis
    --tcp-backlog 4096       # ADD: large connection backlog
```

## Load Testing Plan

### Phase 0 — Baseline (run today)

# Verify current limits before any fix
python3 -c "
from core.harness_builder import *
cfg = HarnessConfig(

### Phase 1 — Single submission correctness (run after each code fix)
```python
# test_single_1000tc.py
import time
from autograder import Autograder, Submission
from core.judge0_client import Judge0Config

ag = Autograder(Judge0Config(base_url="http://localhost:2358"))
sub = Submission(
    student_id="test-001",
    language="python",
    student_code="def solve(x): return x * 2",
    test_cases=[{"inputs": [i], "expected": i*2} for i in range(1000)],
    mode="function",
    function_name="solve",
    param_types=["int"],
    return_type="int",
    per_tc_limit_s=2,
    memory_limit_mb=256
)
t0 = time.time()
result = ag.grade(sub)
elapsed = time.time() - t0
assert result.submission.score == 1000, f"Expected 1000, got {result.submission.score}"
print(f"1,000 TCs in {elapsed:.2f}s — {'PASS' if elapsed < 10 else 'FAIL (too slow)'}")
```

### Phase 2 — Concurrent load (run after worker scaling)
```python
# load_test_concurrent.py
import asyncio, time, statistics
from autograder import Autograder, Submission
from core.judge0_client import Judge0Config

CONCURRENCY_LEVELS = [10, 50, 100, 250, 500, 1000]

async def run_load_test(n_concurrent: int):
    ag = Autograder(
        Judge0Config(base_url="http://localhost:2358"),
        callback_host="host.docker.internal",
        callback_port=9090
    )
    
    def make_submission(i):
        return Submission(
            student_id=f"student-{i:04d}",
            language="python",
            student_code="def solve(x): return x",
            test_cases=[{"inputs": [j], "expected": j} for j in range(100)],  # 100 TCs for speed
            mode="function", function_name="solve",
            param_types=["int"], return_type="int",
            per_tc_limit_s=2, memory_limit_mb=256
        )
    
    latencies = []
    errors = []
    
    async def grade_one(i):
        t0 = time.time()
        try:
            result = await ag.grade_async(make_submission(i))
            latencies.append(time.time() - t0)
            return result.submission.score == 100
        except Exception as e:
            errors.append(str(e))
            return False
    
    t_start = time.time()
    results = await asyncio.gather(*[grade_one(i) for i in range(n_concurrent)])
    total_time = time.time() - t_start
    
    correct = sum(results)
    print(f"\n=== {n_concurrent} concurrent submissions ===")
    print(f"Correct: {correct}/{n_concurrent} ({100*correct/n_concurrent:.1f}%)")
    print(f"Throughput: {n_concurrent/total_time:.2f} sub/sec")
    if latencies:
        print(f"Latency p50={statistics.median(latencies):.1f}s  "
              f"p95={sorted(latencies)[int(len(latencies)*0.95)]:.1f}s  "
              f"max={max(latencies):.1f}s")
    if errors:
        print(f"Errors ({len(errors)}): {set(errors)}")

for n in CONCURRENCY_LEVELS:
    asyncio.run(run_load_test(n))
    time.sleep(5)  # allow queue to drain between levels
```

### Phase 3 — Adversarial/stress tests
```python
# Infinite loop — verify TLE isolation
sub_tle = Submission(student_code="def solve(x):\n    while True: pass", ...)

# Memory bomb — verify OOM isolation
sub_oom = Submission(student_code="def solve(x):\n    return [0]*10**9", ...)

# Large output — verify output truncation
sub_output = Submission(student_code="def solve(x):\n    return 'A'*100000", ...)

# All should complete in < 10s and not affect other concurrent submissions
```

### Pass/Fail Criteria

| Metric | Target | Action if missed |
|---|---|---|
| Correctness (score accuracy) | 100% | Fix harness bugs before re-testing |
| p95 latency at 1,000 concurrent | < 120s | Scale workers |
| p99 latency at 1,000 concurrent | < 300s | Add priority lanes |
| Error rate | < 0.1% | Fix circuit breaker, add retry |
| TLE isolation (infinite loop) | 100% contained | Fix resource limits |
| OOM isolation | 100% contained | Fix mem_limit in docker-compose |
| Throughput | > 10 sub/sec | Scale workers to 50+ |

---

The three changes that unlock everything else: fix `select()` → `poll()` (F-01, unblocks 1,000 TCs), add `SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT` (F-02, prevents silent compile failures), and scale workers to 50 replicas (F-04, achieves the required throughput). Everything else in this document is either a correctness fix or a hardening measure.

=== USER ===
what is fd

=== ASSISTANT ===
**fd** = file descriptor.

In Unix/Linux, a file descriptor is a small non-negative integer that the kernel assigns to any open "resource" — files, pipes, sockets, devices. Every process gets three by default:

| fd | Name | Meaning |
|---|---|---|
| 0 | stdin | standard input |
| 1 | stdout | standard output |
| 2 | stderr | standard error |

When you call `open()`, `pipe()`, or `socket()`, the kernel hands back the next available integer (3, 4, 5, ...).

In the context of the failure analysis: the C/C++ harnesses call `pipe()` once per test case, which creates **two** fds (a read end and a write end). With 1,000 test cases that's 2,000 fds open simultaneously in one process — which is why the `ulimit -n` (max open files per process) matters.

=== USER ===
explain me in restaurent style in detail.

=== ASSISTANT ===
# File Descriptors — Explained as a Restaurant

---

## The Restaurant is Your Computer

Imagine a busy restaurant. The **kitchen** is the operating system (Linux kernel). The **waiters** are your programs (processes). The **tables** are resources — files, pipes, network connections.

---

## The Ticket System

When a waiter needs something from the kitchen, they don't shout "I need Table 7's pasta order and Table 12's soup!" That's chaos.

Instead, the kitchen manager hands every waiter a **numbered ticket** when they open a new table.

> Waiter opens Table 7 → kitchen says "that's ticket **3**"
> Waiter opens Table 12 → kitchen says "that's ticket **4**"
> Waiter opens Table 5 → kitchen says "that's ticket **5**"

From that point on, the waiter never says "Table 7." He just says **"ticket 3."** The kitchen knows what that means.

**That numbered ticket = a file descriptor.**

The kernel hands your program a small integer (3, 4, 5...) every time you open anything — a file, a pipe, a network connection. Your program uses that number to refer to it forever after.

---

## Every Waiter Starts With 3 Permanent Tables

Before the restaurant even opens, every waiter is already assigned three tables they can never close:

| Ticket | Table | Purpose |
|---|---|---|
| **0** | The order pad | Waiter reads customer orders from here (stdin) |
| **1** | The serving window | Waiter delivers food through here (stdout) |
| **2** | The complaint box | Waiter drops error notes here (stderr) |

These are always open. New tables start at ticket **3**.

---

## Opening a Table = Opening a File

```
Waiter:  "I need to open a new table."
Kitchen: "Fine. That's ticket 3."  ← fd = 3

Waiter:  "Another table."
Kitchen: "Ticket 4."               ← fd = 4
```

The waiter now carries a small notepad that says:

```
3 → Table by the window  (a file on disk)
4 → The VIP booth        (a network socket)
5 → The takeaway counter (a pipe)
```

Every time the waiter wants to send food somewhere, he just says **"put it on ticket 5."** He does not need to remember the full address of the takeaway counter.

---

## A Pipe = A Kitchen Pass-Through Hatch

Now here is where it gets interesting for the harness story.

When the head chef (parent process) needs to **talk to a junior chef** (child process), they open a **pass-through hatch** between them.

A hatch has **two sides**:
- One side to **push food through** (write end)
- One side to **receive food** (read end)

```
Head chef calls: "Open a hatch!"
Kitchen assigns:  ticket 6 = push side
                  ticket 7 = receive side
```

In code, this is `pipe()` — it always gives back **two** file descriptors.

---

## What the C Harness Does With 1,000 Test Cases

Now the head chef needs to cook **1,000 dishes simultaneously**.

He hires 1,000 junior chefs (fork = create child process).

Each junior chef needs their own private hatch to send results back.

```
Dish 1   → open hatch → tickets 3 and 4
Dish 2   → open hatch → tickets 5 and 6
Dish 3   → open hatch → tickets 7 and 8
...
Dish 1000 → open hatch → tickets 2001 and 2002
```

The head chef is now holding **2,000 tickets** in his notepad.

---

## The Restaurant Has a Rule: Maximum 1,024 Tickets Per Waiter

The restaurant manager (OS) has a strict policy:

> **No waiter may hold more than 1,024 tickets at one time.**

This is `ulimit -n` — the maximum number of open file descriptors per process.

With 1,000 dishes × 2 hatch sides = **2,000 tickets needed.**

The waiter runs out of tickets at dish **511** (tickets 3–1023 are used up, ticket 1024 is forbidden).

From dish 512 onward, when the waiter tries to open a new hatch:

```
Waiter:  "I need another hatch for dish 512."
Kitchen: "Sorry. You've hit the limit. Request DENIED."  ← EMFILE error
```

The hatch is never opened. Dish 512 through 1,000 are never cooked. The results are never delivered. The customer sees **all 488 dishes as missing** — no explanation, no error message, just... gone.

---

## The `select()` Problem — The Head Chef Uses an Old Clipboard

Once all hatches are open, the head chef waits for junior chefs to finish. He uses an old clipboard (`select()`) to monitor all hatches at once.

But this clipboard is **ancient** — it was designed in 1983.

> **The clipboard only has 1,024 checkbox slots.**

It physically cannot track ticket numbers above 1,023.

```
Clipboard slots:
☐ Ticket 3     ← dish 1 hatch
☐ Ticket 5     ← dish 2 hatch
...
☐ Ticket 1023  ← dish 511 hatch
[NO MORE SPACE]
```

Even if you somehow gave the head chef 2,000 tickets (by relaxing the ulimit), he still **cannot watch tickets 1,024 and above** on his old clipboard. He scribbles on the margin of the clipboard — which corrupts other notes nearby (undefined behavior / memory corruption).

Junior chefs 512–1,000 finish cooking, ring the bell, and wait.

The head chef never looks at their hatch.

Junior chefs keep waiting.

The head chef declares the entire kitchen **overtime** (global TLE).

The customer gets a "Time Limit Exceeded" for all 1,000 dishes.

---

## The Fix — Give the Head Chef a Modern Tablet

Replace `select()` (old clipboard, 1,024 slots) with `poll()` (modern tablet, unlimited slots).

```
Modern tablet slots:
☐ Ticket 3      ← dish 1
☐ Ticket 5      ← dish 2
...
☐ Ticket 2001   ← dish 1000   ✓ no limit!
```

The head chef can now watch all 1,000 hatches simultaneously. When any junior chef rings the bell, the tablet lights up that ticket immediately.

---

## Fix the Ticket Limit Too — Tell the Manager to Allow More

```
# Tell the restaurant manager: each waiter can hold 65,536 tickets
ulimits:
  nofile:
    soft: 65536
    hard: 65536
```

Now the waiter can open 32,768 hatches (65,536 tickets ÷ 2 sides per hatch) — more than enough for 1,000 dishes.

---

## Summary in One Picture

```
BEFORE (broken):

  Head Chef
  │
  ├── Dish 1   hatch (tickets 3-4)    ✓ watched on old clipboard
  ├── Dish 2   hatch (tickets 5-6)    ✓ watched
  ├── ...
  ├── Dish 511 hatch (tickets 1021-1022) ✓ watched (clipboard full)
  ├── Dish 512 hatch → DENIED (ulimit hit)  ✗ never opened
  └── Dish 1000 hatch → DENIED              ✗ never opened


AFTER (fixed):

  Head Chef
  │
  ├── Dish 1    hatch (tickets 3-4)      ✓ watched on modern tablet
  ├── Dish 2    hatch (tickets 5-6)      ✓ watched
  ├── ...
  └── Dish 1000 hatch (tickets 2001-2002) ✓ watched — no limit!
```

**File descriptor = the ticket number the kernel gives you when you open any resource.**
**The clipboard (`select`) = the old system that breaks above ticket 1,023.**
**The tablet (`poll`) = the modern system with no such limit.**
