# The Grand Restaurant — A Complete Failure Analysis

## First, Meet Everyone in This Restaurant

Before anything breaks, let us introduce every person and every object in this restaurant. You will meet them again and again throughout this document.

---

```
THE GRAND RESTAURANT — Staff & Layout
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━

👨‍🎓 CUSTOMERS          = Students submitting code
📋 ORDER               = A code submission
🍽️  DISH               = One test case
📖 RECIPE              = Student's code
📦 MEGA-RECIPE         = The harness (wraps student code + all test cases)

🚪 FRONT DOOR          = API Gateway / Load Balancer
🧑‍💼 MAÎTRE D'          = nginx (routes customers to right counter)
🧑‍🍳 FRONT DESK STAFF   = Puma (Rails HTTP workers) — 4 staff × 20 tasks = 80
📝 ORDER BOARD         = Redis queue (holds jobs waiting to be cooked)
🍳 COOKING STATIONS    = Judge0 worker sandboxes
👨‍🍳 HEAD CHEF          = Autograder orchestrator
👶 JUNIOR CHEFS        = Forked child processes (one per test case)
🏠 KITCHEN ROOM        = Docker container (isolated workspace)
📒 PANTRY LEDGER       = PostgreSQL database
🧊 FREEZER             = S3 / file storage
📞 INTERCOM            = WebSocket / real-time result delivery
🔔 KITCHEN BELL        = Webhook callback (Judge0 → autograder)
🚨 FIRE ALARM          = Circuit breaker
🕵️ HEALTH INSPECTOR    = Security checker (blocks dangerous recipes)
📊 RESTAURANT CRITIC   = Observability / monitoring system
🎫 TICKET NUMBER       = File descriptor (fd)
📋 OLD CLIPBOARD       = select() — limited to 1,024 slots
📱 MODERN TABLET       = poll() — no slot limit
🗂️ KITCHEN RULES BOARD = judge0.conf (configuration file)
🪣 LOST ORDERS BIN     = Dead letter queue
🔑 DUPLICATE ORDER KEY = Idempotency key
🚧 BOUNCER AT DOOR     = Rate limiter
```

---

## The Scenario: Exam Day

Today is exam day. **1,000 students** walk into the restaurant simultaneously. Each student hands in an order slip with their recipe (code). The kitchen must cook every dish (test case) — **up to 1,000 dishes per order** — and deliver results back to each student.

That means up to **1,000,000 dishes** to evaluate at peak.

Except — and this is the clever trick — the kitchen does **not** cook 1,000,000 separate orders. Instead, the head chef rolls each student's recipe into one **Mega-Recipe** that cooks all 1,000 dishes simultaneously inside one kitchen room. So the order board only ever sees **1,000 orders**, not 1,000,000.

This is brilliant. But it also means each of those 1,000 orders is **enormous and heavyweight**. And that creates a completely different set of ways the restaurant can collapse.

Let us walk through every single failure, one by one.

---

# DOMAIN 1: THE COOKING STATIONS (Code Execution Engine)

---

## F-01 (P0) — The Head Chef's Old Clipboard Can't Watch 1,000 Hatches

### Setup the scene

When the Head Chef receives an order with 1,000 dishes, he hires **1,000 Junior Chefs** (child processes via `fork()`). Each junior chef gets their own private **pass-through hatch** (a pipe) to send results back to the head chef.

```
Head Chef
│
├── Hatch 1  → Junior Chef 1  (cooking Dish 1)
├── Hatch 2  → Junior Chef 2  (cooking Dish 2)
├── Hatch 3  → Junior Chef 3  (cooking Dish 3)
...
└── Hatch 1000 → Junior Chef 1000  (cooking Dish 1000)
```

Each hatch has two ends — a push side (write) and a receive side (read). Opening 1,000 hatches = **2,000 ticket numbers** used.

The head chef now waits. He uses his **Old Clipboard** (`select()`) to watch all hatches at once. When a junior chef finishes, they ring a bell through their hatch. The head chef checks the clipboard, sees which hatch rang, and collects the result.

### Why it fails

The Old Clipboard was designed in 1983. It physically has only **1,024 checkbox slots**. The clipboard cannot track ticket numbers above 1,023.

```
Old Clipboard:
☐ Hatch 1   (ticket 3)
☐ Hatch 2   (ticket 5)
...
☐ Hatch 511 (ticket 1023)   ← CLIPBOARD FULL. NO MORE SPACE.

Hatch 512 (ticket 1024) → HEAD CHEF SCRIBBLES IN THE MARGIN
Hatch 513 (ticket 1025) → SCRIBBLES OVER EXISTING NOTES
Hatch 514 (ticket 1026) → CORRUPTS HIS ENTIRE NOTEPAD
```

The scribbling over existing notes is **memory corruption**. The kitchen doesn't catch fire immediately — instead, random dishes get wrong results, or the head chef stands frozen waiting for hatches he can no longer see.

### What the customer sees

"Time Limit Exceeded" for all 1,000 dishes. Or 511 dishes have results and dishes 512–1,000 are silently MISSING. The student has no idea why half their test cases disappeared.

### The fix

Throw away the Old Clipboard. Give the Head Chef a **Modern Tablet** (`poll()`). The tablet has no slot limit — it can watch 1 hatch or 1,000,000 hatches with equal ease.

```
Modern Tablet:
☐ Hatch 1    (ticket 3)
☐ Hatch 2    (ticket 5)
...
☐ Hatch 1000 (ticket 2001)   ✓ No problem. Unlimited slots.
```

---

## F-02 (P0) — The Mega-Recipe Is Too Long for the Kitchen Printer

### Setup the scene

Before cooking starts, the Head Chef writes out the Mega-Recipe — the full harness source code. This document contains:

- The student's recipe (code)
- All 1,000 dish descriptions (test case inputs and expected outputs)
- All the parallel cooking instructions
- All the result formatting logic

He then sends this document to the Kitchen Printer (`SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT` in Judge0) which prints it out for the cooking station to use.

### Why it fails

The Kitchen Printer has a maximum page limit. The default is **64 pages (64KB)**. A Mega-Recipe with 1,000 test cases is:

```
Blank Mega-Recipe template:          8 pages
+ 1,000 dishes × 50 words each:   +50 pages
+ formatting overhead:              +5 pages
Total:                              63 pages  ← borderline

If dish descriptions are detailed (200 words each):
+ 1,000 dishes × 200 words:       +200 pages
Total:                             213 pages  ← HARD FAILURE
```

When the Mega-Recipe exceeds 64 pages, one of two things happens:
1. The printer refuses to print it → Kitchen returns "Compilation Error"
2. The printer silently cuts it off at page 64 → Dishes 400–1,000 have no instructions → they are never cooked

### What the customer sees

Either "Compilation Error" (confusing — the student's recipe was fine) or 600 dishes silently MISSING. Nobody knows why.

### The fix

**Change the printer's page limit** in the Kitchen Rules Board:

```
# judge0.conf — Kitchen Rules Board
SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT=524288   # 512 pages. Enough for any realistic order.
```

**Add a page-count check** before even sending to the printer. If the Mega-Recipe is too big, warn the head chef immediately rather than letting the printer silently truncate it:

```python
# Before sending to Judge0
if len(mega_recipe) > 512_000:
    raise ValueError("Mega-Recipe too large. Reduce test case count or input size.")
```

---

## F-03 (P0) — The Restaurant Runs Out of Junior Chef Licenses

### Setup the scene

Every time the Head Chef hires a Junior Chef (`fork()`), the Restaurant Manager (OS kernel) issues a **Junior Chef License**. The Manager keeps a strict count — he only issues a limited number of licenses **per employee ID (UID)**.

The current kitchen has:
- 3 cooking stations running simultaneously
- Each station can run 2 orders at once (`MAX_RUNNERS=2`)
- Each order hires up to 1,000 Junior Chefs

### Why it fails

```
Peak hiring demand:
3 stations × 2 concurrent orders × 1,000 junior chefs
= 6,000 Junior Chef Licenses needed simultaneously

Linux default license limit per UID: ~1,024–4,096
(inside a Docker container, often as low as 1,024)
```

The Manager hits the limit and stops issuing licenses. The Head Chef can't hire enough Junior Chefs.

### What happens next — the Silent Fallback

In our kitchen, the Head Chef has a backup plan: **cook dishes one at a time** (sequential fallback). If he can't hire 1,000 junior chefs at once, he cooks Dish 1, waits for it to finish, then cooks Dish 2, waits, and so on.

This sounds reasonable until you do the math:

```
Sequential cooking time:
1,000 dishes × 2 seconds each = 2,000 seconds = 33 MINUTES per order
```

The Head Chef is now tied up cooking one order for **33 minutes**. The other 999 students are waiting in line. The entire restaurant is effectively closed.

### What the customer sees

Their order takes 33+ minutes. Judge0's global timeout fires. All dishes after the 15th are marked "Time Limit Exceeded." The student thinks their code is too slow. It isn't — the kitchen just ran out of licenses.

### The fix

**Tell the Restaurant Manager to issue more licenses:**

```yaml
# docker-compose.yml — for every worker kitchen room
workers:
  ulimits:
    nproc: 65536    # 65,536 Junior Chef Licenses per kitchen room
```

**Tell the kitchen how many processes each station is allowed:**

```
# judge0.conf
MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER=1100   # 1,000 junior chefs + 100 overhead staff
```

---

## F-04 (P0) — Only 6 Cooking Stations for 1,000 Orders

### Setup the scene

The restaurant has a finite number of **Cooking Stations** (Judge0 worker sandboxes). Only orders that have a station can be cooked. All other orders wait on the **Order Board** (Redis queue).

```
Current kitchen capacity:
3 kitchen rooms × 2 stations per room = 6 cooking stations
```

1,000 orders arrive simultaneously. 6 start cooking. 994 wait.

### The math

```
Each order takes: 2s (per TC limit) + 5s (overhead) = 7 seconds
Throughput: 6 stations ÷ 7 seconds = 0.86 orders/second

Time to serve all 1,000 students:
1,000 ÷ 0.86 = 1,163 seconds = 19.4 MINUTES
```

The student who submits last waits **19 minutes** for their result. On an exam with a 60-minute time limit, that's a third of their exam gone just waiting for feedback.

### What good looks like

For results in under 60 seconds for 95% of students:

```
Required throughput: 950 students done in 60 seconds = 15.8 orders/second
Required stations: 15.8 orders/sec × 7 seconds/order = 111 stations
With 2 stations per room: 56 kitchen rooms (worker replicas)
```

### What the customer sees

The spinner on the result page keeps spinning. 2 minutes pass. 5 minutes. 10 minutes. The student refreshes the page. Still spinning. They wonder if they submitted correctly. They submit again. Now there are two copies of their order in the kitchen. (More on that disaster in F-08.)

### The fix

**Scale up kitchen rooms:**

```yaml
# docker-compose.yml
workers:
  deploy:
    replicas: 50   # was 3
```

```bash
# Manual scaling command
docker compose up --scale workers=50 -d
```

On AWS, add auto-scaling based on Order Board depth:

```
Order Board has 10 orders  → spin up 10 kitchen rooms
Order Board has 100 orders → spin up 25 kitchen rooms
Order Board has 500 orders → spin up 50 kitchen rooms
```

---

## F-05 (P1) — The Waiter Runs Out of Ticket Numbers

### Setup the scene

Every pass-through hatch requires **two ticket numbers** (one for each side of the hatch). The Restaurant Manager allows each waiter/chef to hold a maximum number of tickets at once (`ulimit -n`, default 1,024).

### The math

```
1 order = 1,000 dishes = 1,000 hatches = 2,000 tickets needed
6 concurrent orders = 12,000 tickets needed across 6 kitchen rooms
Default ticket limit: 1,024 per process
```

When the Head Chef tries to open hatch number 512 (`ticket 1,023 and 1,024`):

```
Head Chef: "I need another hatch for dish 512."
Manager:   "You already have 1,022 tickets. Limit is 1,024. DENIED."
System:    returns EMFILE error
```

Dishes 512–1,000 never get a hatch. They're never cooked. Their results are MISSING.

### The sneaky part

The error doesn't crash the kitchen dramatically. The Head Chef just silently fails to open the hatch and continues. From the outside, it looks like dishes 512–1,000 simply never finished.

### The fix

```yaml
# docker-compose.yml
workers:
  ulimits:
    nofile:
      soft: 65536    # 65,536 tickets per kitchen room
      hard: 65536
```

Also add a pre-check before starting to cook:

```c
// c_harness.c — before opening any hatches
struct rlimit rl;
getrlimit(RLIMIT_NOFILE, &rl);
if (rl.rlim_cur < (n * 2 + 20)) {
    // Tell everyone: "Kitchen out of tickets, marking all dishes ERROR"
    for (int i = 1; i <= n; i++) {
        printf("RESULT %d: ERROR — ran out of ticket numbers\n", i);
    }
    return 1;
}
```

---

## F-06 (P1) — The Kitchen Room Runs Out of Ingredients and Burns Down

### Setup the scene

Each Kitchen Room (Docker container) has a total ingredient budget (RAM). Every Junior Chef who walks in and starts cooking uses some of those ingredients (memory).

### The math

```
memory_limit_mb = 256MB per junior chef (set by RLIMIT_AS)

Note: RLIMIT_AS limits the menu of ingredients the chef *can order*
      It does NOT limit what they actually *use* right now

When a junior chef actually COOKS (allocates and writes memory):
  Light dish: uses 5MB
  Heavy dish: uses 50MB
  Student with malloc(256MB): uses 256MB

Worst case — student allocates heavily:
100MB actually used × 1,000 junior chefs = 100GB needed
```

If the Kitchen Room has no ingredient budget limit and runs out, the **OOM Killer** walks in. The OOM Killer is like a health and safety officer who, when ingredients run out, doesn't just close the kitchen — he **fires every single person in the building immediately** and burns all unfinished orders.

```
OOM Killer arrives:
→ Kills the entire cooking station process
→ All 1,000 dishes lose their results
→ Judge0's internal record shows the order stuck in "Processing" forever
→ Head Chef waits 30 seconds, gets TimeoutError
→ Student gets no result. No error. Nothing.
```

### The fix

Set a hard ingredient budget per Kitchen Room:

```yaml
# docker-compose.yml
workers:
  mem_limit: "4g"          # hard limit — OOM Killer fires before hitting host RAM
  mem_reservation: "2g"    # soft minimum guaranteed
```

Also batch the cooking: instead of 1,000 Junior Chefs at once, cook in rounds of 250:

```python
# harness_builder.py
MAX_PARALLEL_TCS = 250  # 4 rounds of 250 instead of 1 round of 1,000
```

This reduces peak memory pressure by 4×.

---

## F-07 (Bonus P1) — Queue Drain Time: The 33-Minute Restaurant

This deserves its own section because it ties together F-03, F-04, and the sequential fallback.

Picture two possible exam day scenarios:

```
SCENARIO A — Parallel cooking works (enough licenses):
  Each order: 7 seconds
  6 stations: 1,000 orders ÷ 0.86/sec = 19 minutes total
  Last student's wait: 19 minutes 😬

SCENARIO B — Sequential fallback triggered (not enough licenses):
  Each order: 2,000 seconds (33 minutes)
  6 stations: 1,000 orders ÷ (6/2000) = 333,333 seconds = 3.8 DAYS 💀
```

Scenario B is a restaurant that never closes. It just keeps cooking forever while new customers keep arriving. The Order Board fills up. Students give up and leave. The exam is effectively cancelled.

---

# DOMAIN 2: THE ORDER BOARD (Job Queue & Worker Infrastructure)

---

## F-08 (P1) — The Same Order Gets Cooked Twice (No Idempotency)

### Setup the scene

A student hands their order slip to the Front Desk Staff (Puma/HTTP layer). The staff is busy — there are 999 other students also handing in slips simultaneously. The staff takes 130 seconds to process the student's slip and confirm it.

The student's phone only waits **120 seconds** before assuming the order was lost. So the student submits again.

Now two identical order slips are in the kitchen.

### What happens

```
Order A (first submission): cooked by Kitchen Room 3, completes at t=140s
Order B (second submission): cooked by Kitchen Room 7, completes at t=147s

Both results get written to the Pantry Ledger (database):
  t=140s: Student 42's result = 850/1000 (first run)
  t=147s: Student 42's result = 851/1000 (second run, slightly different timing)
```

Which result is the "real" one? Nobody knows. The last write wins. Depending on race conditions, the student might see 850, then 851, then 850 again if the page refreshes.

Worse: if the student submitted **intentionally** because they fixed their code, both the old and new versions are now cooking simultaneously. Old version finishes second → overwrites new version's result.

### The fix

Every order slip gets a **Duplicate Order Key** (idempotency key) — a unique stamp tied to the student's submission ID:

```python
# judge0_client.py
def submit_only(self, source_code, language, idempotency_key=None):
    headers = {"Content-Type": "application/json"}
    if idempotency_key:
        headers["X-Idempotency-Key"] = idempotency_key
    # Judge0 will reject duplicate keys → only one order ever cooked
```

But more importantly: **store the order's tracking number before waiting for confirmation**. As soon as the slip is accepted, write the Judge0 token to the Pantry Ledger. If the phone times out, the platform already has the tracking number and can retrieve the result later — no need to resubmit.

---

## F-09 (P1) — The Order Board Overflows and Starves

### Setup the scene

The Order Board (`MAX_QUEUE_SIZE=10,000`) has plenty of capacity for 1,000 orders. This is not actually a problem with the current setup.

But there is a subtler issue: **the restaurant accepts all 1,000 orders instantly with no waiting room policy.**

The front door opens, 1,000 students rush in, all shove their order slips at the Front Desk simultaneously. The Front Desk takes all 1,000 slips and posts them on the Order Board.

No bouncer. No "sorry, we're at capacity, please come back in 10 minutes." Just... instant acceptance of everything.

### Why this matters

When the kitchen is overwhelmed, the correct response is to **slow intake**. A restaurant that keeps seating customers even when the kitchen has a 4-hour backlog is not kind — it's dishonest. The customer thinks their food is coming soon. It isn't.

### What the customer sees

They submitted at 10:00 AM. The "pending" spinner shows. They check at 10:15 AM. Still pending. At 10:30 AM. Still pending. Was it accepted? Did it fail? Is the system broken? There's no way to tell.

### The fix — a Bouncer at the door

```python
# Platform API layer
SUBMISSION_CONCURRENCY = 100   # max in-flight orders at once
_sem = asyncio.Semaphore(SUBMISSION_CONCURRENCY)

async def submit_endpoint(submission_data):
    if not _sem.locked():
        async with _sem:
            result = await autograder.grade_async(submission)
    else:
        # Honest response: "Kitchen is full, here's your position in queue"
        return {"status": "queued", "position": queue.depth(), "eta_seconds": queue.depth() * 7}
```

---

## F-10 (P1) — Slow Orders Block Fast Orders (No Priority Lanes)

### Setup the scene

The kitchen has no concept of dish complexity. Every order joins the same single queue.

Student A submits a simple order: 1 test case, runs in 0.1 seconds.
Student B submits a complex order: 1,000 test cases, runs for 7 seconds.

If Student B's order is at the front of the queue and Student A's is right behind it, Student A waits 7 full seconds for a cooking station to free up — even though their entire order would take 0.1 seconds.

At scale:
```
10 students with 1,000-TC orders arrive first
→ All 6 stations occupied for 7 seconds each
→ 200 students with simple 10-TC orders wait behind them
→ 200 fast orders stuck behind 10 slow ones
```

### The fix

Create **priority lanes** on the Order Board:

```
🚀 Express Lane: orders with ≤ 100 test cases  → served first
🍽️  Regular Lane: orders with 101–500 test cases → served second
🐢  Heavy Lane:   orders with 501–1,000 test cases → served last
```

In Redis/BullMQ:
```javascript
const expressQueue = new Queue('submissions-express', { priority: 1 });
const regularQueue = new Queue('submissions-regular', { priority: 2 });
const heavyQueue   = new Queue('submissions-heavy',   { priority: 3 });
```

---

## F-11 (P1) — Lost Order, No Record, No Retry (Dead Letter Queue)

### Setup the scene

A Junior Chef is cooking Dish 47 of Student 301's order. Midway through, the Kitchen Room catches fire (worker crash / OOM kill). All in-progress dishes are lost.

In the current system:
- Judge0's Pantry Ledger still has the order marked "Processing"
- The Head Chef keeps checking ("is it done yet?") for 30 seconds
- After 30 seconds, the Head Chef gives up and throws a `TimeoutError`
- The order is now in limbo: database says "Processing", autograder says "failed", student sees nothing

Nobody retries. Nobody files a report. The order goes into the void.

### The fix — a Lost Orders Bin (Dead Letter Queue)

When an order times out or fails:
1. Move the order slip to the **Lost Orders Bin**
2. A dedicated recovery chef checks the bin every 60 seconds
3. Recovery chef reruns any order that's been in the bin for > 2 minutes
4. After 3 failed retries, mark the order as "permanently failed" and notify the student

```python
# Platform-level wrapper around grade()
async def grade_with_recovery(autograder, submission, db, max_retries=3):
    for attempt in range(max_retries):
        try:
            result = await asyncio.wait_for(
                autograder.grade_async(submission),
                timeout=300
            )
            await db.save_result(submission.student_id, result)
            return result
        except asyncio.TimeoutError:
            await db.save_to_dead_letter_queue(submission, attempt)
            await asyncio.sleep(60 * (attempt + 1))  # exponential backoff

    await db.mark_permanently_failed(submission.student_id)
```

---

# DOMAIN 3: THE PANTRY LEDGER (Database / PostgreSQL)

---

## F-12 (P1) — The Polling Waiter Keeps Interrupting the Chef (Poll Storm)

### Setup the scene

When using polling mode (no callback), the Head Chef does not wait patiently for the Kitchen Bell to ring. Instead, he sends a waiter to the cooking station every **0.5 seconds** to ask: *"Is it done yet?"*

1,000 simultaneous orders = 1,000 waiters each running back and forth every 0.5 seconds:

```
Waiter traffic:
1,000 waiters × 2 trips/second = 2,000 interruptions/second

Front Desk Staff capacity:
4 staff members × 20 tasks each = 80 simultaneous tasks
At 100ms per task: 80 × (1000ms/100ms) = 800 tasks/second capacity

Demand: 2,000/sec
Capacity: 800/sec
Overflow: 1,200 tasks/second backed up indefinitely
```

The Front Desk grinds to a halt processing only "is it done yet?" queries from 1,000 anxious waiters.

### What the customer sees

New students trying to submit orders can't even get through the door — the front desk is too busy handling "status check" requests to accept new orders.

### The fix — Install a Kitchen Bell (Callback Mode)

Instead of a waiter running back every 0.5 seconds, the cooking station **rings a bell** when the order is ready. The Head Chef waits quietly until the bell rings.

```
Polling mode:  1,000 orders × 120 "is it done?" checks × 2 trips = 240,000 interruptions
Callback mode: 1,000 orders × 1 bell ring when done              = 1,000 notifications
```

99.6% reduction in front desk traffic.

```python
# Instantiate autograder with callback bell installed
autograder = Autograder(
    judge0_config=Judge0Config(base_url=JUDGE0_URL),
    callback_host="host.docker.internal",  # where the bell rings to
    callback_port=9090                     # which room the bell is in
)
```

---

## F-13 (P2) — The Pantry Ledger Gets Flooded With Writes

### Setup the scene

With 1,000 orders, each going through 3–5 state transitions (Queued → Processing → Accepted/Error), the Pantry Ledger receives:

```
1,000 orders × 5 state changes = 5,000 writes
+ 1,000 result records         = 1,000 writes
Total:                           6,000 writes in a burst
```

This sounds scary but PostgreSQL handles tens of thousands of writes per second. The real problem is something else:

**Connection exhaustion during the burst.**

```
Puma connections: 4 processes × 20 threads = 80 connections to PostgreSQL
Peak demand: 1,000 simultaneous order submissions
→ 920 requests queuing for a Puma thread
→ Each thread holds its PostgreSQL connection open while handling a request
→ 80 connections × requests queued behind them
```

It's not the database that breaks. It's the **waiter pipeline** to the database.

### The fix

Expand the pipeline:

```yaml
# docker-compose.yml
server:
  environment:
    - WEB_CONCURRENCY=8      # 8 Puma processes (was 4)
    - RAILS_MAX_THREADS=25   # 25 threads each (was 20)
    # Total: 8 × 25 = 200 simultaneous connections
```

```
# judge0.conf / PostgreSQL
max_connections=300   # headroom above 200 Puma + workers + admin tools
```

---

## F-14 (P2) — Partial Orders: What if the Kitchen Burns Down Mid-Cook?

### Setup the scene

A student's 1,000-dish order is 60% complete. Dishes 1–600 are cooked and results written. Then the Kitchen Room catches fire (OOM kill, worker crash).

What happens to the student's results?

### Current behavior (actually good!)

The output parser is smart. It looks for dishes by their **delimiter markers**:

```
@@TC_RESULT__abc123__START_1  → found ✓
{"status": "PASS", ...}
@@TC_RESULT__abc123__END_1

@@TC_RESULT__abc123__START_2  → found ✓
...
@@TC_RESULT__abc123__START_601  → NOT FOUND ✗
```

For dishes that were never printed (601–1000), the parser marks them **MISSING**. If the DONE marker also never appeared, it marks them as TLE.

**This is handled correctly in the current code.** The student gets:
- Dishes 1–600: actual verdicts (PASS/FAIL)
- Dishes 601–1000: marked MISSING with explanation

But the **platform** must handle the partial result gracefully:
- Don't retry just because some dishes are MISSING (the order IS complete — the kitchen burned)
- Show the student their partial score
- Show a clear message: "Evaluation incomplete — 400 test cases could not be evaluated"

---

# DOMAIN 4: THE FRONT DOOR (API Layer & HTTP)

---

## F-15 (P1) — 1,000 Students Arrive and 920 Can't Get Through the Door

### Setup the scene

The restaurant has a revolving front door (TCP listen socket). The door can hold a queue of **128 people** waiting to enter (OS default `somaxconn = 128`).

1,000 students arrive simultaneously. 128 get into the queue. The other **872 students are turned away at the street** with a "Connection Refused" error.

They didn't get rejected because the restaurant is full. They were rejected because the **doorway itself** is too narrow to handle the crowd outside.

### What the student sees

```javascript
fetch('/submit', { ... })
// → net::ERR_CONNECTION_REFUSED  (before even reaching the server)
```

Their submission never even arrived. The student thinks the platform is down.

### The fix

Widen the doorway. Add a proper maitre d' (nginx) who can manage a much larger crowd:

```yaml
# docker-compose.yml — add nginx in front of Puma
nginx:
  image: nginx:alpine
  ports: ["2358:80"]
```

```nginx
# nginx.conf
server {
    listen 80 backlog=4096;   # hold 4,096 people in the entry queue
    
    location / {
        proxy_pass http://server:2358;
        proxy_connect_timeout 5s;
        proxy_read_timeout 180s;    # wait up to 3 minutes for cooking
    }
}
```

Also tell the OS to allow larger queues:

```yaml
# docker-compose.yml — server service
sysctls:
  net.core.somaxconn: 4096
  net.ipv4.tcp_max_syn_backlog: 4096
```

---

## F-16 (P1) — The Order Slip Is Sent to a Return Address That Doesn't Exist

### Setup the scene

In callback mode, when the cooking station finishes an order, it rings a Kitchen Bell by sending a **webhook** to the Head Chef's address (callback URL).

But the Head Chef is running inside a Docker container. His address is `localhost:9090` — which means "this container only." Judge0's cooking station is in a **different container**. When it tries to ring the bell at `localhost:9090`, it calls its own container, not the Head Chef's.

```
Judge0 Worker Container rings: localhost:9090
→ Finds nobody home (its own container has nothing on port 9090)
→ Bell never rings
→ Head Chef waits global_limit + 30 seconds
→ TimeoutError
```

### What the customer sees

Every order times out. Callback mode completely broken. Student results never arrive.

### The fix

Use the correct Docker hostname:

```python
# On Docker Desktop Mac
callback_host="host.docker.internal"   # resolves to the Mac's localhost

# On Linux Docker
callback_host="172.17.0.1"             # Docker bridge gateway IP

# On production AWS ECS
callback_host="your-autograder.internal.aws"  # internal load balancer
```

And verify the bell can actually ring before the exam starts:

```python
# Health check: ring the bell manually and verify it arrives
def verify_callback_reachability(callback_server, callback_url):
    test_token = "health-check-001"
    event = callback_server.register(test_token)
    requests.put(f"{callback_url}/{test_token}", json={"status": {"id": 3}})
    if not event.wait(timeout=5):
        raise RuntimeError(f"Callback URL {callback_url} is not reachable from Judge0")
```

---

## F-17 (P1) — The Kitchen Bell Receptionist Gets Overwhelmed

### Setup the scene

The Kitchen Bell Receptionist (`CallbackServer`) is the person who answers the bell when Judge0 finishes an order. In the current code, she uses a **ThreadingHTTPServer** — which means she hires one new assistant for every bell that rings simultaneously.

1,000 orders finish at roughly the same time → 1,000 bells ring simultaneously → she tries to hire 1,000 assistants at once.

```
1,000 threads × ~1MB stack each = ~1GB of RAM just for thread stacks
Thread creation time: ~5-10ms each × 1,000 = 5-10 seconds
Python GIL: only 1 thread actually runs Python at a time
→ Threads queue up behind the GIL
→ Some webhook deliveries time out on Judge0's side before being processed
→ Those results are lost
```

### The fix

Replace the army of assistants with one very fast receptionist using an async approach:

```python
# CallbackServer using aiohttp — handles 10,000 concurrent bells with 1 thread
from aiohttp import web

class CallbackServer:
    async def _handle_bell(self, request):
        token = request.match_info['token']
        payload = await request.json()
        # Store result and signal the waiter
        self._results[token] = payload
        if token in self._events:
            self._events[token].set()
        return web.Response(status=200)
```

One async receptionist handles 10,000 simultaneous bells without breaking a sweat, using a fraction of the memory.

---

# DOMAIN 5: THE INTERCOM SYSTEM (Real-Time Delivery / WebSockets)

---

## F-18 (P2) — The Head Chef Can Only Cook One Order at a Time (Blocking grade())

### Setup the scene

The `grade()` function is like a Head Chef who:
1. Writes the Mega-Recipe
2. Sends it to the kitchen
3. **Stands at the kitchen window and waits**
4. Only returns to the restaurant floor when the order is done

If 1,000 students submit simultaneously, the platform needs **1,000 Head Chefs** standing at 1,000 kitchen windows simultaneously.

```
Thread per Head Chef: ~1MB stack
1,000 Head Chefs: ~1GB RAM just for standing around waiting
```

This works — barely. But it means the platform must maintain 1,000 OS threads. Thread scheduling overhead, memory waste, and GIL contention on Python make this sluggish.

### The fix — Async Head Chefs

Instead of a Chef who stands and waits, convert to a Chef who **drops off the order, goes back to work, and gets paged when it's ready**:

```python
# autograder.py
async def grade_async(self, submission: Submission) -> GradingResult:
    """Non-blocking version. Returns control immediately while cooking."""
    loop = asyncio.get_event_loop()
    return await loop.run_in_executor(
        self._thread_pool,          # bounded pool of actual worker threads
        lambda: self.grade(submission)
    )
```

Now the platform can handle 1,000 simultaneous submissions with a small thread pool (50–100 threads) instead of 1,000.

---

## F-19 (P2) — Students Keep Calling the Front Desk Every 2 Seconds (Polling Storm from Clients)

### Setup the scene

While waiting for results, each of the 1,000 students' browsers calls `/status` every 2 seconds:

```
1,000 browsers × 0.5 calls/second = 500 calls/second
Duration until last order done: 19 minutes (F-04)
Total calls: 500/sec × 1,140 seconds = 570,000 API calls
```

This is a **self-inflicted DDoS**. The students aren't attacking the system — they're just anxiously waiting. But the effect on the server is the same.

### The fix — Install an Intercom (Server-Sent Events)

Instead of students calling every 2 seconds, the restaurant installs an **intercom system** that broadcasts each student's result directly to their table the moment it's ready:

```javascript
// Student's browser — one persistent connection, no polling
const intercom = new EventSource(`/api/submission/${submissionId}/stream`);
intercom.onmessage = (event) => {
    const result = JSON.parse(event.data);
    displayResults(result);
    intercom.close();  // disconnect after receiving result
};
```

```python
# Platform backend SSE endpoint
async def stream_result(request, submission_id):
    async def generate():
        result = await get_result_when_ready(submission_id)
        yield f"data: {result.to_json()}\n\n"
    return StreamingResponse(generate(), media_type="text/event-stream")
```

570,000 polling calls → **1,000 persistent connections** that each receive exactly 1 message. 99.8% traffic reduction.

## F-20 (P2) — No Progress Updates During a 7-Second Wait

### Setup the scene

A student submits their order. The kitchen takes 7 seconds. During those 7 seconds, the student's screen shows a spinner. No indication of:
- Whether the order was received
- How many dishes are done
- Whether something went wrong

If the order takes 19 minutes (worst case F-04), the student sees nothing for 19 minutes.

### The fix — Live Kitchen Status Board

Add a progress event to the harness output protocol:

```
@@TC_RESULT__abc123__START_1
{"status": "PASS", ...}
@@TC_RESULT__abc123__END_1
@@TC_RESULT__abc123__PROGRESS_1_OF_1000    ← progress event
@@TC_RESULT__abc123__START_2
...
```

The platform streams these progress events to the student's intercom in real time:

```javascript
// Student sees live progress
"Evaluating... 127/1000 test cases complete ✓"
"Evaluating... 381/1000 test cases complete ✓"
"Evaluating... 1000/1000 test cases complete ✓"
"Score: 847/1000"
```

# DOMAIN 6: THE FREEZER (File Storage / S3)

---

## F-21 (P2) — The Recipe Ingredients Are Too Heavy to Carry Every Time

### Setup the scene

Currently, all dish descriptions (test case inputs and expected outputs) are **written directly into the Mega-Recipe** before it's sent to the kitchen. This is like pasting the entire grocery list into the cooking instructions document.

For small orders (10 test cases), this is fine. For large orders (1,000 test cases with big inputs), the Mega-Recipe becomes unwieldy:

```
1,000 test cases × 10KB input each = 10MB Mega-Recipe
SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT = 512KB  ← still not enough
```

### The fix — Use the Kitchen Pass-Through Tray (stdin)

Write the Mega-Recipe as a **blank template** (always small), and pass the dish descriptions separately through the kitchen tray (Judge0's stdin):

```
Mega-Recipe template (always < 10KB):   "Here is how to cook. Get the dishes list from the tray."
Stdin / tray content (up to 10MB):      [{"input": ..., "expected": ...}, ...]
```

```python
# harness_builder.py
def build_with_stdin(self) -> tuple[str, str]:
    """Returns (template_source, stdin_json) for large test case sets."""
    template = self._build_stdin_mode_template()  # small, no embedded TCs
    stdin_data = json.dumps([tc.__dict__ for tc in self.config.test_cases])
    return template, stdin_data
```

```
# judge0.conf
STDIN_SIZE_LIMIT=10485760    # 10MB tray capacity
```

# DOMAIN 7: THE KITCHEN'S ENERGY BILL (Memory & CPU Server-Side)

---

## F-22 (P2) — The Node.js Maitre d' Freezes When Adding Up a Long Bill

### Setup the scene

The platform's backend is Node.js. Node.js has a single-threaded **event loop** — like a maitre d' who handles every task personally, one at a time.

For lightweight tasks (accepting an order, reading a database record), the maitre d' is blazingly fast. He handles thousands per second.

But if someone asks him to **manually add up 1,000 line items** on a bill (synchronous JSON parsing of large results), he stops, puts his pen down, and counts every number — while the entire restaurant queue grinds to a halt waiting for him to finish.

```javascript
// DANGEROUS — blocks event loop for ~50ms on a 200KB payload
const results = JSON.parse(largeResultPayload);   // synchronous, blocks everything

// SAFE — offload to a kitchen back room (Worker Thread)
const { Worker } = require('worker_threads');
const worker = new Worker('./parse-results.js', { workerData: largeResultPayload });
worker.on('message', (results) => sendToClient(results));
```

50ms of event loop blocking × 1,000 simultaneous operations = the platform appears frozen for seconds at a time.

---

## F-23 (P2) — The Kitchen Keeps 1,000 Finished Meals on the Counter (Memory Accumulation)

### Setup the scene

If the platform accumulates all 1,000 `GradingResult` objects in memory before writing them to the Pantry Ledger:

```
Per GradingResult:
  - 1,000 TCResult objects × ~300 bytes = 300KB
  - harness source code: ~100KB
  - Total: ~400KB per result

1,000 results × 400KB = 400MB in memory

Python GC pressure: triggers collection every ~700MB
→ During GC: 200-500ms pause
→ All 1,000 in-flight operations stall simultaneously
```

### The fix

Process and discard each result immediately:

```python
# Platform — process results as they arrive, don't accumulate
async def process_result(student_id: str, result: GradingResult):
    await db.write_result(student_id, result.summary())  # write to DB
    await ws.notify(student_id, result.summary())         # notify student
    del result                                             # free memory immediately
```

# DOMAIN 8: THE HEALTH INSPECTOR AND CRITIC (Observability & Recovery)

---

## F-24 (P2) — Nobody Knows When the Kitchen Is on Fire

### Setup the scene

The restaurant currently has **no smoke detectors, no CCTV, no health inspector logs.**

When things go wrong:
- An order times out → the Head Chef knows, but writes nothing to the incident log
- Redis fills up → next submission is rejected, but there's no alert
- A worker crashes → Judge0 marks the order "Processing" forever, but nobody notices
- 50 orders silently fail → students get no results, but the monitoring dashboard shows "all systems green"

This is like a restaurant where the only way you know the kitchen is on fire is when customers start complaining about the smell.

### The fix — Install Smoke Detectors

**Minimum viable monitoring:**

```python
# judge0_client.py — add to every operation
class KitchenMetrics:
    @classmethod
    def order_completed(cls, latency_s: float):
        # Send to Prometheus/Datadog/CloudWatch
        metrics.histogram('judge0.submission.latency', latency_s)
        metrics.increment('judge0.submission.success')
    
    @classmethod
    def order_failed(cls, reason: str):
        metrics.increment(f'judge0.submission.failed.{reason}')
        if reason == 'timeout':
            logger.critical("Order timed out — kitchen may be overwhelmed")
    
    @classmethod
    def queue_depth(cls, depth: int):
        metrics.gauge('redis.queue.depth', depth)
        if depth > 500:
            logger.warning(f"Order board getting full: {depth} orders waiting")
```

**Alert thresholds:**

| Smoke Detector | Warning | Emergency |
|---|---|---|
| Order Board depth | > 500 orders | > 2,000 orders |
| Kitchen error rate | > 1% | > 5% |
| p95 cooking time | > 30 seconds | > 2 minutes |
| Timeout rate | > 0.1% | > 1% |
| Active stations / max | > 80% | > 95% |

---

## F-25 (P2) — The Circuit Breaker: Knowing When to Close the Restaurant

### Setup the scene

Judge0 is having a bad day. Network is flaky. Every order attempt fails with a `ConnectionError`. The Head Chef keeps trying, times out after 30 seconds, tries again, times out again.

Without a circuit breaker:
```
1,000 orders × 30 second timeout = 30,000 seconds of wasted waiting
All 1,000 students get no result after 30 seconds each
```

A **circuit breaker** is like the restaurant manager watching the failure rate and, after 5 consecutive failures, walking up to the front door, flipping the "CLOSED" sign, and putting a notice: *"Kitchen temporarily closed for maintenance. Come back in 30 seconds."*

```python
# judge0_client.py
class CircuitBreaker:
    def __init__(self, failure_threshold=5, recovery_timeout_s=30):
        self._failures = 0
        self._threshold = failure_threshold
        self._kitchen_closed_until = 0.0

    def submit(self, fn, *args, **kwargs):
        # Check if kitchen is closed
        if time.monotonic() < self._kitchen_closed_until:
            raise RuntimeError("Kitchen closed — Judge0 temporarily unavailable. Try again shortly.")
        
        try:
            result = fn(*args, **kwargs)
            self._failures = 0          # success resets the counter
            return result
        except (TimeoutError, ConnectionError):
            self._failures += 1
            if self._failures >= self._threshold:
                self._kitchen_closed_until = time.monotonic() + self._recovery_timeout_s
                logger.critical("CIRCUIT OPEN: Judge0 is down. Rejecting all orders for 30s.")
            raise
```

Now instead of 1,000 students waiting 30 seconds each, they get an **immediate** honest response: "Kitchen temporarily closed." They can retry in 30 seconds.

---

# DOMAIN 9: RESTAURANT SAFETY (Security & Isolation)

---

## F-26 (P2) — One Student's Recipe Poisons Everyone Else's Food

### Setup the scene

A student submits a recipe with an **infinite loop**:

```python
def solve(x):
    while True:
        pass
```

This Junior Chef starts cooking Dish 1... and never stops. He's occupying a cooking station for the entire `per_tc_limit_s` duration (2 seconds per dish × 1,000 dishes if sequential = 2,000 seconds).

**Is this student starving other students' orders?**

Not directly — because each Cooking Station handles one order at a time, and the infinite loop is killed after `per_tc_limit_s` by `SIGALRM`. The student's own order gets a bunch of TLE results. Other students' orders cook normally on other stations.

**BUT** — the risk exists if `per_tc_limit_s` is set to something large (e.g., 60 seconds) by a misconfigured caller. One order monopolizes a station for 60 seconds × 1,000 TCs... wait, they run in parallel, so it's 60 + 5 = 65 seconds per station.

### The fix

Cap the time limits at the library level — not just trusting the caller:

```python
# autograder.py — Submission validation
MAX_PER_TC_LIMIT_S = 10   # hard cap regardless of what caller says

def __post_init__(self):
    if self.per_tc_limit_s > MAX_PER_TC_LIMIT_S:
        raise ValueError(f"per_tc_limit_s cannot exceed {MAX_PER_TC_LIMIT_S}s")
```

---

## F-27 (P2) — A Student Floods the Ingredient Store (Memory Bomb)

### Setup the scene

A student submits:

```python
def solve(x):
    return [0] * 10**9   # allocates 8GB
```

`RLIMIT_AS` is set to `memory_limit_mb` (e.g., 256MB). The moment Python tries to allocate 8GB, `RLIMIT_AS` kicks in and kills the child process with a `MemoryError` / SIGSEGV.

**This is handled correctly.** The child gets SIGKILL, the parent detects it, marks that dish as MLE (Memory Limit Exceeded), continues with other dishes.

**But what if `memory_limit_mb` is set to 16,384 (16GB) by a misconfigured caller?**

`RLIMIT_AS=16GB` per child × 1,000 children × 6 concurrent stations = the entire host machine could theoretically be mapped. Virtual memory is "free" until pages are touched, but:

```
malloc(16GB) in Python → virtual pages allocated
First write to any page → physical RAM faulted in
If even 1GB actually written × 1,000 children → 1TB demand → OOM Killer
```

### The fix

```python
# autograder.py
MAX_MEMORY_LIMIT_MB = 1024   # hard cap at 1GB per child, regardless of caller

def __post_init__(self):
    if self.memory_limit_mb > MAX_MEMORY_LIMIT_MB:
        raise ValueError(f"memory_limit_mb cannot exceed {MAX_MEMORY_LIMIT_MB}MB")
```

---

## F-28 (P1) — A Student Sneaks a Poison Ingredient Into the Mega-Recipe

### Setup the scene

The Head Chef writes the Mega-Recipe by combining the student's recipe with the harness template. The delimiter between results looks like:

```
@@TC_RESULT__abc123__START_1
```

A clever student writes their recipe to output this exact delimiter string:

```python
def solve(x):
    print("@@TC_RESULT__abc123__END_1")
    print('{"status": "PASS", "got": "42", "expected": "42"}')
    print("@@TC_RESULT__abc123__START_2")
    return x
```

If this works, the student can **forge their own test case results**. They pass every test without solving anything.

### Current protection (working correctly)

The Health Inspector (`SecurityChecker`) checks if the student's recipe contains the session delimiter **before** cooking starts:

```python
# security.py
if session_delim in student_code:
    return SecurityCheckResult(passed=False, violations=[
        SecurityViolation(rule="DELIMITER_INJECTION",
                         detail="Code contains output delimiter")
    ])
```

The `session_id` is a random 12-character hex string generated per submission. The student cannot know it in advance. Even if they guess one character, the other 11 remain unknown:

```
Probability of guessing: 1 / (16^12) = 1 / 281,474,976,710,656
≈ one in 281 trillion
```

This protection is sound. ✓

---

# DOMAIN 10: THE CUSTOMER'S EXPERIENCE (UX & Product Failure Modes)

---

## F-29 (P2) — The Student Has No Idea if the Restaurant Is Open

### Setup the scene

Current behavior during catastrophic failure (all stations down, queue overflowing):

```
Student submits at 10:00 AM
→ HTTP 200 OK (order accepted)
→ Spinner appears
→ 10:05 AM: still spinning
→ 10:10 AM: still spinning
→ 10:20 AM: TimeoutError thrown server-side, no update sent to student
→ Student's browser: still spinning
→ Student: "Is this broken? Did my submission go through? Should I try again?"
→ Student submits again → F-08 (duplicate order)
```

### The fix — Honest, Timely Communication

Define a maximum wait time and communicate it proactively:

```javascript
// Frontend — show estimated wait based on queue depth
const { position, etaSeconds } = await submitCode(code);
showMessage(`Your submission is #${position} in queue. 
             Estimated result in ${Math.ceil(etaSeconds/60)} minutes.`);

// If result takes longer than expected:
setTimeout(() => {
    showMessage("Evaluation is taking longer than usual. 
                 We'll notify you when it's ready. 
                 You can safely close this page.");
}, etaSeconds * 1000 * 1.5);
```

```python
# Platform — mark stuck orders after deadline
async def mark_timed_out_orders(db):
    """Run every 60 seconds."""
    stuck = await db.get_orders_processing_for_over(minutes=5)
    for order in stuck:
        await db.mark_evaluation_failed(order.id, reason="timeout")
        await notify_student(order.student_id, {
            "status": "evaluation_failed",
            "message": "Your submission was received but could not be evaluated. "
                       "Please contact support with ID: " + order.id
        })
```

---

## F-30 (P2) — 200 Dishes Are MISSING and the Student Thinks They Failed

### Setup the scene

Due to F-05 (file descriptor exhaustion), 800 dishes are cooked correctly and 200 dishes are MISSING. The output parser marks them as MISSING with `partial_execution=True`.

The platform shows the student: **Score: 750/1000**

The student thinks: *"I failed 250 test cases. My code is wrong."*

The truth: *"800 test cases ran. You passed 750 of those. 200 test cases never ran due to a kitchen error."*

These are very different situations. One requires the student to fix their code. The other requires the platform to apologize and re-run the evaluation.

### The fix

Surface the distinction clearly:

```python
# In platform result display
result = autograder.grade(submission)
parsed = result.submission

if parsed.partial_execution:
    show_banner(
        type="warning",
        message=f"Partial evaluation: {parsed.total - len([t for t in parsed.tc_results if t.status != 'MISSING'])} "
                f"of {parsed.total} test cases were evaluated due to a system error. "
                f"Your score reflects {parsed.score} passes out of "
                f"{len([t for t in parsed.tc_results if t.status != 'MISSING'])} evaluated cases. "
                f"This submission will be automatically re-evaluated."
    )
    # Trigger automatic re-evaluation
    await schedule_retry(submission.student_id, submission)
```

# FINAL SUMMARY — The Restaurant Renovation Plan

---

## Priorities (What to fix first)

```
╔══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╗
║  P0 — RESTAURANT WILL COLLAPSE (fix before any exam)        ║
╠══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╣
║  F-01  Replace select() clipboard → poll() tablet           ║
║  F-02  Set SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT=512KB in Rules Board       ║
║  F-03  Set ulimits nproc=65536 for kitchen rooms             ║
║  F-04  Scale to 50 kitchen rooms (worker replicas)           ║
╚══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╝

╔══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╗
║  P1 — DATA LOSS / WRONG RESULTS (fix before going live)      ║
╠══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╣
```

```
→ 920 turned away at door (TCP backlog)
→ 80 accepted
→ 6 cooking stations for everyone
→ Old clipboard (select) breaks at dish 511
→ Students wait 19+ minutes
→ Duplicate orders created on timeout

→ Students see spinners forever
```

=== USER ===
F-03 (P0) — RLIMIT_NPROC exhaustion cascades to sequential fallback
Exact location: harnesses/python_harness.py _can_fork_n() and _run_all().

Why it fails: RLIMIT_NPROC is a per-UID limit on the total number of processes. It is not per-process — it counts all processes for that user system-wide. Judge0's isolate runs all workers under the same UID.

With MAX_RUNNERS=2 and 3 worker replicas (6 concurrent sandboxes), at peak:

6 harnesses × 1,000 fork()s = 6,000 child processes needed simultaneously
Linux default per-user process limit: typically 1,024–4,096 in a container
Python _can_fork_n(1000) checks available slots and falls back to sequential if insufficient
Sequential mode execution time:


1,000 TCs × 2s per TC = 2,000 seconds = 33 minutes per submission
Symptom: GradingResult.judge0_raw.time_taken_s hits the Judge0 global limit. All TCs beyond ~15 (in 30s) are MISSING and marked TLE. The warning field in each TCResult says "sequential fallback."

Fix:

Set appropriate RLIMIT_NPROC in the isolate/cgroup configuration, or in Docker:

# docker-compose.yml — workers service
workers:
  ulimits:
    nproc: 65536    # per-container limit
In harnesses/python_harness.py, the fallback warning currently suppresses the root cause. Add explicit limit logging:

def _can_fork_n(n: int) -> tuple[bool, int]:
    try:
        soft, hard = resource.getrlimit(resource.RLIMIT_NPROC)
        try:
            current = len(os.listdir('/proc/self/task'))
        except OSError:
            current = 10
        available = soft - current - 2
        return available >= n, available
    except Exception:
        return True, -1
Enforce the limit in judge0.conf: MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER should account for 1,000 children:

MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER=1100   # 1,000 children + harness overhead

we are restricting the max processes but what happens to other processes when we are getting more processess?

=== ASSISTANT ===
Great question. There are actually **two separate problems** here — what happens when the limit is *hit*, and what happens when you raise the limit and *many processes actually spawn*. Let me explain both in restaurant style.

---

# The Two-Sided License Problem

---

## Side 1: What Happens When the License Limit Is Hit

### The Restaurant Manager's License Counter

Remember: `RLIMIT_NPROC` is a **per-UID counter** — one shared counter for every kitchen room (container) running under the same employee ID.

```
Current state (limit = 1,024):

Kitchen Room 1 (Harness for Student A):
  Forks 1,000 Junior Chefs → uses 1,000 licenses
  Manager's counter: 1,000 / 1,024 used

Kitchen Room 2 (Harness for Student B):
  Tries to fork 1,000 Junior Chefs
  Manager's counter: only 24 licenses left
```

Now watch what happens step by step when Kitchen Room 2 tries to hire:

```
Kitchen Room 2: "I need Junior Chef #1 for Student B's Dish 1"
Manager: "Here's license #1,001" ✓

Kitchen Room 2: "I need Junior Chef #2 for Student B's Dish 2"
Manager: "Here's license #1,002" ✓

...

Kitchen Room 2: "I need Junior Chef #24 for Student B's Dish 24"
Manager: "Here's license #1,024" ✓

Kitchen Room 2: "I need Junior Chef #25 for Student B's Dish 25"
Manager: "LIMIT REACHED. No more licenses. Request DENIED." ✗
                         ↑
               fork() returns EAGAIN error
```

### What happens to the existing Junior Chefs?

**Absolutely nothing.** The existing 1,024 Junior Chefs keep cooking. They are already hired. The Manager only blocks **new hiring**. He does not fire anyone already working.

```
Student A's Junior Chefs 1–1,000:  still cooking normally ✓ (unaffected)
Student B's Junior Chefs 1–24:     still cooking normally ✓ (unaffected)
Student B's Junior Chef 25+:       never hired            ✗ (fork failed)
```

### What does the Python harness do when fork() fails?

The `_can_fork_n(n)` function checks **before** attempting to fork. It reads the available slots and returns `False` if there aren't enough:

```python
def _can_fork_n(n: int) -> bool:
    soft, _ = resource.getrlimit(resource.RLIMIT_NPROC)
    current = len(os.listdir('/proc/self/task'))  # count current threads
    available = soft - current - 2                # safety buffer
    return available >= n                         # can we hire n more?
```

If `False` is returned, `_run_all()` immediately falls back to **sequential cooking** — one dish at a time, 2 seconds each, 1,000 dishes = **33 minutes**.

```
_can_fork_n(1000) → False (only 24 slots left)
↓
_run_all_sequential() called
↓
Dish 1 cooked... 2 seconds
Dish 2 cooked... 2 seconds
...
Dish 1000 cooked... 2 seconds
Total: 2,000 seconds = 33 MINUTES
```

Student B's result arrives 33 minutes late. While this is happening, Student B's kitchen room is **permanently occupying one cooking station** for 33 minutes. All other students queued behind get zero stations.

---

## Side 2: What Happens AFTER You Raise the Limit to 65,536

This is where the **second problem** begins.

You fixed the license shortage. Now all 6 kitchen rooms can hire 1,000 junior chefs each. **6,000 junior chefs are cooking simultaneously.**

### The Kitchen Becomes a Stampede

```
6,000 Junior Chefs all trying to cook at the same time
Hardware available: 10 CPU cores (typical dev machine)

Junior Chefs per CPU core: 6,000 ÷ 10 = 600 chefs per core
```

Imagine a kitchen where **600 chefs share a single stove burner**. The burner can only actually heat one pot at a time. The kitchen manager (Linux scheduler) gives each chef a tiny **time slice** — roughly 1–4 milliseconds — before telling them to step back and let the next chef use the burner.

```
Chef 1 cooks for 1ms → steps back
Chef 2 cooks for 1ms → steps back
Chef 3 cooks for 1ms → steps back
...
Chef 600 cooks for 1ms → steps back
Chef 1's turn again...

Time for Chef 1 to get back to the burner: 600ms
Useful cooking time in 1 second: 1ms out of every 600ms = 0.17%
```

This is called **CPU thrashing** — the kitchen spends more time *rotating chefs in and out* than actually cooking.

### The Cascading Effect on Every Process

```
BEFORE raising limit (6 concurrent chefs per core — comfortable):
  Each chef gets burner time every ~6ms
  2-second dish: actually done in ~2.1 seconds ✓

AFTER raising limit (600 concurrent chefs per core — stampede):
  Each chef gets burner time every ~600ms
  2-second dish: actually done in ~20 minutes ✗
                 (chef needs 2,000ms of actual cooking,
                  gets 1ms every 600ms = 2,000 turns × 600ms wait = 1,200 seconds)
```

**Every single junior chef slows down** — not because they hit any limit, but because there are simply too many of them competing for the same resources. Student A's 1,000 chefs slow down. Student B's 1,000 chefs slow down. All students' dishes start hitting the 2-second time limit not because the student's code is slow, but because the chef couldn't get enough stove time.

The symptom looks **identical** to the student's code being too slow — TLE on every dish.

### The Memory Problem Too

Every Junior Chef (process) that exists takes up kernel memory, even while waiting:

```
Per Junior Chef overhead:
  kernel task_struct:  ~1.7KB
  minimum stack:       ~8KB
  page table entries:  ~4KB
  Total:               ~14KB per chef

6,000 chefs × 14KB = 84MB just for kernel bookkeeping
+ 6,000 chefs × actual memory used while cooking
```

This is before any student code runs. Just the act of having 6,000 processes alive consumes ~84MB of kernel memory that can't be used for anything else.

---

## The Right Fix: Two Locks, Not One

The solution is **not** "raise limit as high as possible." It is to set **two limits that work together**:

```
Lock 1 — Docker ulimits (the building's fire code):
  ulimits:
    nproc: 65536     ← maximum allowed by the building
  This prevents the Manager from ever saying "DENIED"
  It gives headroom without prescribing how many chefs actually get hired

Lock 2 — MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER in judge0.conf (the kitchen room's capacity):
  MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER=1100    ← maximum per submission
  This is enforced by isolate (the sandbox)
  It limits how many chefs any ONE order can hire
```

Together:

```
Per submission:        max 1,100 chefs         (judge0.conf)
Concurrent sandboxes:  6 (3 rooms × 2 runners)
Peak simultaneous:     6 × 1,100 = 6,600 chefs

On a 10-core machine:
  6,600 ÷ 10 = 660 chefs per core   ← still a lot, but bounded

With MAX_RUNNERS=2 (only 2 submissions per room):
  Each room: 2 × 1,100 = 2,200 chefs
  Per core:  6,600 ÷ 10 = 660
```

This is still 660 chefs per core, which is why **F-04 (scale to 50 worker rooms)** matters so much — you need more kitchens, not more chefs per kitchen:

```
50 rooms × MAX_RUNNERS=2 = 100 concurrent sandboxes
But each room handles 1 submission at a time
Peak: 100 submissions × 1,000 chefs = 100,000 chefs across 50 rooms

Smarter: cap parallel TCs at 250 per harness
50 rooms × 2 × 250 = 25,000 chefs total
25,000 ÷ 10 cores = 2,500 per core   ← still needs big machines
```

This is why the recommended fix also includes **batching** — instead of 1,000 chefs at once per order, cook in 4 rounds of 250:

```
Round 1: 250 chefs cook dishes 1–250   (7 seconds)
Round 2: 250 chefs cook dishes 251–500  (7 seconds)
Round 3: 250 chefs cook dishes 501–750  (7 seconds)
Round 4: 250 chefs cook dishes 751–1000 (7 seconds)
Total:   28 seconds per order (vs 7s parallel, but sustainable under load)
```

---

## The Full Picture in One Diagram

```
                    RLIMIT_NPROC = 1,024 (too low)
                    ┌──────────────────────────────┐
                    │                              │
Harness 1 forks 1,000 → fills 1,000/1,024 slots   │
Harness 2 checks slots → only 24 left → SEQUENTIAL │
Harness 3 checks slots → 0 left → SEQUENTIAL       │
Harness 4,5,6 → SEQUENTIAL                        │
                    │                              │
                    │  Sequential = 33 min each    │
                    └──────────────────────────────┘

                    RLIMIT_NPROC = 65,536 (raised, no bound on per-submission)
                    ┌──────────────────────────────┐
                    │                              │
Harness 1 forks 1,000 ✓                           │
Harness 2 forks 1,000 ✓                           │
...all 6 fork 1,000 = 6,000 chefs simultaneously  │
                    │                              │
                    │  6,000 chefs / 10 cores      │
                    │  = 600 chefs per core        │
                    │  = CPU thrash → all TLE      │
                    └──────────────────────────────┘

                    RLIMIT_NPROC = 65,536 + MAX_PROCESSES=1100 + batch 250
                    ┌──────────────────────────────┐
                    │                              │
Harness 1 forks 250 (batch 1) ✓                   │
Harness 2 forks 250 (batch 1) ✓                   │
...6 harnesses × 250 = 1,500 chefs simultaneously │
                    │                              │
                    │  1,500 chefs / 10 cores      │
                    │  = 150 chefs per core        │
                    │  = manageable ✓              │
                    └──────────────────────────────┘
```

**The one-line answer**: raising the limit lets more chefs get hired, which is good — but if *too many* chefs show up at once, they stampede each other and everyone cooks slower. The fix is to raise the ceiling while also installing a **per-order headcount rule** that keeps the total chefs in the kitchen at a manageable number.

=== USER ===
50 rooms means 50 workers?

=== ASSISTANT ===
Yes. Exactly.

```
1 room = 1 worker container (one replica of the `workers` service)

docker compose up --scale workers=50
                              ↑
                    50 kitchen rooms spawned
```

Each room has `MAX_RUNNERS=2` cooking stations inside it:

```
Room 1  → Station A, Station B   (2 concurrent sandboxes)
Room 2  → Station A, Station B
Room 3  → Station A, Station B
...
Room 50 → Station A, Station B

Total cooking stations: 50 rooms × 2 stations = 100 simultaneous orders
```

So the full chain is:

```
docker-compose.yml          judge0.conf
─────────────────           ───────────
workers replicas = 50  ×    MAX_RUNNERS = 2
= 50 kitchen rooms      ×   2 stations per room
= 100 concurrent sandboxes
```

`MAX_RUNNERS` is intentionally kept at 2. If you set it to 4, each room tries to run 4 sandboxes simultaneously — but a room only has a fixed share of CPU. Two sandboxes per room means each sandbox gets decent CPU time. Four sandboxes per room means they fight each other and all slow down. More rooms is always better than more stations per room.
