# From Student Submission to Test Case Result — The Complete Restaurant Journey

Every single step. Every single function call. In restaurant style.

---

## The Cast (Quick Reminder)

```
👨‍🎓 Student           = Person submitting code
📋 Submission object  = The filled-out order form
🕵️ Health Inspector   = SecurityChecker
📖 Recipe Writer      = HarnessBuilder
🧾 Mega-Recipe        = The harness source code (wraps student code + all TCs)
🎫 Order Token        = Judge0 submission token (tracking number)
📝 Order Board        = Redis queue
🍳 Cooking Station    = Judge0 worker sandbox (isolate)
👨‍🍳 Head Chef          = Parent harness process
👶 Junior Chef        = Forked child process (one per test case)
🔔 Kitchen Bell       = Webhook callback
📊 Result Ledger      = OutputParser
🏆 Final Score Card   = GradingResult
```

---

## THE FULL JOURNEY — 9 Stations

---

## Station 1 — The Student Walks In and Fills an Order Form

The student finishes writing their code on the platform. They click **Submit**.

The platform takes everything the student gave — their code, the language, the test cases, the time limits — and fills out a standardized **Order Form**:

```python
# autograder.py — Submission dataclass
submission = Submission(
    student_id      = "student_042",
    language        = "python",
    student_code    = "def solve(a, b):\n    return a + b",
    test_cases      = [
        TestCase(inputs=[2, 3],   expected=5),
        TestCase(inputs=[10, 20], expected=30),
        TestCase(inputs=[7, 8],   expected=15),
    ],
    mode            = "function",
    function_name   = "solve",
    per_tc_limit_s  = 2,       # each dish must be done in 2 seconds
    memory_limit_mb = 256,     # each dish gets 256MB of ingredients
    param_types     = ["int", "int"],
    return_type     = "int",
)
```

This form is handed to the **Autograder** — the restaurant manager who coordinates everything:

```python
# autograder.py — Autograder.grade()
result = autograder.grade(submission)
```

The `grade()` function is the **entire restaurant** in one method. Everything that follows happens inside it.

---

## Station 2 — The Recipe Writer Generates a Secret Order Code

**Before** the Health Inspector even looks at the student's code, the Recipe Writer (`HarnessBuilder`) is called first. Why? Because the Health Inspector needs a **secret code** (the session delimiter) that is unique to this order — so the Inspector can check whether the student's recipe contains that secret code (delimiter injection attack).

```python
# autograder.py line 103
builder = HarnessBuilder(config)
```

Inside `HarnessBuilder.__init__()`:

```python
# harness_builder.py line 66-67
self.session_id = uuid.uuid4().hex[:12]        # e.g. "a3f9c1e82b40"
self.delim      = f"@@TC_RESULT__{self.session_id}__"
# → "@@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__"
```

Think of this like the kitchen stamping every order with a **unique secret barcode** before anything else happens. This barcode will later appear on every dish result. Nobody outside the kitchen knows what today's barcode looks like.

```
Order barcode generated: @@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__
```

The Recipe Writer does NOT write the full Mega-Recipe yet. Just the barcode. The actual Mega-Recipe writing happens later (Station 4).

---

## Station 3 — The Health Inspector Checks the Recipe for Poison

Now the Health Inspector (`SecurityChecker`) gets the student's recipe and the secret barcode. She has two jobs:

**Job 1: Check if the student tried to forge their own barcode** (delimiter injection)

```python
# security.py — inside SecurityChecker.check()
if session_delim in student_code:
    return SecurityCheckResult(passed=False, violations=[
        SecurityViolation(rule="DELIMITER_INJECTION", ...)
    ])
```

If the student's code contains `@@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__`, they're trying to **print fake results** that look like legitimate kitchen output. Rejected immediately.

**Job 2: Check for dangerous ingredients in the recipe**

For Python, the Inspector uses **X-ray vision** (AST — Abstract Syntax Tree analysis). She doesn't just read the recipe — she breaks it down into a tree of every single instruction:

```python
# security.py — _check_python_ast()
tree = ast.parse(student_code)
# Walks every node: imports, function calls, attribute access
```

She checks for:

```
BLOCKED INGREDIENTS:
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━
import os              → can read/delete files
import subprocess      → can run shell commands
import socket          → can connect to internet
import signal          → can override kitchen timers
exec(...)              → can run arbitrary code
eval(...)              → same
__import__("os")       → sneaky way to import os
().__class__.__bases__ → can escape Python sandbox
io.open(...)           → bypasses the safe open() we installed
pathlib.Path(...)      → another bypass for file reading
```

If Python, she also checks:

```python
# Does "import os as o" get caught?
# YES — visit_Import() checks alias.asname too:
# "Module 'os' is not allowed (aliased as 'o')"
```

For C/C++/Java, she uses **pattern matching** (regex) instead of X-ray:

```
C/C++ BLOCKED PATTERNS:
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━
system(...)            → shell execution
fork()                 → spawning more processes (student code, not harness)
exec*()                → replacing current process
#include <sys/socket>  → network access
asm / __asm__          → raw CPU instructions
signal(SIGALRM, ...)   → overriding the kitchen timer
```

**If the Inspector finds anything dangerous:**

```python
# autograder.py line 111-124
if not sec.passed:
    empty_sub = ParsedSubmission(tc_results=[], total=len(submission.test_cases), score=0)
    return GradingResult(
        student_id     = submission.student_id,
        security_error = sec.reason,   # "BlockedImport (line 1): Module 'os' not allowed"
        submission     = empty_sub,
        judge0_raw     = None,         # never sent to kitchen
        harness_code   = "",
    )
```

The student gets **immediately rejected** — score 0, no cooking, no Judge0. The Health Inspector's decision is final.

**If the recipe passes inspection**, the Inspector stamps it: ✓ APPROVED.

---

## Station 4 — The Recipe Writer Wraps the Student's Recipe in a Mega-Recipe

Now the real work begins. The Recipe Writer (`HarnessBuilder.build()`) takes the student's approved recipe and wraps it inside a **Mega-Recipe** — a complete, self-contained cooking program that knows how to:

1. Run all test cases in parallel
2. Enforce time and memory limits on each dish
3. Print results with the secret barcode

But first, a quick **sanitization** step:

```python
# autograder.py line 127-131
config.student_code = sanitize_for_injection(submission.student_code, submission.language)
harness_code = builder.build()
```

`sanitize_for_injection()` does two things:
- **Python**: strips trailing whitespace from each line (prevents indentation errors when code is pasted into the harness template)
- **C/C++/Java**: replaces `*/` with `* /` (prevents the student's code from accidentally closing a harness comment block)

Now `builder.build()` dispatches to the language-specific builder. Let's follow **Python** since it's the most visible:

### Python Mega-Recipe Construction (`_build_python()`)

```python
# harness_builder.py line 80-129
def _build_python(self) -> str:
    template = (_HARNESSES_DIR / "python_harness.py").read_text()
```

The Recipe Writer picks up the **blank recipe template** from `harnesses/python_harness.py` — a 515-line Python program with placeholders.

Then she fills in the blanks:

```python
filled = template.format(
    session_id      = "a3f9c1e82b40",
    mode            = "function",
    student_code    = "def solve(a, b):\n    return a + b",
    test_cases_json = '[{"input": [2,3], "expected": 5}, ...]',
    per_tc_limit_s  = 2,
    memory_limit_mb = 256,
)
```

**The brace-escaping trick**: The student's code might contain `{` and `}` characters (dict literals, f-strings). Python's `.format()` would mistake those for template placeholders and crash. So before filling, every `{` in student code becomes `{{` and every `}` becomes `}}`. After `.format()` runs, `{{` becomes `{` again.

**The sentinel trick**: The student's raw source code is needed inside `_STUDENT_SOURCE` for stdio mode. But we can't pass it through `.format()` safely. So a null-byte sentinel `\x00STUDENT_SOURCE_RAW\x00` is inserted as a placeholder, and the actual code is substituted AFTER `.format()` completes.

The finished Mega-Recipe looks like this (simplified):

```python
# ═══ GENERATED MEGA-RECIPE (python_harness.py filled) ═══

import os, sys, json, signal, resource, builtins
_real_open = open

# ── SECURITY: install safe versions of dangerous functions ──
def _safe_open(file, mode='r', *args, **kwargs):
    if str(file) == __file__:
        raise PermissionError("Cannot read harness file")
    return _real_open(file, mode, *args, **kwargs)
builtins.open = _safe_open        # student's open() calls go here

def _safe_signal(sig, handler):
    if sig == signal.SIGALRM:
        raise ValueError("Cannot override SIGALRM")
    return _real_signal(sig, handler)
signal.signal = _safe_signal      # student can't override the kitchen timer

# ── STUDENT'S RECIPE (pasted in here) ──
def solve(a, b):
    return a + b

# ── SECRET BARCODE ──
DELIM = "@@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__"

# ── TEST CASES ──
TEST_CASES = [
    {"input": [2, 3],   "expected": 5},
    {"input": [10, 20], "expected": 30},
    {"input": [7, 8],   "expected": 15},
]

PER_TC_LIMIT_S  = 2
MEMORY_LIMIT_MB = 256

# ── COOKING FUNCTIONS (defined below) ──
# _run_all_parallel()
# _run_all_sequential()
# _child_run()
# ... etc
```

For C/Java, the same idea applies but with different template files and different parallel execution mechanisms.

The Mega-Recipe is now a complete, self-contained program. It's roughly **50–200KB** for 1,000 test cases.

---

## Station 5 — The Order Is Placed on the Counter (Submit to Judge0)

The Mega-Recipe is now ready. The platform hands it to the **Judge0 Client** (`submit_and_wait()`), which packages it up and sends it to the Judge0 kitchen:

```python
# autograder.py line 134-140
judge0_result = self.judge0.submit_and_wait(
    source_code     = harness_code,      # the full Mega-Recipe text
    language        = "python",
    per_tc_limit_s  = 2,
    tc_count        = 3,
    memory_limit_mb = 256,
)
```

### Inside `submit_and_wait()` — Preparing the Delivery Package

```python
# judge0_client.py line 231
global_limit_s = per_tc_limit_s + overhead_s   # 2 + 5 = 7 seconds
```

This is the critical v3 fix: the global time limit is `per_tc + 5` seconds, **not** `per_tc × tc_count + 5`. All test cases run in parallel, so the slowest one takes at most 2 seconds. The 5-second overhead covers Python import time, fork() startup, and JVM warmup for Java.

The Mega-Recipe is encoded in **Base64** (like putting the recipe in a sealed envelope so it survives HTTP transport without character encoding issues):

```python
# judge0_client.py line 233-245
payload = {
    "source_code":     base64.b64encode(harness_code.encode()).decode(),
    "language_id":     71,             # Python = 71 in Judge0's list
    "cpu_time_limit":  7,              # 7 seconds total
    "wall_time_limit": 9,              # 7 + 2 extra for I/O
    "memory_limit":    256 * 1024,     # 256MB in KB
    "stdin":           "",
    "base64_encoded":  True,
    "enable_per_process_and_thread_time_limit":   True,
    "enable_per_process_and_thread_memory_limit": True,
}
```

If callback mode is on, the return address is attached:

```python
payload["callback_url"] = "http://host.docker.internal:9090/result"
```

The package is sent to Judge0's front door with a **120-second timeout** (because under heavy load, Puma's 80 connection threads might be busy and the request queues for up to 60+ seconds before being accepted):

```python
# judge0_client.py line 258-265
resp = requests.post(
    f"{JUDGE0_URL}/submissions?base64_encoded=true&wait=false",
    json=payload,
    headers=headers,
    timeout=120,         # wait up to 2 minutes for Judge0 to accept
)
token = resp.json()["token"]   # e.g. "7b3e9c2a-f841-..."
```

Judge0 responds immediately with a **tracking token** — like the receipt slip you get at a restaurant. The order is now in the kitchen's system. The cooking hasn't started yet.

---

## Station 6 — Inside Judge0: The Order Board, Workers, and Sandbox

This is what happens **inside** the Judge0 Docker containers after the token is issued.

### Step 6a — Saved to the Pantry Ledger (PostgreSQL)

Judge0's Rails server writes the submission to PostgreSQL:

```
Table: submissions
Row: {
    token: "7b3e9c2a-f841-...",
    source_code: "<base64 of Mega-Recipe>",
    language_id: 71,
    status_id: 1,      ← "In Queue"
    created_at: now()
}
```

Status 1 means "In Queue" — the order is received but not yet picked up.

### Step 6b — Posted to the Order Board (Redis Queue)

Judge0 pushes the token onto the Redis queue:

```
Redis List: "judge0:submissions:queue"
RPUSH → "7b3e9c2a-f841-..."
```

The Order Board now has this job. Workers are listening.

### Step 6c — A Worker Picks Up the Order

One of the Worker containers (running `./scripts/workers`) is sitting idle, watching the Redis queue. It pops the token:

```
BLPOP "judge0:submissions:queue"
→ "7b3e9c2a-f841-..."
```

The Worker fetches the full submission record from PostgreSQL, updates status to 2 ("Processing"), and prepares to cook.

### Step 6d — The Sandbox Opens (isolate)

Judge0 uses a Linux tool called **`isolate`** to create a completely sealed kitchen room for cooking. Isolate:

- Creates a **chroot jail** (the kitchen room has no access to the real filesystem)
- Sets **cgroup limits** (CPU time, wall time, memory — enforced at kernel level)
- Drops privileges (no root access inside the sandbox)
- Writes the Mega-Recipe source code into the sandbox as a file

On Mac with Docker Desktop (cgroup v2), the `cgroupv1-init` service has already mounted the v1 cgroup controllers that isolate needs.

The sandbox runs:

```bash
# Inside isolate sandbox
python3 /sandbox/solution.py
```

The Mega-Recipe starts executing.

---

## Station 7 — Inside the Sandbox: The Mega-Recipe Cooks All Dishes Simultaneously

This is the most detailed part. We're now inside the harness execution. Let's follow **Python** line by line.

### Step 7a — Security Monkey-Patching (Runtime Layer)

The very first thing the Mega-Recipe does, before any student code runs, is install **safe replacements** for dangerous kitchen tools:

```python
# python_harness.py — runs at module import time
_real_open   = open
_real_signal = signal.signal
_HARNESS_FILE = __file__

def _safe_open(file, mode='r', *args, **kwargs):
    if str(file) == _HARNESS_FILE:
        raise PermissionError("Harness file is protected")
    return _real_open(file, mode, *args, **kwargs)

builtins.open   = _safe_open    # ALL open() calls go through this now
signal.signal   = _safe_signal  # student can't install their own SIGALRM handler
sys.exit        = _safe_exit    # sys.exit() raises exception instead of killing process
```

This is the **runtime security layer** — even if the Health Inspector missed something, these replacements provide a second line of defense.

### Step 7b — Student Code Loaded Into Memory

For function mode, the student's `solve()` function is defined at module level:

```python
# (Pasted into harness at the student_code placeholder)
def solve(a, b):
    return a + b
```

This definition runs in the **parent process**. When children are forked later, they inherit this function via copy-on-write memory. No re-loading needed per child.

### Step 7c — Checking the License Count (`_can_fork_n`)

Before attempting to hire 3 junior chefs (one per test case), the head chef checks how many licenses are available:

```python
# python_harness.py — _can_fork_n()
def _can_fork_n(n: int) -> bool:
    soft, hard = resource.getrlimit(resource.RLIMIT_NPROC)
    try:
        current = len(os.listdir('/proc/self/task'))   # count current threads
    except OSError:
        current = 10
    available = soft - current - 2    # 2 = safety buffer
    return available >= n
```

If enough licenses exist → parallel cooking. If not → sequential fallback (F-03 disaster).

### Step 7d — The Fork Phase: Hiring All Junior Chefs at Once

Assuming licenses are available, the head chef opens one **pass-through hatch** (pipe) per test case and forks a child:

```python
# python_harness.py — _run_all_parallel()
pipe_read_fds  = []
pipe_write_fds = []
child_pids     = []

for i, tc in enumerate(TEST_CASES):
    r_fd, w_fd = os.pipe()         # open hatch: r_fd=receive side, w_fd=push side
    pid = os.fork()                # HIRE a junior chef

    if pid == 0:
        # ═══ JUNIOR CHEF'S CODE (runs in child process) ═══
        os.close(r_fd)             # child doesn't need the receive side
        _child_run(tc, w_fd, PER_TC_LIMIT_S, MEMORY_LIMIT_MB)
        os._exit(0)                # never returns

    else:
        # ═══ HEAD CHEF'S CODE (continues in parent) ═══
        os.close(w_fd)             # parent doesn't need the push side
        pipe_read_fds.append(r_fd)
        child_pids.append(pid)
```

After the fork loop, the timeline looks like this:

```
t=0ms:

HEAD CHEF (parent process):
  Holding 3 receive-side hatches: [fd_3, fd_5, fd_7]
  Waiting for results

JUNIOR CHEF 1 (child pid=1001):
  Has test case: inputs=[2,3], expected=5
  Has push-side hatch: fd_4
  Starting to cook Dish 1...

JUNIOR CHEF 2 (child pid=1002):
  Has test case: inputs=[10,20], expected=30
  Has push-side hatch: fd_6
  Starting to cook Dish 2...

JUNIOR CHEF 3 (child pid=1003):
  Has test case: inputs=[7,8], expected=15
  Has push-side hatch: fd_8
  Starting to cook Dish 3...

ALL THREE COOKING SIMULTANEOUSLY ← key advantage
```

### Step 7e — Inside Each Junior Chef (`_child_run`)

Each junior chef runs `_child_run()` which does three things before touching the student's function:

**1. Set the memory limit:**
```python
# resource.RLIMIT_AS = virtual address space limit
resource.setrlimit(resource.RLIMIT_AS, (mem_limit_bytes, mem_limit_bytes))
```
If the student's `solve()` tries to allocate more than 256MB, the OS kills the child with SIGKILL → parent sees signal → marks dish as MLE.

**2. Set the time limit (SIGALRM kitchen timer):**
```python
def _timeout_handler(signum, frame):
    raise TimeoutError("Time limit exceeded")

signal.alarm(PER_TC_LIMIT_S)    # set 2-second timer
# Note: _safe_signal() is installed, but alarm() itself is not blocked
# because alarm() is a system call, not a signal installation
```
If `solve()` takes longer than 2 seconds, SIGALRM fires → `_timeout_handler()` raises `TimeoutError` → child writes TLE result to hatch and exits.

**3. Call the student's function:**

For **function mode**:
```python
# python_harness.py — _child_run_function()
try:
    result = solve(*tc["input"])          # solve(2, 3) → 3

    # Float-tolerant comparison
    # "0.3" vs "0.30000000000000004" → both parse as floats → epsilon compare
    if _num_equal(str(result), str(tc["expected"])):
        status = "PASS"
        got = str(result)
    else:
        status = "FAIL"
        got = str(result)

except TimeoutError:
    status, got = "TLE", ""
except MemoryError:
    status, got = "MLE", ""
except Exception as e:
    status, got = "ERROR", str(e)
```

For **stdio mode**:
```python
# python_harness.py — _child_run_stdio()
# Replace stdin with fake input
sys.stdin = io.StringIO(tc["stdin_text"])
# Replace stdout to capture output
captured_stdout = io.StringIO()
sys.stdout = captured_stdout

# Run student's ENTIRE program in a fresh namespace
exec(compile(_STUDENT_SOURCE, "<student>", "exec"), {})

output = captured_stdout.getvalue().strip()
status = "PASS" if output == tc["expected"].strip() else "FAIL"
```

**4. Write result through the hatch:**
```python
result_json = json.dumps({
    "status":   status,          # "PASS"
    "got":      str(result),     # "5"
    "expected": str(tc["expected"]),  # "5"
    "detail":   ""
}).encode()

os.write(w_fd, result_json)    # push through the hatch
os.close(w_fd)                 # close the hatch (signals EOF to parent)
os._exit(0)                    # exit without running atexit handlers
```

Why `os._exit()` instead of `sys.exit()`? Because `sys.exit()` runs Python cleanup handlers that could flush buffers or run `__del__` methods — these could interfere with the parent process's output. `os._exit()` is immediate, clean, and brutal.

### Step 7f — The Head Chef Collects Results (`select()` / `poll()`)

While junior chefs are cooking, the head chef waits at all hatches simultaneously using `select()` (or `poll()` after the F-01 fix):

```python
# python_harness.py — _run_all_parallel(), collect phase
import select

deadline = time.monotonic() + PER_TC_LIMIT_S + 1.5   # 2s + 1.5s grace = 3.5s
buffers  = {fd: b"" for fd in pipe_read_fds}

while open_fds and time.monotonic() < deadline:
    # Wait for ANY hatch to have data (or timeout)
    readable, _, _ = select.select(open_fds, [], [], deadline - time.monotonic())

    for fd in readable:
        chunk = os.read(fd, 65536)
        if chunk:
            buffers[fd] += chunk     # accumulate data
        else:
            # Empty read = EOF = junior chef closed their hatch = done
            os.close(fd)
            open_fds.remove(fd)

            # Reap the child (clean up zombie process)
            pid = fd_to_pid[fd]
            _, status = os.waitpid(pid, 0)

            # Check how the child exited
            if os.WIFSIGNALED(status):
                sig = os.WTERMSIG(status)
                if   sig == signal.SIGSEGV: override_status = "SEGV"
                elif sig == signal.SIGFPE:  override_status = "FPE"
                elif sig == signal.SIGKILL: override_status = "MLE"
                # (SIGKILL = OOM killer fired, not our SIGALRM)
```

The `select()` call is **non-blocking** on fast dishes — if Junior Chef 1 finishes in 0.1 seconds, `select()` returns immediately and collects that result. No artificial delay. Junior Chefs 2 and 3 are still cooking in the background.

```
t=0ms:    All 3 junior chefs start cooking
t=80ms:   Junior Chef 1 finishes solve(2,3)=5 → writes to hatch
          select() notices hatch 1 is readable → head chef reads result
t=82ms:   Junior Chef 2 finishes solve(10,20)=30 → writes to hatch
          select() notices hatch 2 → head chef reads result
t=85ms:   Junior Chef 3 finishes solve(7,8)=15 → writes to hatch
          select() notices hatch 3 → head chef reads result
t=86ms:   All hatches closed, all children reaped
```

Total cooking time: **86ms**. Not 6 seconds (3 × 2s sequential). Parallelism wins.

### Step 7g — Kill Any Overrunning Junior Chefs

If any junior chef is still alive after the deadline, the head chef fires them:

```python
# python_harness.py — cleanup phase
for pid in still_alive_pids:
    os.kill(pid, signal.SIGKILL)   # immediate termination, no questions asked
    os.waitpid(pid, 0)             # reap zombie
    # Mark that dish as TLE
    results[fd_to_tc_idx[fd]] = {"status": "TLE", "detail": f"Exceeded {PER_TC_LIMIT_S}s"}
```

### Step 7h — Print Results with Secret Barcode

The head chef now prints all results to `stdout` **in the original test case order**, wrapped in the secret barcode:

```python
# python_harness.py — emit phase
for i, tc_result in enumerate(results, start=1):
    print(f"{DELIM}START_{i}")
    print(json.dumps(tc_result))
    print(f"{DELIM}END_{i}")

print(f"{DELIM}DONE")   # ← this marker is CRITICAL
```

The actual stdout output looks like this:

```
@@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__START_1
{"status": "PASS", "got": "5", "expected": "5", "detail": ""}
@@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__END_1
@@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__START_2
{"status": "PASS", "got": "30", "expected": "30", "detail": ""}
@@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__END_2
@@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__START_3
{"status": "PASS", "got": "15", "expected": "15", "detail": ""}
@@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__END_3
@@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__DONE
```

The **DONE marker** is everything. If it appears → cooking completed normally. If it's missing → the harness was killed mid-execution (OOM, Judge0 global TLE, worker crash) → `global_tle = True`.

---

## Station 8 — Judge0 Returns the Cooked Food to the Autograder

### Path A — Polling Mode (default)

The autograder keeps sending a waiter to check:

```python
# judge0_client.py — _poll()
for _ in range(60):    # max 60 checks
    resp = requests.get(f"{JUDGE0_URL}/submissions/{token}?base64_encoded=true")
    data = resp.json()
    status_id = data["status"]["id"]

    if status_id > 2:    # > 2 means finished (not "In Queue" or "Processing")
        return Judge0Result(
            stdout         = base64.b64decode(data["stdout"]).decode(),
            stderr         = base64.b64decode(data["stderr"]).decode(),
            status_str     = JUDGE0_STATUS[status_id],   # "Accepted"
            status_id      = status_id,
            compile_output = base64.b64decode(data["compile_output"] or "").decode(),
            time_taken_s   = float(data["time"]),        # e.g. 0.086
            memory_kb      = data["memory"],             # e.g. 9420
        )

    time.sleep(0.5)    # wait 500ms before checking again
```

**FIX-7**: The status is checked **before** sleeping. So a job that finishes in 80ms is collected at the first poll check (no forced 500ms wait).

### Path B — Callback Mode (production)

The Kitchen Bell rings. Judge0 fires a PUT request to the callback URL:

```
PUT http://host.docker.internal:9090/result
Body: {
    "token": "7b3e9c2a-f841-...",
    "status": {"id": 3, "description": "Accepted"},
    "stdout": "<base64 of harness output>",
    "time": "0.086",
    "memory": 9420
}
```

The `CallbackServer` HTTP handler receives this:

```python
# judge0_client.py — _Handler._handle()
payload = json.loads(body)
token   = payload["token"]
server_ref._deliver(token, payload)
```

`_deliver()` stores the payload and signals the waiting thread:

```python
# judge0_client.py — CallbackServer._deliver()
with self._lock:
    self._results[token] = payload     # store result
    evt = self._events.get(token)
if evt:
    evt.set()    # wake up the thread that's blocking in evt.wait()
```

The autograder's `_wait_callback()` was blocking on `evt.wait()`. It immediately wakes up and collects the result:

```python
# judge0_client.py — _wait_callback()
fired = evt.wait(timeout=global_limit_s + 30)   # blocks here until bell rings
raw   = self.callback_server.pop_result(token)
return self._parse_webhook_payload(raw)
```

**Race condition handled**: If Judge0 fires the callback **before** `register(token)` is called (can happen with fast compile errors), `_deliver()` stores the result in `_results`. When `register()` is eventually called, it checks `_results` and pre-sets the event immediately.

---

## Station 9 — The Result Ledger Reads the Barcoded Output

The `judge0_result.stdout` now contains the full harness output. The **OutputParser** (`parse_judge0_response()`) reads it:

### Step 9a — Judge0-level failure check first

```python
# output_parser.py — parse_judge0_response()
if judge0_status == "Time Limit Exceeded":
    # Judge0's global clock fired — harness never printed DONE
    # Mark ALL test cases as TLE
    return ParsedSubmission(tc_results=[
        TCResult(tc_num=i, status="TLE",
                 detail="Judge0 global time limit exceeded — infinite loop likely")
        for i in range(1, total_tc_count + 1)
    ])

if judge0_status in ("Compilation Error", "Internal Error"):
    # Code didn't even compile — mark all ERROR
    return ParsedSubmission(tc_results=[
        TCResult(tc_num=i, status="ERROR", detail=f"Judge0: {judge0_status}")
        for i in range(1, total_tc_count + 1)
    ])
```

### Step 9b — DONE marker check

```python
# output_parser.py — OutputParser.parse()
done_marker       = f"{self.delim}DONE"
result.global_tle = done_marker not in self.raw
```

If `global_tle = True`, test cases that are missing will be marked **TLE** (because time caused the kill). If `global_tle = False` but some TCs are missing, they're marked **MISSING** (because a crash stopped the harness).

### Step 9c — Extract each TC block using regex

```python
# output_parser.py line 58-69
pattern = re.compile(
    rf"{re.escape(self.delim)}START_(\d+)\n(.*?){re.escape(self.delim)}END_\1",
    re.DOTALL
)

for match in pattern.finditer(self.raw):
    tc_num  = int(match.group(1))       # 1, 2, 3...
    content = match.group(2).strip()    # the JSON block between START and END
    found_tcs.add(tc_num)

    tc_result = self._parse_tc_block(tc_num, content)
    result.tc_results.append(tc_result)
```

The regex anchors on `START_N ... END_N` pairs. Any garbage the student printed between blocks (like random `print()` calls) is **ignored** — only what appears between the barcoded delimiters counts.

### Step 9d — Parse each TC block

```python
# output_parser.py — _parse_tc_block()
try:
    data   = json.loads(content)
    status = data.get("status", "ERROR")

    return TCResult(
        tc_num   = tc_num,
        status   = status,       # "PASS", "FAIL", "TLE", "MLE", "SEGV", "FPE", "ERROR"
        got      = str(data.get("got",      "")),
        expected = str(data.get("expected", "")),
        detail   = str(data.get("detail",   "")),
        warning  = str(data.get("warning",  "")),
    )

except json.JSONDecodeError:
    # Student printed something between delimiters that broke JSON
    # Scan raw text for status keyword as fallback
    for s in VALID_STATUSES:
        if s in content:
            return TCResult(tc_num=tc_num, status=s, detail="Output parse error")
```

### Step 9e — Mark missing TCs

```python
# output_parser.py line 74-86
for i in range(1, self.total_tc_count + 1):
    if i not in found_tcs:
        status = "TLE"     if result.global_tle else "MISSING"
        detail = ("TC not reached — earlier TC caused global time limit exceeded"
                  if result.global_tle else
                  "TC not reached — likely crash or error in earlier TC")
        result.tc_results.append(TCResult(tc_num=i, status=status, detail=detail))
```

### Step 9f — Calculate score

```python
result.score             = sum(1 for r in result.tc_results if r.status == "PASS")
result.partial_execution = len(found_tcs) < self.total_tc_count
```

---

## The Final Handoff — GradingResult Back to the Platform

Back in `autograder.py`, the `grade()` function assembles the final report card:

```python
# autograder.py line 150-156
return GradingResult(
    student_id   = submission.student_id,    # "student_042"
    language     = submission.language,      # "python"
    submission   = parsed,                   # ParsedSubmission with all TCResults
    judge0_raw   = judge0_result,            # raw timing, memory, status
    harness_code = harness_code,             # the Mega-Recipe (for debugging)
)
```

The platform calls `result.summary()` to display to the student:

```
Student: student_042
Score:   3/3
Global TLE: False

  TC1: PASS
  TC2: PASS
  TC3: PASS

Judge0 time: 0.086s
Judge0 mem:  9420KB
```

---

## The Complete Flow in One Picture

```
👨‍🎓 Student clicks Submit
│
▼
📋 Submission object created (student_id, code, test_cases, limits)
│
▼
🏗️  HarnessBuilder.__init__()
│   → Generates session_id = "a3f9c1e82b40"
│   → Generates delim = "@@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__"
│
▼
🕵️  SecurityChecker.check()
│   → Checks delimiter injection
│   → Python: AST analysis (blocked modules, builtins, dunders)
│   → C/C++/Java: regex patterns
│   → FAIL → return GradingResult(security_error=...) immediately
│   → PASS → continue
│
▼
🧼  sanitize_for_injection()
│   → Python: strip trailing whitespace
│   → C/C++/Java: escape */ sequences
│
▼
📖  HarnessBuilder.build()
│   → Loads template (python_harness.py / c_harness.c / etc.)
│   → Fills placeholders: session_id, test_cases, limits, student_code
│   → Brace-escaping trick for student code
│   → Sentinel trick for _STUDENT_SOURCE
│   → Returns complete Mega-Recipe source code (~50–200KB)
│
▼
📮  Judge0Client.submit_and_wait()
│   → Calculates global_limit = per_tc_limit + 5
│   → Base64-encodes Mega-Recipe
│   → POST /submissions → gets token "7b3e9c2a-..."
│   │
│   ├── Polling mode: _poll()
│   │   → GET /submissions/{token} every 0.5s
│   │   → Wait for status_id > 2
│   │
│   └── Callback mode: _wait_callback()
│       → Register token → block on threading.Event
│       → CallbackServer receives PUT from Judge0
│       → Event fires → collect result
│
│   [INSIDE JUDGE0]
│   PostgreSQL: submission record written (status=1 In Queue)
│   Redis: token pushed to queue
│   Worker: pops token, fetches submission, status=2 Processing
│   isolate: creates sandbox, writes source to /sandbox/solution.py
│   Sandbox: python3 /sandbox/solution.py runs
│       │
│       ├── Security monkey-patching (open, signal, sys.exit replaced)
│       ├── Student code loaded into parent namespace
│       ├── _can_fork_n(N) → parallel or sequential decision
│       │
│       ├── FORK PHASE: N children spawned simultaneously
│       │   Each child:
│       │   → Sets RLIMIT_AS (memory limit)
│       │   → Sets SIGALRM (time limit)
│       │   → Calls solve(*inputs) or exec(student_code)
│       │   → Writes JSON result to pipe
│       │   → os._exit(0)
│       │
│       ├── COLLECT PHASE: select()/poll() watches all pipes
│       │   → Reads results as children finish
│       │   → Detects SIGSEGV/SIGFPE/SIGKILL from exit status
│       │   → Kills any overrunning children after deadline
│       │
│       └── EMIT PHASE: prints barcoded JSON blocks to stdout
│           @@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__START_1
│           {"status":"PASS","got":"5","expected":"5"}
│           @@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__END_1
│           ...
│           @@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__DONE
│
│   isolate: collects stdout, writes to PostgreSQL result record
│   Judge0: status=3 Accepted, fires callback (or waits for poll)
│
▼
📊  parse_judge0_response()
│   → Check Judge0-level status (TLE/CompileError → all TCs affected)
│   → OutputParser.parse():
│       → DONE marker present? → global_tle = True/False
│       → Regex extract TC blocks by barcode
│       → Parse each JSON block → TCResult
│       → Identify missing TCs → mark TLE or MISSING
│       → Sort by TC number
│       → Count PASS → score
│
▼
🏆  GradingResult returned to platform
    → student_id, language, submission (score, tc_results), judge0_raw
    → Platform writes to DB, sends to student via WebSocket/SSE
```

That is every step, every function, every decision point — from the moment the student clicks Submit to the moment their score appears on screen.

=== USER ===
Exact location: harnesses/python_harness.py _can_fork_n() and _run_all().

Why it fails: RLIMIT_NPROC is a per-UID limit on the total number of processes. It is not per-process — it counts all processes for that user system-wide. Judge0's isolate runs all workers under the same UID.

With MAX_RUNNERS=2 and 3 worker replicas (6 concurrent sandboxes), at peak:

6 harnesses × 1,000 fork()s = 6,000 child processes needed simultaneously
Linux default per-user process limit: typically 1,024–4,096 in a container
Python _can_fork_n(1000) checks available slots and falls back to sequential if insufficient



here the linux per container what does it typically mean explain it in detail.

=== ASSISTANT ===
Great question. "Per-user process limit in a container" is one of the most misunderstood things in Linux. Let me break it down completely.

---

# The Junior Chef License System — In Full Detail

---

## First: What Does "Per-User" Actually Mean in Linux?

Linux doesn't issue licenses per **room** (container). It issues licenses per **employee ID** (UID — User ID).

Every process on Linux is owned by a UID. `RLIMIT_NPROC` says:

> "This UID may own at most N processes simultaneously, anywhere on this machine."

The key word is **anywhere**. Not "in this container." Not "in this program." Anywhere the kernel can see that UID running.

```
UID 1000 process limit = 2,000

Process A (UID 1000) running in Container 1    → counts as 1
Process B (UID 1000) running in Container 2    → counts as 1
Process C (UID 1000) running in Container 3    → counts as 1

Total owned by UID 1000: 3
Remaining budget: 2,000 - 3 = 1,997
```

The containers don't get separate budgets. The **UID gets one budget**, shared across everything running under that UID.

---

## Where Does the Default Number Come From?

There is no single answer. The limit is set at **multiple layers**, and the most restrictive one wins. Let me show every layer from the hardware upward.

---

### Layer 1 — The Linux Kernel Itself (the Building's Fire Code)

The kernel has a global ceiling for how many processes can exist at all on the machine:

```bash
cat /proc/sys/kernel/threads-max
# e.g. 63796 on a 4GB machine
# formula: RAM / (8KB per thread stack) roughly
```

No single UID can ever exceed this. But this is usually a large number (tens of thousands) and almost never the bottleneck.

---

### Layer 2 — The Linux User Limits File (the Restaurant's HR Policy)

On a bare Linux machine, per-user limits are set in:

```bash
/etc/security/limits.conf
```

```
# Format: <domain>  <type>  <item>   <value>
*          soft     nproc   1024     ← all users, soft limit = 1,024
*          hard     nproc   4096     ← all users, hard limit = 4,096
root       soft     nproc   unlimited
```

**Soft limit**: The current enforced limit. A process CAN raise it, up to the hard limit.
**Hard limit**: The ceiling. Only root can raise this.

This file is read when a user **logs in** via PAM (login manager). The limits are inherited by all child processes of that login session.

On old Linux systems (pre-2010), `1024` was the common soft limit for non-root users. On modern systems, defaults are much higher. But many Docker base images (Ubuntu 18.04, Alpine, CentOS 7) still ship with `/etc/security/limits.conf` unchanged from the OS distribution defaults — which can be `1024`.

---

### Layer 3 — The Docker Daemon (the Restaurant Chain's Policy)

When Docker starts a container, it applies `ulimits` to the container's init process. This becomes the starting limit for everything inside that container.

**Default Docker ulimit for nproc**: Since Docker 1.10 (2016), Docker sets `nproc=unlimited` by default for containers. BUT — "unlimited" here means "inherit from the Docker daemon's own limits."

The Docker daemon itself is a process running on the host (or in a VM). Its own `RLIMIT_NPROC` is what gets inherited.

```bash
# Check what the Docker daemon itself is limited to
cat /proc/$(pgrep dockerd)/limits | grep processes
# Limit                     Soft Limit           Hard Limit           Units
# Max processes             unlimited            unlimited            processes
```

On native Linux Docker: usually unlimited → containers inherit unlimited → no issue.

**BUT** — Docker Desktop on Mac is different.

---

### Layer 4 — Docker Desktop Mac (the Franchise Running Inside a Food Truck)

Docker Desktop on Mac does **not** run Docker natively. It runs Docker inside a **Linux virtual machine** (Lima VM, formerly HyperKit). Every container runs inside this VM.

```
Your Mac
└── Lima VM (Linux kernel, ~4 cores, 8GB RAM)
    └── Docker daemon (running inside VM)
        ├── Container: judge0-server
        ├── Container: judge0-workers-1
        ├── Container: judge0-workers-2
        └── Container: judge0-workers-3
```

The Linux VM has its own kernel limits. The VM is a stripped-down Linux — it often has conservative defaults:

```bash
# Inside Docker Desktop's VM
cat /proc/sys/kernel/threads-max
# → 32768  (much lower than a dedicated server)

ulimit -u
# → 1024  (the VM's own per-user soft limit)
```

When your worker containers start inside this VM and run processes, they inherit the VM's limits — not Mac's limits. Mac's process system is irrelevant. The VM's kernel is what matters.

---

### Layer 5 — The Container's Own UID (the Employee Badge)

Inside a Judge0 worker container, the `./scripts/workers` process starts and eventually calls `isolate` (the sandbox program) to run student code.

`isolate` creates a **restricted user** to run each submission. On most Linux systems, it uses a UID from the range `60000–65535` (often `65534` = `nobody`, or a specific `isolate` user).

Let's say isolate uses UID `65000` for all sandboxes. Then:

```
Worker Container 1 → isolate → sandbox runs as UID 65000
Worker Container 2 → isolate → sandbox runs as UID 65000
Worker Container 3 → isolate → sandbox runs as UID 65000
```

All three sandboxes are owned by **the same UID** from the kernel's perspective.

Here's the critical question: are these UIDs in the **same user namespace**?

---

### User Namespaces — The Franchise Branches

Docker containers by default do **not** use user namespaces (unless you configure `userns-remap`). Without user namespaces:

```
Container 1 UID 65000 = Host VM UID 65000   ← SAME PERSON
Container 2 UID 65000 = Host VM UID 65000   ← SAME PERSON
Container 3 UID 65000 = Host VM UID 65000   ← SAME PERSON
```

The kernel sees them all as the same UID. Their process counts are pooled.

```
UID 65000 limit: 1,024 processes (from VM's /etc/security/limits.conf)

Container 1 sandbox forks 400 children → 400/1024 used
Container 2 sandbox forks 400 children → 800/1024 used
Container 3 sandbox tries to fork 400 children:
  fork() #1   → 801/1024 ✓
  fork() #2   → 802/1024 ✓
  ...
  fork() #224 → 1024/1024 ✓
  fork() #225 → EAGAIN ✗  ← "No more licenses"
```

With user namespaces enabled (each container gets its own UID 65000 that maps to a different host UID):

```
Container 1 UID 65000 = Host UID 100000   ← different people
Container 2 UID 65000 = Host UID 165000   ← different people
Container 3 UID 65000 = Host UID 230000   ← different people
```

Now each container gets its own separate budget. But user namespaces require explicit configuration and have performance overhead.

---

## The Exact Numbers: Where Does "1,024–4,096" Come From?

| Environment | Typical `RLIMIT_NPROC` | Why |
|---|---|---|
| Ubuntu 22.04 bare metal | `unlimited` (soft), `unlimited` (hard) | Modern distro defaults |
| CentOS 7 bare metal | `4096` (soft), `unlimited` (hard) | Old Red Hat convention |
| Alpine Linux | `unlimited` | Minimal base, no PAM |
| Docker Desktop Mac VM | `1024`–`4096` | Lima VM inherits stripped kernel defaults |
| AWS EC2 (Amazon Linux 2) | `4096` (soft) | AMI default |
| AWS EC2 (Ubuntu) | `unlimited` | Modern Ubuntu default |
| Judge0 inside Docker Desktop | `1024` (observed in practice) | VM's per-user default |

The "1,024–4,096" range in the failure analysis reflects **what Judge0 workers actually encounter on Docker Desktop Mac** — the environment described in your setup.

---

## Visualizing the Full Stack for Your Setup

```
YOUR MAC (macOS)
│
│  ← Mac process limits are irrelevant here
│
└── Lima VM (Linux 5.15 kernel, 10 cores, 16GB RAM)
    │
    │  kernel/threads-max = 32,768
    │  UID 65000 RLIMIT_NPROC soft = 1,024  ← THE BINDING LIMIT
    │  UID 65000 RLIMIT_NPROC hard = 4,096
    │
    ├── Docker daemon (UID 0)
    │
    ├── Container: workers-1
    │   └── isolate (UID 65000)
    │       └── python harness
    │           └── fork() × 1,000 children
    │               → 1,000 processes owned by UID 65000
    │
    ├── Container: workers-2
    │   └── isolate (UID 65000)   ← SAME UID, SAME BUDGET
    │       └── python harness
    │           └── fork() × 1,000 children
    │               → tries to add 1,000 more to UID 65000's count
    │               → but only 24 slots left after workers-1 used 1,000
    │               → _can_fork_n(1000) → False → SEQUENTIAL FALLBACK
    │
    └── Container: workers-3
        └── isolate (UID 65000)   ← SAME UID, SAME BUDGET
            └── _can_fork_n(1000) → 0 slots left → SEQUENTIAL FALLBACK
```

---

## How `_can_fork_n()` Detects This

The function reads the current process count by listing `/proc/self/task` (which shows all threads of the current process) and compares against the soft limit:

```python
def _can_fork_n(n: int) -> bool:
    soft, hard = resource.getrlimit(resource.RLIMIT_NPROC)
    # soft = 1,024  (what the kernel enforces right now)
    # hard = 4,096  (maximum we could raise soft to)

    try:
        current = len(os.listdir('/proc/self/task'))
        # /proc/self/task lists one entry per thread in this process
        # e.g. 3 threads → current = 3
    except OSError:
        current = 10   # safe fallback if /proc not readable

    available = soft - current - 2
    # soft=1024, current=3, buffer=2 → available = 1019

    return available >= n
    # n=1000: 1019 >= 1000 → True  (just barely passes for the first harness)
    # But: this only counts THIS process's threads, NOT other containers' processes
```

**This is the bug**: `_can_fork_n()` reads the soft limit correctly but counts only the current process's threads. It does **not** count other processes already owned by UID 65000 in other containers. So it thinks 1,019 slots are available, attempts to fork 1,000 children — and the first container succeeds. By the time the second container runs the same check, the kernel has already granted 1,000 licenses to the first container. The second container's `fork()` calls start failing around #25, causing a mid-fork collapse, not a clean sequential fallback.

The corrected version would need to count processes across the entire UID:

```python
def _can_fork_n(n: int) -> bool:
    soft, _ = resource.getrlimit(resource.RLIMIT_NPROC)

    # Count ALL processes owned by our UID across the whole system
    our_uid = os.getuid()
    try:
        uid_process_count = sum(
            1 for pid in os.listdir('/proc')
            if pid.isdigit() and _get_uid(pid) == our_uid
        )
    except OSError:
        uid_process_count = 10

    available = soft - uid_process_count - 2
    return available >= n

def _get_uid(pid: str) -> int:
    try:
        status = open(f'/proc/{pid}/status').read()
        for line in status.splitlines():
            if line.startswith('Uid:'):
                return int(line.split()[1])   # real UID
    except (OSError, ValueError):
        pass
    return -1
```

This is slower (scans all /proc entries) but accurate. In practice, the **docker-compose ulimit fix** (`nproc: 65536`) is the right solution — it raises the ceiling high enough that the check never hits the limit in the first place, and each container effectively gets enough headroom even when sharing the UID budget.

---

## Summary in One Table

```
Question                              Answer
─────────────────────────────────── ─────────────────────────────────────────
What does "per-user" mean?          Per UID — one counter for ALL processes
                                    owned by that UID, anywhere on the machine

What is "the machine" in Docker?    The Linux VM (Docker Desktop) or the
                                    Linux host (native Docker)

Do containers get separate budgets? Only if user namespaces are enabled.
                                    By default: NO. Same UID = shared budget.

Where does 1,024 come from?         Lima VM's /etc/security/limits.conf
                                    soft limit for non-root UIDs

Where does 4,096 come from?         The hard limit — you can raise soft
                                    up to this value without root

What does ulimits: nproc: 65536 do? Raises the soft AND hard limit for that
                                    container's init process, which all child
                                    processes inherit. But it only helps if
                                    the UID isn't shared across containers.

What is the real fix?               ulimits: nproc: 65536 in docker-compose
                                    + userns-remap OR per-submission UID
                                    + MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER=1100 in
                                    judge0.conf to cap per-sandbox usage
```

here is want one confirmation uid means is it student id?

=== ASSISTANT ===
No. UID and student ID are completely different things.

---

**UID = User ID**

It is a number that Linux uses to identify **who owns a process**. It has nothing to do with your application or your students. It is a Linux operating system concept.

```
UID 0     = root (the superuser)
UID 1000  = a regular Linux user (e.g. "ubuntu" or "john")
UID 65000 = the restricted user that isolate creates to run sandboxes
```

When Linux asks "how many processes does this user own?", it means **this UID number** — not a student, not a database record, not an application user.

---

**Student ID = your application's concept**

That is what you store in your database. `student_042`, `student_999`, etc. Linux has no idea this exists.

---

**The relationship in your system**

```
Student 001 submits code
Student 002 submits code        ← all three students' code
Student 003 submits code           runs as UID 65000 inside isolate

Linux sees:
  UID 65000 → 3,000 processes   ← Linux only sees this number
                                   it has no concept of "student"
```

All student submissions run under the **same UID** inside the sandbox. Linux does not create a new UID per student. One UID, all students, one shared process budget.

That is exactly why the limit is dangerous — 1,000 students competing for one UID's process quota.

# Production Audit: AWS Ubuntu Server for Judge0 at Scale

---

## PART 1 — Platform Audit: What Automatically Changes on AWS Ubuntu

The first thing to establish is what you **get for free** by moving from Docker Desktop Mac to AWS Ubuntu, and what stays broken regardless.

---

### 1.1 — The cgroup Situation

Your current `docker-compose.yml` has this entire service:

```yaml
cgroupv1-init:
  image: alpine
  privileged: true
  command: >-
    sh -c "for ctrl in memory cpuacct pids cpu; do
      mount -t cgroup -o $$ctrl cgroup /sys/fs/cgroup/$$ctrl
    done"
```

This exists **only** because Docker Desktop Mac uses cgroup v2 exclusively. The isolate sandbox needs cgroup v1 controllers.

On AWS Ubuntu:

| Ubuntu Version | cgroup Default | cgroupv1-init needed? |
|---|---|---|
| Ubuntu 20.04 LTS | cgroup **v1** native | **NO — remove it** |
| Ubuntu 22.04 LTS | cgroup **v2** (unified) | **YES — keep it** |
| Ubuntu 22.04 + kernel flag | cgroup **v1** forced | NO |

**Recommendation**: Use **Ubuntu 20.04 LTS** for Judge0 production. It has native cgroup v1 support. The `cgroupv1-init` service can be removed entirely, which eliminates one `privileged: true` container and one point of failure.

If you want Ubuntu 22.04, you must either keep `cgroupv1-init` OR boot with:
```
# /etc/default/grub
GRUB_CMDLINE_LINUX="systemd.unified_cgroup_hierarchy=0"
```

---

### 1.2 — The VM Layer Disappears

| Aspect | Docker Desktop Mac | AWS Ubuntu |
|---|---|---|
| Docker runs inside | Lima VM (Linux) | Directly on host kernel |
| Kernel version | VM's kernel (~5.15) | Host kernel (5.15 on 22.04, 5.4 on 20.04) |
| `host.docker.internal` | Resolves to Mac host | Does NOT exist by default |
| CPU cores visible | Limited by VM config | All EC2 vCPUs available |
| RAM available | Limited by VM config | Full instance RAM |
| RLIMIT_NPROC source | VM's `/etc/security/limits.conf` | Host's `/etc/security/limits.conf` |

The `host.docker.internal` disappearance matters for your callback server (F-09). On AWS, you'd use the **EC2 private IP** or the container network gateway (`172.17.0.1`) instead.

---

### 1.3 — RLIMIT_NPROC Defaults: Mac VM vs AWS Ubuntu

| Environment | Soft nproc for UID 65000 | Hard nproc |
|---|---|---|
| Docker Desktop Mac Lima VM | ~1,024 (old Alpine defaults) | ~4,096 |
| AWS Ubuntu 20.04 fresh install | **unlimited** | **unlimited** |
| AWS Ubuntu 22.04 fresh install | **unlimited** | **unlimited** |

Ubuntu 20.04 and 22.04 ship with an empty `/etc/security/limits.conf` for nproc. The default is `unlimited`. This means **F-03 is significantly less severe on AWS Ubuntu out of the box** — but still needs explicit `ulimits` in `docker-compose.yml` because "unlimited" inherited by a Docker container still gets capped by Docker's own defaults and by what isolate sets internally.

---

## PART 2 — Per-Failure Audit on AWS Ubuntu

---

### F-01 — `select()` → `poll()` Audit

**Status: STILL BROKEN. Platform makes no difference.**

`FD_SETSIZE = 1024` is a compile-time constant defined in the Linux kernel headers:

```c
// /usr/include/x86_64-linux-gnu/bits/typesizes.h
#define __FD_SETSIZE    1024
```

This is the same value on every x86_64 Linux machine — AWS, Mac, bare metal. The bug is in **your harness code**, not the platform.

Current broken code location in `harness_builder.py` line 282–304:

```python
# _build_c_parallel_runner() — currently generates this C code:
fd_set _rfds;
FD_ZERO(&_rfds);
for (int _i = 0; _i < {n}; _i++) {
    FD_SET(_fds[_i], &_rfds);   # ← SILENT CORRUPTION above FD 1023
    if (_fds[_i] > _maxfd) _maxfd = _fds[_i];
}
int _sel = select(_maxfd + 1, &_rfds, NULL, NULL, &_tv);
```

With 1,000 test cases on AWS: same crash. Same silent result corruption.

**Needs**: Code change in `harness_builder.py` `_build_c_parallel_runner()` and `_build_cpp_parallel_runner()`.

---

### F-02 — `SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT` Audit

**Status: STILL MISSING. Not in `judge0.conf`.**

Current `judge0.conf` has 10 lines. `SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT` is absent. Judge0 1.13.0's default for this value is **64KB**.

A harness with 1,000 test cases at even modest input sizes:

```
Baseline Python template:           ~8KB
+ 1,000 TCs × 50 bytes avg input:  +50KB
+ JSON encoding overhead:           +5KB
= ~63KB  ←  borderline at default 64KB limit

With real-world inputs (200 bytes avg):
+ 1,000 TCs × 200 bytes:           +200KB
= ~213KB  ←  HARD FAILURE on AWS same as Mac
```

**Additionally**: Judge0 1.13.0 has a second limit — the nginx `client_max_body_size` in front of Puma. Default nginx `client_max_body_size = 1MB`. Base64 encoding inflates size by ~33%, so a 200KB harness becomes ~267KB — still under 1MB, but large inputs can push past it.

**Needs**: Two additions to `judge0.conf`:
```
SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT=524288    # 512KB
```
And nginx config (inside the Judge0 image) may also need patching for very large submissions.

---

### F-03 — RLIMIT_NPROC Audit on AWS Ubuntu

**Status: BETTER on AWS Ubuntu, but still needs explicit configuration.**

Three things to audit here:

**Finding 3a — Ubuntu host defaults are permissive:**
```bash
# On AWS Ubuntu 20.04 EC2
cat /etc/security/limits.conf | grep nproc
# → (empty — no nproc limit set)

ulimit -u
# → unlimited
```

This is good. The host has no artificial nproc restriction.

**Finding 3b — Docker container still inherits limits:**

When Docker starts a container, it reads the daemon's own `ulimits`. Docker daemon on Ubuntu typically runs as root with unlimited nproc. But the **containerized process** (Judge0 workers) runs as a non-root user inside the container. Without explicit `ulimits:` in `docker-compose.yml`, the limit depends on what the Judge0 image sets in its own `/etc/security/limits.conf` — which is controlled by the image maintainer, not you.

**Finding 3c — isolate sets its own RLIMIT_NPROC per sandbox:**

isolate explicitly sets `RLIMIT_NPROC` on the sandboxed process to prevent student code from fork-bombing. The Judge0 config value `MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER` controls this:

```
# judge0.conf — CURRENTLY MISSING
MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER=???   # not set → uses isolate's hardcoded default
```

isolate's hardcoded default for `MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER` in version 1.13.0 is **64 processes**. This means each sandbox can only have 64 concurrent child processes — not 1,000, not 250.

This is the **binding limit** that `_can_fork_n(1000)` would hit immediately, triggering sequential fallback, regardless of what Docker or Ubuntu sets.

**The three limits in play simultaneously:**

```
Limit 1 — Ubuntu host:        unlimited          (not the problem)
Limit 2 — Docker container:   inherited/explicit  (need ulimits: nproc: 65536)
Limit 3 — isolate per-sandbox: 64 default         (need MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER=1100)

The MINIMUM of all three wins.
Currently: min(unlimited, inherited, 64) = 64
```

**Needs**: Both `ulimits: nproc: 65536` in `docker-compose.yml` AND `MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER=1100` in `judge0.conf`.

---

### F-04 — 50 Worker Replicas on AWS: Feasibility Audit

**This is the most complex finding. Three separate constraints determine feasibility.**

---

#### Constraint 1 — CPU

Each worker replica runs `MAX_RUNNERS=2` concurrent sandboxes. Each sandbox runs student code.

Conservative rule (from current docs): `replicas = available_cores - 2`

```
For 50 replicas: need 52 vCPUs minimum
Realistic with overhead: 60+ vCPUs recommended
```

#### Constraint 2 — Memory

With `MAX_PARALLEL_TCS=250` (F-19 fix):

```
Per child process (Python, simple function):
  Shared pages (Python interpreter, copy-on-write): ~12MB
  Unique dirty pages (student's allocations):       ~2MB
  Effective cost per child:                         ~2MB (shared is amortized)

Per sandbox (250 children):
  12MB shared + 250 × 2MB unique = ~512MB per sandbox

Per worker replica (2 sandboxes):
  2 × 512MB = ~1GB per worker replica

50 workers × 1GB = ~50GB for children
+ Judge0 server (Rails 4 processes):  ~2GB
+ PostgreSQL:                         ~4GB
+ Redis:                              ~1GB
+ OS overhead:                        ~4GB
Total:                                ~61GB
```

Without F-19 (1,000 parallel TCs per sandbox):
```
Per sandbox: 12MB + 1,000 × 2MB = ~2GB
50 workers × 2 × 2GB = ~200GB — needs a very large instance
```

F-19 reduces memory requirement from ~200GB to ~61GB — **a 3× difference in instance sizing**.

#### Constraint 3 — Process count (RLIMIT_NPROC)

```
50 workers × 2 sandboxes × 250 children = 25,000 processes peak
All potentially under the same UID

Need: ulimits nproc ≥ 25,000 + overhead
Set:  ulimits nproc = 65,536  (covers this with headroom)
```

---

#### Instance Sizing Matrix for 50 Workers

| Instance | vCPU | RAM | Price/hr | Price/month | 50 Workers? | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| `c5.9xlarge` | 36 | 72GB | $1.53 | $1,100 | ❌ | CPU insufficient |
| `c5.18xlarge` | 72 | 144GB | $3.06 | $2,200 | ⚠️ | 35 workers max (CPU rule) |
| `c5.24xlarge` | 96 | 192GB | $4.08 | $2,940 | ⚠️ | 47 workers (just under 50) |
| `c5.metal` | 96 | 192GB | $4.08 | $2,940 | ⚠️ | 47 workers, bare metal better for isolate |
| `m5.24xlarge` | 96 | 384GB | $4.61 | $3,320 | ⚠️ | 47 workers, extra RAM headroom |
| `c5a.24xlarge` | 96 | 192GB | $3.67 | $2,640 | ⚠️ | AMD, slightly cheaper |

**Finding**: On a **single EC2 instance**, 50 worker replicas is not achievable within standard instance types due to the `replicas = cores - 2` / 2 formula. Maximum on `c5.24xlarge` is **47 workers** (94 usable vCPUs / 2 MAX_RUNNERS = 47).

However — with a 2× CPU oversubscription approach (acceptable for bursty student code that isn't 100% CPU-bound for the full duration):

```
c5.18xlarge (72 vCPU):
  Rule: replicas = vCPUs (1 vCPU per worker, not per sandbox)
  72 - 2 overhead = 70 workers possible
  70 × 2 sandboxes = 140 concurrent sandboxes
  BUT: 140 sandboxes × 250 children = 35,000 processes → CPU thrash risk
```

**The real answer: separate server and worker nodes.**

---

#### Recommended Production Architecture: 2-Tier

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────┐
│  SERVER NODE (1 instance)           │
│  Instance: c5.2xlarge               │
│  vCPU: 8    RAM: 16GB               │
│  Runs: Judge0 API + Redis + Postgres│
│  Cost: $0.34/hr                     │
└─────────────────┬───────────────────┘
                  │ Private VPC
    ┌─────────────┼──────────────┐
    ▼             ▼              ▼
┌─────────┐  ┌─────────┐  ┌─────────┐   ... × N
│WORKER-1 │  │WORKER-2 │  │WORKER-3 │
│c5.4xl   │  │c5.4xl   │  │c5.4xl   │
│16 vCPU  │  │16 vCPU  │  │16 vCPU  │
│32GB RAM │  │32GB RAM │  │32GB RAM │
│6 replicas│  │6 replicas│  │6 replicas│
│$0.68/hr │  │$0.68/hr │  │$0.68/hr │
└─────────┘  └─────────┘  └─────────┘
```

```
For 50 workers (replicas):
  ceil(50 / 6) = 9 worker nodes × c5.4xlarge

Cost:
  1 server  × $0.34/hr  =  $0.34/hr
  9 workers × $0.68/hr  =  $6.12/hr
  Total:                    $6.46/hr  =  ~$4,650/month on-demand
                                      =  ~$2,800/month reserved (1yr)
```

**BUT**: Multi-node requires **Docker Swarm or ECS** — not plain `docker-compose up --scale`. This is a significant architecture change.

---

#### Simplest Path to ~50 Workers (Single Node, No Architecture Change)

If you want 50 workers with minimal architecture change:

```
Instance: c5.metal (96 vCPU, 192GB RAM, $4.08/hr)
Bare metal preferred because:
  → isolate uses Linux namespaces and cgroups more reliably on bare metal
  → No hypervisor overhead for system calls (fork, clone, etc.)
  → Better cgroup isolation for sandboxes

Worker replicas: 47 (96 - 2 = 94 vCPUs / 2 = 47)
This is close enough to "50" for practical purposes.

With F-19 (MAX_PARALLEL_TCS=250):
  Memory: 47 × 2 × 512MB = ~48GB used by children
  + services ~11GB
  Total: ~59GB ← fits in 192GB with massive headroom ✓

PostgreSQL max_connections needed:
  (8 × 25 Puma threads) + (47 workers × 5) + 20 headroom = 455
  → Need max_connections=500
```

---

### F-19 — MAX_PARALLEL_TCS=250 Audit

**Status: NOT IMPLEMENTED. Code change needed.**

Currently `harness_builder.py` has no concept of `MAX_PARALLEL_TCS`. The builder blindly forks as many children as there are test cases:

```python
# harness_builder.py _build_c_parallel_runner() line 255
for i, tc in enumerate(self.cfg.test_cases):  # iterates ALL test cases
    # forks one child per TC — no cap
```

Same in `_build_python()` → template → `_run_all_parallel()`.

Without this cap:
- 1,000 TCs = 1,000 forks = 2,000 FDs = FD exhaustion risk (F-01 and F-05 combined)
- 1,000 forks × 47 workers × 2 runners = 94,000 concurrent processes

With `MAX_PARALLEL_TCS=250`:
- 4 rounds of 250 forks = FD peak is 500 (safely below FD_SETSIZE even with current select())
- 250 × 47 × 2 = 23,500 concurrent processes — manageable

**Impact on throughput** (with 250 batching):
```
Old (1,000 parallel):  1 round  × 7s  = 7s per submission
New (250 batches):     4 rounds × 7s  = 28s per submission

With 47 workers × 2 runners = 94 sandboxes:
  Throughput: 94 / 28s = 3.36 submissions/second
  1,000 submissions drain time: 1,000 / 3.36 = ~298 seconds = ~5 minutes
```

5 minutes for 1,000 concurrent submissions is acceptable for an exam context.

---

**Recommended instance for single-node 50-worker deployment**: `c5.metal` (96 vCPU, 192GB, $4.08/hr) running Ubuntu 20.04 LTS with 47 worker replicas.

**Recommended instance for multi-node**: 1× `c5.2xlarge` server + 9× `c5.4xlarge` workers (~$6.46/hr total, 54 worker replicas, proper production isolation).

## PART 2 — The Architecture Change: What You Are Actually Taking On

Moving from `docker-compose up --scale workers=9` on one machine to a multi-node setup is not just a config change. It is a new set of operational problems. Here is every scenario you will encounter.

---

### Scenario 1 — How Do Workers on 9 Nodes Find Redis?

**The Problem**

With single-node docker-compose, all containers share one Docker network. The worker container reaches Redis by just typing `redis` as the hostname. Docker's internal DNS resolves it.

```
Worker container → redis:6379 → Docker DNS → Redis container on same host ✓
```

On multi-node, worker node 7 has no Redis container. It needs to reach Redis on the **server node** — a completely different EC2 machine.

```
Worker node 7 → redis:6379 → ??? → Docker DNS only knows this node → FAILS ✗
```

**What you must handle**

You must either:

- **Option A**: Replace `REDIS_HOST=redis` in `judge0.conf` with the server node's **private IP**:
  ```
  REDIS_HOST=10.0.1.45    # server node's EC2 private IP
  ```
  This works but breaks if the server node is replaced (IP changes).

- **Option B**: Use an AWS internal DNS hostname:
  ```
  REDIS_HOST=server.judge0.internal   # Route 53 private hosted zone
  ```
  Better — survives IP changes.

- **Option C (ECS)**: ECS Service Discovery automatically creates DNS entries for each service. Workers find Redis via `redis.judge0.local` automatically.

- **Option D (Swarm)**: Overlay network with Swarm DNS. Services find each other by service name across nodes — same as docker-compose, except now it works across machines.

---

### Scenario 2 — PostgreSQL and Redis Must Stay on the Server Node

**The Problem**

PostgreSQL stores all submission records on disk. Redis stores the job queue in memory (with AOF to disk). These are **stateful** — their data must survive container restarts.

With single-node docker-compose, the `postgres-data` and `redis-data` volumes are on that one machine's disk. Simple.

With multi-node, you must prevent PostgreSQL and Redis from ever being scheduled on a worker node. If they move to a different machine, their data stays behind on the old machine's disk.

**What you must handle**

You must add **placement constraints** — rules that pin stateful services to the server node:

```yaml
# Docker Swarm syntax
services:
  db:
    deploy:
      placement:
        constraints:
          - node.hostname == judge0-server   # ONLY run on this machine

  redis:
    deploy:
      placement:
        constraints:
          - node.hostname == judge0-server   # ONLY run on this machine

  workers:
    deploy:
      placement:
        constraints:
          - node.hostname != judge0-server   # NEVER run on server machine
```

Without this, Swarm might schedule Redis on worker node 3 one day and worker node 7 the next. Your queue would fragment across two Redis instances with no data sharing.

---

### Scenario 3 — judge0.conf Must Reach Every Node

**The Problem**

Currently `judge0.conf` is a file sitting in `/Users/akhilesh/Edwisely/judge0_3/`. Your `docker-compose.yml` mounts it:

```yaml
volumes:
  - ./judge0.conf:/judge0.conf:ro
```

On a single machine, this works because the file exists locally.

On 9 worker nodes, each machine needs the same file. If you change `MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER=1100` in `judge0.conf`, you must update it on all 9 worker nodes and 1 server node — 10 machines.

**What you must handle**

Three options:

- **Docker Swarm Configs**: Swarm has a built-in config distribution mechanism. You store `judge0.conf` once in Swarm, and it is automatically distributed to every node that needs it:
  ```bash
  docker config create judge0_conf ./judge0.conf
  # All workers receive it automatically
  ```

- **AWS Systems Manager Parameter Store**: Store `judge0.conf` contents as a parameter. A startup script on each EC2 pulls the latest config on boot. One update propagates to all nodes.

- **S3 + User Data script**: Each EC2 instance downloads `judge0.conf` from S3 on startup. Update the S3 file, roll new instances.

---

### Scenario 4 — Deploying a New Judge0 Image Across 10 Machines

**The Problem**

On single-node: `docker-compose pull && docker-compose up -d` — done.

On 10 nodes: you need to update the image on every machine. If you SSH into each machine manually, that is 10 SSH sessions, 10 pull commands, 10 restart commands. Inevitable human error.

**What you must handle**

Both Swarm and ECS handle rolling updates:

```bash
# Docker Swarm — update image on all nodes with zero downtime
docker service update --image judge0/judge0:1.14.0 judge0_workers
# Swarm takes down 1 worker at a time, pulls new image, restarts, waits for health check, then moves to next
```

```bash
# ECS — update task definition, ECS does the rest
aws ecs update-service --cluster judge0 --service workers --force-new-deployment
```

Without orchestration, you are doing this manually. At 50 workers across 9 nodes, that is 50 containers to update.

---

### Scenario 5 — A Worker Node Dies

**The Problem**

Worker node 4 (c5.4xlarge) crashes — hardware failure, AWS spot interruption, kernel panic. It was running 6 worker replicas, actively processing 12 submissions.

**What you must handle**

Three separate things happen:

**1. In-flight submissions are lost**

The 12 submissions being processed on node 4 had their Judge0 status set to "Processing" in PostgreSQL. The processing never completes. Your autograder is polling or waiting for a callback that will never arrive. After 30 seconds it raises `TimeoutError`.

With Docker Swarm: Swarm detects the node is gone and reschedules the 6 worker replicas on surviving nodes. But the 12 **in-flight** submissions are not retried automatically — they are orphaned in PostgreSQL as "Processing" forever. You need the Dead Letter Queue logic (F-11) to detect and retry these.

**2. The other 6 workers on surviving nodes continue normally**

Jobs already queued in Redis that have NOT been picked up yet are safe — Redis is on the server node, still running. New workers (rescheduled on surviving nodes) pick them up.

**3. You temporarily have 44 workers instead of 54**

Until Swarm reschedules the 6 replicas onto other nodes, throughput drops. With auto-scaling (ECS), a replacement node can be launched automatically.

---

### Scenario 6 — The Callback Server Across 9 Worker Nodes

**The Problem**

Your autograder's `CallbackServer` listens on a port for Judge0 webhooks. With single-node, Judge0 workers and the autograder are on the same network — the callback URL is simple.

With 9 worker nodes, Judge0 workers on node 7 fire a webhook to the callback URL. That URL must be reachable from all 9 nodes. If your autograder platform runs in a separate service:

```
Worker node 7 fires: PUT http://autograder-service:9090/result
Where is autograder-service? Not on worker node 7.
```

**What you must handle**

The callback URL must be a **stable, publicly or internally routable address** — either:
- Your platform server's internal load balancer
- An internal ALB endpoint
- A service discovery DNS name

And your CallbackServer must be able to handle 54 workers all firing webhooks simultaneously — the F-09 issue (1,000 threads) becomes critical here.

---

### Scenario 7 — Scaling Workers Up and Down During the Exam

**The Problem**

Your exam starts at 10:00 AM. 1,000 students submit simultaneously. You need 54 workers. The exam ends at 12:00 PM. Now you need 0 workers (or just a few) to save money.

With single-node docker-compose:
```bash
docker compose up --scale workers=54 -d    # scale up
docker compose up --scale workers=2  -d    # scale down
```

With multi-node, scaling means **adding and removing EC2 instances**, not just containers.

**What you must handle**

Two approaches:

**Docker Swarm approach** — manual EC2 management:
```bash
# Before exam: launch 9 worker EC2 instances, join them to Swarm
aws ec2 run-instances --count 9 --instance-type c5.4xlarge ...
# After exam: drain and terminate
docker node update --availability drain judge0-worker-7
aws ec2 terminate-instances --instance-ids i-xxx
```

This is 9 `run-instances` commands + 9 `swarm join` commands + 9 `terminate-instances` later. Scriptable but not automatic.

**ECS approach** — automatic:
```
CloudWatch Alarm: Redis queue depth > 100
→ ECS Auto Scaling: add 2 worker tasks
→ CloudWatch Alarm: Redis queue depth < 10
→ ECS Auto Scaling: remove worker tasks down to minimum
```

ECS can scale from 6 workers at idle to 54 at peak **automatically** based on queue depth. No human intervention. This is the biggest operational advantage of ECS over Swarm.

---

### Scenario 8 — Logs Are Now Spread Across 10 Machines

**The Problem**

On single-node: `docker logs judge0_workers_1` — one command, one machine.

On 10 nodes: an error occurs on worker node 6. Which container? Which log? SSH to node 6, find the container ID, run `docker logs`. If the container restarted, the logs are gone.

**What you must handle**

Centralized logging:

- **ECS + CloudWatch**: automatic. Every container's stdout goes to CloudWatch Logs. You query across all 54 workers in one place.
- **Swarm + ELK or Loki**: manual setup of a logging driver. Add to each service:
  ```yaml
  logging:
    driver: awslogs
    options:
      awslogs-group: /judge0/workers
      awslogs-region: us-east-1
  ```

Without centralized logging, debugging production issues across 9 nodes is effectively impossible.

---

### Scenario 9 — Security Groups and Network Isolation

**The Problem**

On single-node, all containers share one host. Network security is simple — only expose port 2358 externally.

On 10 nodes, you have 10 EC2 instances in a VPC. You must control which instances can talk to which.

**What you must handle**

```
Security Group: judge0-server
  Inbound:
    Port 2358 (Judge0 API) → from ALB security group only
    Port 5432 (PostgreSQL) → from judge0-workers security group only
    Port 6379 (Redis)      → from judge0-workers security group only
    Port 22   (SSH)        → from your IP only
  Outbound: all

Security Group: judge0-workers
  Inbound:
    Port 9090 (callback)   → from judge0-server only (for webhook)
    Port 22   (SSH)        → from your IP only
  Outbound:
    Port 5432 → to judge0-server
    Port 6379 → to judge0-server
    Port 2358 → to judge0-server (for job polling, if not callback mode)
```

Without proper security groups, your PostgreSQL and Redis are potentially reachable from anywhere inside the VPC — or worse, from the internet if misconfigured.

---

## PART 3 — Docker Swarm vs ECS: The Decision

```
╔══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╗
║  COMPARISON: Docker Swarm vs ECS                                        ║
╠══════════════════════════╦═══════════════════════╦══════════════════════╣
║  Aspect                  ║  Docker Swarm          ║  ECS                 ║
╠══════════════════════════╬═══════════════════════╬══════════════════════╣
║  Setup complexity        ║  Low                   ║  High                ║
║  Syntax                  ║  docker-compose v3     ║  Task definitions    ║
║  Migration from compose  ║  Minimal changes       ║  Full rewrite        ║
║  Auto-scaling workers    ║  Manual / scripted     ║  Built-in (CW alarm) ║
║  Service discovery       ║  Built-in overlay DNS  ║  Built-in (Cloud Map)║
║  Rolling updates         ║  Built-in              ║  Built-in            ║
║  Node failure recovery   ║  Automatic             ║  Automatic           ║
║  Centralized logs        ║  Manual setup          ║  CloudWatch auto     ║
║  Cost overhead           ║  $0 (same EC2)         ║  +ECS control plane  ║
║  AWS managed service     ║  No (you manage Swarm) ║  Yes                 ║
║  Spot instance support   ║  Manual                ║  Capacity providers  ║
║  Learning curve          ║  Low (know compose?)   ║  High                ║
╚══════════════════════════╩═══════════════════════╩══════════════════════╝
```

---

## PART 4 — Recommendation

Given your current setup (docker-compose, Judge0 1.13.0, team likely familiar with compose syntax):

**Start with Docker Swarm.**

The migration from `docker-compose.yml` to a Swarm stack file is minimal — mostly adding `deploy:` blocks. You get multi-node orchestration, rolling updates, and service discovery without rewriting your entire infrastructure.

```
Current docker-compose.yml → add deploy: sections → docker stack deploy
                                                      works across 10 nodes
```

ECS makes sense when:
- You want queue-depth-based auto-scaling (critical for handling 1,000 submissions that arrive unpredictably)
- You want fully managed infrastructure (no SSH, no node maintenance)
- You have AWS expertise on the team

**The honest summary of what you are taking on:**

```
Docker Swarm adds:
  9 new scenarios to manage (nodes 1-9)
  2 new config concerns (placement constraints, Swarm configs)
  1 new deployment tool (docker stack deploy)
  Centralized logging setup
  Security group design

ECS adds:
  Everything in Swarm PLUS:
  Task definition JSON files (replaces docker-compose)
  IAM roles for containers
  ALB configuration
  CloudWatch alarms for auto-scaling
  VPC subnet design (public/private)
  Potentially RDS + ElastiCache instead of self-managed DB
```

If this is a new system being built for the first time: **ECS from the start** saves pain later.

If this is an existing docker-compose system being scaled up: **Docker Swarm first**, migrate to ECS when you need auto-scaling.

Shall I proceed with the production configuration for the simpler path — the single `c5.metal` node (47 workers, no multi-node complexity) — or the Docker Swarm 2-tier setup?

=== USER ===
give me the costs for the c5.xlarge instances.

=== ASSISTANT ===

## Workers Per c5.xlarge

```
c5.xlarge = 4 vCPU, 8GB RAM

Worker replicas per node:
  Rule: replicas = vCPUs / 2 = 4 / 2 = 2 replicas
  Sandboxes: 2 replicas × MAX_RUNNERS=2 = 4 sandboxes

Memory check (with F-19, 250 parallel TCs):
  4 sandboxes × 512MB = 2GB for children
  + OS + Docker overhead = ~2GB
  Total: ~4GB used out of 8GB ✓
```

So each c5.xlarge gives you **2 worker replicas**.

For 50 workers: `50 / 2 = 25 worker nodes`

---

## Full Cost: 2-Tier with c5.xlarge Workers

```
1 × server node  (c5.2xlarge recommended — c5.xlarge is tight for Rails+Redis+PostgreSQL)
25 × worker nodes (c5.xlarge)
```

### Why c5.xlarge is tight as a server node

```
c5.xlarge server (4 vCPU):
  Rails 4 processes × 20 threads  → needs ~2 vCPU
  PostgreSQL                       → needs ~1 vCPU
  Redis                            → needs ~0.5 vCPU
  Total needed:                    ~3.5 vCPU on a 4 vCPU machine
  Headroom:                        0.5 vCPU — risky under 1,000 concurrent submissions
```

### Option A — c5.xlarge everywhere (26 nodes)

| Node | Instance | Count | $/hr each | $/month (on-demand) | $/month (1yr) | $/month (3yr) | $/month (spot workers) |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Server | c5.xlarge | 1 | $0.170 | $124 | $77 | $49 | $124 (on-demand) |
| Workers | c5.xlarge | 25 | $0.170 | $3,100 | $1,925 | $1,225 | ~$925 |
| **Total** | | **26** | | **$3,224** | **$2,002** | **$1,274** | **~$1,049** |

### Option B — c5.2xlarge server + c5.xlarge workers (26 nodes)

| Node | Instance | Count | $/hr each | $/month (on-demand) | $/month (1yr) | $/month (3yr) | $/month (spot workers) |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Server | c5.2xlarge | 1 | $0.340 | $248 | $154 | $103 | $248 (on-demand) |
| Workers | c5.xlarge | 25 | $0.170 | $3,100 | $1,925 | $1,225 | ~$925 |
| **Total** | | **26** | | **$3,348** | **$2,079** | **$1,328** | **~$1,173** |
