## Full Comparison: All Architecture Options

| Architecture | Nodes | Workers | $/month on-demand | $/month 1yr | $/month spot | Complexity |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Single c5.metal | 1 | 47 | $2,978 | $1,854 | N/A (metal) | Lowest |
| c5.2xlarge + 9× c5.4xlarge | 10 | 54 | $4,716 | $2,992 | ~$1,695 | Medium |
| c5.2xlarge + 25× c5.xlarge | 26 | 50 | $3,348 | $2,079 | ~$1,173 | High (25 nodes) |
| c5.xlarge + 25× c5.xlarge | 26 | 50 | $3,224 | $2,002 | ~$1,049 | High + risky server |

---

## The Real Cost of 25 Nodes vs 9 Nodes

The c5.xlarge option looks cheaper on paper but has a hidden cost: **you are managing 25 machines instead of 9**.

```
25 nodes means:
  25 × EC2 instances to patch/update
  25 × Docker engines to maintain
  25 × nodes to monitor for health
  25 × Swarm join tokens to manage
  25 × Security group configurations
  25 × EBS volumes for Docker storage

9 nodes means:
  9 × everything above
```

In engineer-hours spent on operations, 25 nodes costs meaningfully more than 9 nodes — even if the AWS bill is lower.

---

## Recommendation

```
╔══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╗
║  If cost is the priority:                                    ║
║    c5.2xlarge server + 25× c5.xlarge workers (spot)         ║
║    ~$1,173/month                                            ║
║    Trade-off: 25 nodes to manage                            ║
║                                                              ║
║  If simplicity is the priority:                              ║
║    Single c5.metal node                                      ║
║    ~$2,978/month, 1 machine, no orchestration needed        ║
║    47 workers, docker-compose up --scale workers=47          ║
║                                                              ║
║  If balanced (cost + manageability):                         ║
║    c5.2xlarge server + 9× c5.4xlarge workers (spot)         ║
║    ~$1,695/month, 10 nodes, reasonable to manage            ║
╚══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╝
```

Which direction do you want to go?

=== USER ===
can you explain what exactly are the nodes where will the workers run what will be the max process, max runners etc everything details and if we are adapting to this approach i want you to explain how ill student code will flow end to end from submission to result calculations.

=== ASSISTANT ===
# Nodes, Workers, Configuration, and the Complete Student Code Flow

---

## PART 1 — What Exactly Is a Node

A **node** is simply one EC2 virtual machine. One computer in AWS's data center. It has a fixed number of CPU cores, a fixed amount of RAM, and runs Ubuntu 20.04.

```
Node = 1 EC2 instance = 1 physical-ish computer in AWS

Your 2-tier setup has 10 of these computers:

Computer 1  (server node)   → c5.2xlarge  →  8 vCPU,  16GB RAM
Computer 2  (worker node 1) → c5.4xlarge  → 16 vCPU,  32GB RAM
Computer 3  (worker node 2) → c5.4xlarge  → 16 vCPU,  32GB RAM
Computer 4  (worker node 3) → c5.4xlarge  → 16 vCPU,  32GB RAM
Computer 5  (worker node 4) → c5.4xlarge  → 16 vCPU,  32GB RAM
Computer 6  (worker node 5) → c5.4xlarge  → 16 vCPU,  32GB RAM
Computer 7  (worker node 6) → c5.4xlarge  → 16 vCPU,  32GB RAM
Computer 8  (worker node 7) → c5.4xlarge  → 16 vCPU,  32GB RAM
Computer 9  (worker node 8) → c5.4xlarge  → 16 vCPU,  32GB RAM
Computer 10 (worker node 9) → c5.4xlarge  → 16 vCPU,  32GB RAM
```

All 10 computers are connected to each other via AWS's private network (VPC). They can talk to each other but are invisible to the public internet (except port 2358 on the server node, which is exposed through a load balancer).

---

## PART 2 — What Runs on the Server Node

The server node is the **restaurant's main building** — it holds the front desk, the order board, and the pantry ledger. No actual cooking happens here.

```
SERVER NODE (Computer 1 — c5.2xlarge, 8 vCPU, 16GB RAM)
─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ubuntu 20.04
Docker Engine
│
├── Container: judge0-server
│     Image: judge0/judge0:1.13.0
│     Command: (default — runs Puma web server)
│     Port: 2358 exposed to internet (via ALB)
│     RAM: ~2GB
│     CPU: ~2 vCPU
│     Role: Accepts HTTP submissions, serves API
│     Config:
│       WEB_CONCURRENCY=4     (4 Puma processes)
│       RAILS_MAX_THREADS=25  (25 threads each)
│       Total HTTP slots: 4×25 = 100 connections
│
├── Container: postgres
│     Image: postgres:13
│     Port: 5432 (internal only, NOT exposed to internet)
│     RAM: ~4GB
│     CPU: ~2 vCPU
│     Role: Stores all submissions, results, status
│     Config:
│       max_connections=500
│
├── Container: redis
│     Image: redis:6.0
│     Port: 6379 (internal only, NOT exposed to internet)
│     RAM: ~2GB
│     CPU: ~0.5 vCPU
│     Role: Job queue — holds tokens waiting for a worker
│     Config:
│       maxmemory=2gb
│       maxmemory-policy=noeviction
│
└── Remaining: ~4GB RAM, ~3.5 vCPU for OS + Docker overhead
```

---

## PART 3 — What Runs on Each Worker Node

Each worker node is a **satellite cooking branch** — it has no front desk, no ledger, no order board. It only cooks. It picks up orders from the central order board (Redis on the server node) and sends results back to the central ledger (PostgreSQL on the server node).

```
WORKER NODE (any of Computers 2–10 — c5.4xlarge, 16 vCPU, 32GB RAM)
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ubuntu 20.04
Docker Engine
│
├── Container: judge0-worker-1
│     Image: judge0/judge0:1.13.0
│     Command: ./scripts/workers
│     Role: Polls Redis, runs sandboxes, writes results
│
├── Container: judge0-worker-2
│     (identical to worker-1)
│
├── Container: judge0-worker-3
│     (identical to worker-1)
│
├── Container: judge0-worker-4
│     (identical to worker-1)
│
├── Container: judge0-worker-5
│     (identical to worker-1)
│
└── Container: judge0-worker-6
      (identical to worker-1)

6 worker containers per node
9 nodes × 6 containers = 54 worker containers total
```

Each worker container is completely independent. They all connect to the same Redis and PostgreSQL on the server node. They do not talk to each other.

---

## PART 4 — Configuration Values Explained (Every Single One)

### judge0.conf — applies to ALL containers (server + all workers)

```ini
# ── POSTGRESQL ────────────────────────────────────────────
POSTGRES_HOST=10.0.1.45        # server node's private IP
                                # workers use this to find the database
POSTGRES_PORT=5432
POSTGRES_DB=judge0
POSTGRES_USER=judge0
POSTGRES_PASSWORD=judge0pass   # change before production

# ── REDIS ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
REDIS_HOST=10.0.1.45           # same server node private IP
REDIS_PORT=6379
REDIS_PASSWORD=judge0redis     # change before production

# ── RAILS ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
RAILS_ENV=production
RACK_ENV=production
SECRET_KEY_BASE=<64char random hex>

# ── JUDGE0 QUEUE ──────────────────────────────────────────
MAX_QUEUE_SIZE=10000
# How many tokens Redis holds waiting for a worker
# 54 workers drain fast — 10,000 is headroom

# ── JUDGE0 SANDBOX ────────────────────────────────────────
MAX_RUNNERS=2
# How many isolate sandboxes each worker container runs simultaneously
# 2 is the sweet spot — more causes CPU thrash within one container
# Formula: total sandboxes = worker_containers × MAX_RUNNERS
#          = 54 × 2 = 108 concurrent sandboxes

MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER=1100
# How many processes isolate ALLOWS inside one sandbox
# With F-19 (250 parallel TCs):
#   250 children + 1 head chef + overhead = ~260 per sandbox
#   2 sandboxes per container = ~520 per container
#   Set to 1100 to give headroom
# Without this: isolate's default is 64 → sequential fallback

SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT=524288
# Maximum harness source code size in bytes (512KB)
# 1000 TCs × 200 bytes avg input = ~220KB harness
# 512KB gives comfortable headroom

JUDGE0_TELEMETRY_ENABLE=false
```

### docker-compose.yml workers service — applies only to worker containers

```yaml
workers:
  image: judge0/judge0:1.13.0
  command: ["./scripts/workers"]
  privileged: true              # needed for isolate to create namespaces
  restart: always
  mem_limit: "6g"               # hard RAM cap per worker container
                                # 2 sandboxes × 250 children × ~12MB = ~6GB max
                                # prevents OOM cascade killing the whole node
  ulimits:
    nproc:
      soft: 65536               # max processes this container's UID can create
      hard: 65536               # 54 workers × 250 children = 13,500 peak
                                # 65,536 gives comfortable headroom
    nofile:
      soft: 65536               # max file descriptors
      hard: 65536               # 250 TCs × 2 pipe ends = 500 FDs per sandbox
                                # 2 sandboxes = 1,000 FDs per container
                                # 65,536 gives large headroom
  deploy:
    replicas: 6                 # 6 containers per node
    placement:
      constraints:
        - node.hostname != judge0-server   # NEVER run on server node
```

### The complete numbers pyramid

```
1 student submission
│
└── 1 Judge0 job (token)
    │
    └── picked up by 1 worker container (out of 54 total)
        │
        └── runs in 1 isolate sandbox (out of 2 per container)
            │
            └── harness forks children
                │
                ├── With F-19 (MAX_PARALLEL_TCS=250):
                │   Round 1: 250 children simultaneously
                │   Round 2: 250 children simultaneously
                │   Round 3: 250 children simultaneously
                │   Round 4: 250 children simultaneously
                │   Total: 1,000 TCs, 4 rounds × 7s = 28s
                │
                └── Each child:
                    RLIMIT_AS = 256MB (virtual memory cap)
                    SIGALRM   = 2s   (time cap per TC)
                    RLIMIT_NPROC = 1 (child cannot fork)


CAPACITY NUMBERS:
─────────────────
Worker containers:      54  (6 per node × 9 nodes)
MAX_RUNNERS per worker:  2
Total sandboxes:       108  (54 × 2)
Parallel TCs per batch: 250 (F-19 cap)
Peak concurrent children: 108 × 250 = 27,000 processes

Per worker NODE (c5.4xlarge, 16 vCPU):
  Containers:  6
  Sandboxes:   12  (6 × 2)
  Peak children: 12 × 250 = 3,000 processes
  Children per vCPU: 3,000 / 16 = 187 — manageable with bursty code
```

---

## PART 5 — End-to-End Student Code Flow

Now the complete journey. Every hop. Every machine. Every config value in action.

---

### STEP 1 — Student Clicks Submit (Your Platform)

The student finishes their code and clicks Submit on your platform's frontend (React). The browser sends a POST to your platform's backend (Node.js).

```
Student's browser
  → POST https://platform.edwisely.com/api/submit
  → Body: { code: "def solve(a,b): return a+b", language: "python", ... }
```

Your platform backend creates a `Submission` object and calls:

```python
result = autograder.grade(submission)
```

This is a blocking call. Your platform must either:
- Run it in a thread pool (1,000 students = 1,000 threads)
- Or use `grade_async()` with asyncio (recommended)

---

### STEP 2 — Autograder Builds the Mega-Recipe (Your Server / Platform)

Still on your platform's machine — not on any Judge0 node yet.

```
autograder.grade() runs:

① HarnessBuilder.__init__()
   → session_id = "a3f9c1e82b40"   (random 12-char hex)
   → delim = "@@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__"

② SecurityChecker.check()
   → AST scan of student code
   → Check: no import os, no exec(), no delimiter injection
   → PASS ✓

③ sanitize_for_injection()
   → Strip trailing whitespace (Python)

④ HarnessBuilder.build()
   → Load python_harness.py template
   → Fill: session_id, test_cases JSON, per_tc_limit_s=2, memory_limit_mb=256
   → Output: complete Mega-Recipe source code (~63KB for 1,000 TCs)
```

---

### STEP 3 — Mega-Recipe Sent to Server Node (Network Hop #1)

Your platform's autograder sends an HTTP POST to the Judge0 server node.

```
YOUR PLATFORM SERVER
  → POST http://10.0.1.45:2358/submissions?base64_encoded=true&wait=false
  → Headers: Content-Type: application/json
  → Body: {
      source_code: "<base64 of 63KB Mega-Recipe>",
      language_id: 71,
      cpu_time_limit: 7,
      wall_time_limit: 9,
      memory_limit: 262144,
      callback_url: "http://platform.edwisely.com:9090/result"
    }
  → timeout: 120 seconds
```

This POST goes across the internet (or VPC if platform is in same VPC) to the server node at `10.0.1.45`.

---

### STEP 4 — Server Node Accepts the Order (Server Node, Computer 1)

The request arrives at the server node. Puma (one of 4 processes × 25 threads = 100 available threads) picks it up.

```
SERVER NODE (10.0.1.45)
Judge0 API container (Puma)

Puma thread #14 handles this request:

① Decodes base64 source code
② Validates source_code size < SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT (512KB) ✓
③ Writes to PostgreSQL:
   INSERT INTO submissions (
     token     = "7b3e9c2a-f841-4d12-b093-a1234567890",
     source_code = "<base64>",
     language_id = 71,
     status_id   = 1,        ← "In Queue"
     created_at  = NOW()
   )

④ Pushes token to Redis:
   RPUSH "judge0:queue" "7b3e9c2a-f841-4d12-b093-a1234567890"
   Redis queue depth: was 47, now 48

⑤ Returns HTTP 201:
   { "token": "7b3e9c2a-f841-4d12-b093-a1234567890" }

Puma thread #14 is FREE again — total time: ~8ms
```

Your autograder receives the token. If using callback mode, it registers the token and blocks on `threading.Event`. If polling, it starts the poll loop.

---

### STEP 5 — A Worker Picks Up the Order (Network Hop #2)

On one of the 9 worker nodes, one of the 54 worker containers has been sitting idle, watching the Redis queue. Let's say it is **worker container 3 on worker node 5** (Computer 6).

```
WORKER NODE 5 (Computer 6 — 10.0.5.23)
Worker container 3

The worker process runs a tight loop:
  BLPOP "judge0:queue" timeout=5
  → blocks, waiting...
  → Redis on 10.0.1.45:6379 returns: "7b3e9c2a-f841-4d12-b093-a1234567890"

Got a token. Time to cook.

① Fetch submission from PostgreSQL (Network Hop to server node):
   SELECT * FROM submissions WHERE token = "7b3e9c2a-..."
   → Gets: source_code, language_id=71, cpu_time_limit=7, memory_limit=262144

② Update status in PostgreSQL:
   UPDATE submissions SET status_id=2 WHERE token="7b3e9c2a-..."
   → status: "In Queue" → "Processing"

③ This worker container currently has 0 of 2 MAX_RUNNERS slots in use.
   Slot available → proceed.
   (If both slots were busy, the token would be re-queued after a delay)
```

---

### STEP 6 — The Sandbox Opens (Worker Node 5, Container 3)

The worker invokes `isolate` to create a sealed environment.

```
WORKER NODE 5 — Worker Container 3
Inside the container, the worker process calls:

isolate --init
→ Creates /var/local/lib/isolate/0/ (the sandbox directory)
→ Sets up Linux namespaces:
    PID namespace:    sandbox sees its own PID 1
    Mount namespace:  sandbox has its own filesystem view
    Network namespace: sandbox has no network (ALLOW_ENABLE_NETWORK=false)
    User namespace:   sandbox runs as UID 65000 (restricted user)

→ Writes Mega-Recipe to /var/local/lib/isolate/0/box/solution.py

→ Sets cgroup limits:
    memory:   262144 KB (256MB) — from memory_limit
    cpu time: 7 seconds         — from cpu_time_limit
    wall time: 9 seconds        — from wall_time_limit
    processes: 1100             — from MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER

→ Runs: isolate --run -- python3 solution.py
```

The sandbox is now alive. `python3 solution.py` starts executing the Mega-Recipe.

---

### STEP 7 — Inside the Sandbox: The Mega-Recipe Executes (Worker Node 5, Container 3, Sandbox)

This is where all 1,000 test cases actually get evaluated.

```
SANDBOX (inside worker container 3, worker node 5)
UID: 65000
PID namespace: starts at PID 1

python3 solution.py begins:

① Runtime security installed:
   builtins.open  = _safe_open    (blocks harness file reads)
   signal.signal  = _safe_signal  (blocks SIGALRM override)
   sys.exit       = _safe_exit    (raises instead of exits)

② Student function defined in parent namespace:
   def solve(a, b):
       return a + b

③ _can_fork_n(250) called:   ← F-19: batch size is 250, not 1000
   resource.getrlimit(RLIMIT_NPROC) → (65536, 65536)
   current threads: 3
   available: 65536 - 3 - 2 = 65531
   65531 >= 250 → True ✓  (parallel mode)

────────────────────────────────────
ROUND 1: Test cases 1–250
────────────────────────────────────

④ FORK PHASE (250 children spawned simultaneously):

   For TC #1:
     r_fd, w_fd = os.pipe()   → tickets 3, 4
     pid = os.fork()          → PID 2 (Junior Chef 1)
     Child PID 2:
       resource.setrlimit(RLIMIT_AS, 268435456)  ← 256MB virtual memory cap
       signal.alarm(2)                            ← 2-second SIGALRM timer
       result = solve(2, 3)                       ← calls student code
       → returns 5
       write(w_fd, '{"status":"PASS","got":"5","expected":"5"}')
       os._exit(0)

   For TC #2:
     r_fd, w_fd = os.pipe()   → tickets 5, 6
     pid = os.fork()          → PID 3 (Junior Chef 2)
     Child PID 3: ... same pattern ...

   ... 248 more forks ...

   For TC #250:
     → PID 251 (Junior Chef 250) cooking simultaneously

   All 250 children are NOW RUNNING IN PARALLEL.
   Total FDs open: 250 × 2 = 500  ← well under ulimit 65536 ✓
   Total processes for UID 65000: 251 (1 parent + 250 children)

⑤ COLLECT PHASE (select/poll watches all 250 hatches):

   t=0ms:   select() waits on FDs [3,5,7,...,499]

   t=80ms:  TC#1 child writes result, closes FD 4
            select() wakes: FD 3 is readable
            parent reads: {"status":"PASS","got":"5","expected":"5"}
            os.waitpid(PID 2) — reaps child, frees process slot

   t=82ms:  TC#2 child writes result → collected
   t=83ms:  TC#3 child writes result → collected
   ...
   t=250ms: TC#250 child writes result → collected

   All 250 results collected in ~250ms total.

⑥ KILL PHASE (any overruns):
   None killed — all finished in time.

⑦ EMIT PHASE (print results 1–250):
   @@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__START_1
   {"status": "PASS", "got": "5", "expected": "5", "detail": ""}
   @@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__END_1
   ...
   @@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__START_250
   ...
   @@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__END_250

────────────────────────────────────
ROUND 2: Test cases 251–500
────────────────────────────────────
   Same process. 250 more forks. ~250ms.

ROUND 3: Test cases 501–750    (~250ms)
ROUND 4: Test cases 751–1000   (~250ms)

⑧ FINAL MARKER:
   @@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__DONE

Total sandbox execution time: ~1,000ms actual cooking
+ Python startup overhead: ~1,500ms
+ Fork overhead per round: ~200ms × 4 = 800ms
Total wall time: ~3.3 seconds (well within 9s wall_time_limit)
```

---

### STEP 8 — Worker Writes Result Back (Network Hop #3)

The sandbox exits. The worker process collects the stdout.

```
WORKER NODE 5 — Worker Container 3

isolate returns:
  exit code: 0
  stdout: <full harness output with all 1,000 TC blocks>
  stderr: ""
  time: 3.3
  memory: 18420  (KB)
  status: Accepted (Judge0 status_id = 3)

Worker writes to PostgreSQL (Network Hop to server node):
  UPDATE submissions SET
    stdout      = base64("<harness output>"),
    stderr      = "",
    time        = "3.3",
    memory      = 18420,
    status_id   = 3,          ← "Accepted"
    finished_at = NOW()
  WHERE token = "7b3e9c2a-..."

Worker fires callback (Network Hop to your platform):
  PUT http://platform.edwisely.com:9090/result
  Body: {
    token: "7b3e9c2a-...",
    status: { id: 3, description: "Accepted" },
    stdout: "<base64 of full output>",
    time: "3.3",
    memory: 18420
  }

Worker container 3 is now FREE.
  MAX_RUNNERS slot released.
  Back to watching Redis queue for next token.
```

---

### STEP 9 — Autograder Receives Result (Your Platform, Network Hop #4)

```
YOUR PLATFORM SERVER

CallbackServer receives PUT on port 9090:
  token = "7b3e9c2a-..."
  payload stored in _results dict
  threading.Event for this token → .set()

grade() was blocking on evt.wait() → wakes up immediately

Judge0Result assembled:
  stdout = base64decode(payload.stdout)
           = full harness output (all TC delimiter blocks)
  status_str = "Accepted"
  time_taken_s = 3.3
  memory_kb = 18420
```

---

### STEP 10 — OutputParser Reads the Barcoded Output (Your Platform)

```
YOUR PLATFORM SERVER

parse_judge0_response() called:
  judge0_status = "Accepted" → not TLE, not compile error → parse normally

OutputParser.parse():

① DONE marker check:
   "@@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__DONE" in stdout → True
   global_tle = False ✓

② Regex extraction:
   Pattern: @@TC_RESULT__a3f9c1e82b40__START_(\d+)...(.*?)...END_\1
   Finds: TC blocks 1 through 1000
   found_tcs = {1, 2, 3, ..., 1000}

③ Per-TC parsing:
   TC#1:   json.loads → status=PASS, got="5",  expected="5"  → TCResult(1, PASS)
   TC#2:   json.loads → status=PASS, got="30", expected="30" → TCResult(2, PASS)
   ...
   TC#997: json.loads → status=FAIL, got="14", expected="15" → TCResult(997, FAIL)
   ...
   TC#1000: json.loads → status=PASS ...

④ Missing TC check:
   All 1000 found → no MISSING TCs

⑤ Score:
   score = count of PASS results = 987
   total = 1000
   partial_execution = False

Returns ParsedSubmission(score=987, total=1000, tc_results=[...])
```

---

### STEP 11 — Result Delivered to Student (Your Platform → Browser)

```
YOUR PLATFORM SERVER

GradingResult assembled:
  student_id = "student_042"
  score      = 987
  total      = 1000
  global_tle = False

Platform writes to your DB:
  INSERT INTO assessment_results (student_id, score, total, ...) VALUES (...)

Platform sends to student via SSE/WebSocket:
  { "score": 987, "total": 1000, "tc_results": [...] }

Student's browser receives:
  "Score: 987 / 1000"
  TC1: PASS ✓
  TC2: PASS ✓
  ...
  TC997: FAIL ✗  (got 14, expected 15)
  ...
```

## PART 6 — The Complete Picture

```
STUDENT                    YOUR PLATFORM          SERVER NODE           WORKER NODE 5
BROWSER                    (Node.js + Python)     (Computer 1)         (Computer 6)
   │                              │                    │                     │
   │──POST /submit────────────────▶│                    │                     │
   │                              │                    │                     │
   │                         HarnessBuilder            │                     │
   │                         SecurityChecker           │                     │
   │                         Build Mega-Recipe         │                     │
   │                              │                    │                     │
   │                              │──POST /submissions─▶│                     │
   │                              │    (120s timeout)   │                     │
   │                              │                 Puma writes              │
   │                              │                 PostgreSQL               │
   │                              │                 Redis RPUSH              │
   │                              │◀──{ token: "7b3e9c2a" }─│               │
   │                              │                    │     │               │
   │                              │                    │  Redis BLPOP        │
   │                              │                    │◀────────────────────│
   │                              │                 PostgreSQL               │
   │                              │                 SELECT submission        │
   │                              │──────────────────────────────────────────▶
   │                              │                 isolate sandbox          │
   │                              │                 python3 solution.py      │
   │                              │                 fork × 250 × 4 rounds   │
   │                              │                 collect results          │
   │                              │                 print delimited output   │
   │                              │                    │                     │

   │                              │   stdout=<harness output>               │
   │                         OutputParser             │                     │
   │                         parse TC blocks          │                     │
   │                         score=987/1000           │                     │
   │◀──SSE: { score: 987 }────────│                    │                     │
   │                              │                    │                     │
"987/1000 ✓"                      │                    │                     │


TOTAL TIME:
  Security check + Harness build:  ~50ms   (your platform)
  POST to Judge0 + queue:          ~10ms   (network + Redis)
  Queue wait (if busy):            0–60s   (depends on worker availability)
  Sandbox execution:               ~3.3s   (4 rounds × 250 TCs)
  Result write + callback:         ~20ms   (PostgreSQL + network)
  OutputParser:                    ~30ms   (regex over ~200KB output)
  ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  Total (no queue wait):           ~3.5s
  Total (worst case, queue full):  ~63.5s
```

ecs?

=== ASSISTANT ===
# ECS — The Fully Managed Restaurant Chain

---

## PART 1 — What ECS Actually Is

With Docker Compose, you are a **restaurant owner who also manages the building** — you hire staff, you arrange the kitchen, you decide which chef stands where, you handle it if a chef quits.

With Docker Swarm, you hired a **building manager** who coordinates the staff across multiple branches — but you still own and maintain all the buildings.

With ECS, you handed everything to **AWS Hospitality Group** — a corporation that owns the buildings, hires the managers, monitors every kitchen, and scales the number of branches up and down based on how many customers are in line. You only write the **menu** (task definitions) and say how many dishes you want served per hour.

```
Docker Compose  = You own 1 restaurant, you do everything
Docker Swarm    = You own 10 restaurants, you hire a coordinator
ECS             = You franchise your concept to AWS
                  AWS owns the buildings, manages the staff
                  You define the recipes and the standards
                  AWS handles everything else
```

---

## PART 2 — Every ECS Component Explained

### Component 1 — The ECS Cluster (The Franchise Territory)

A cluster is just a **named boundary** that groups all your EC2 instances and containers together.

```
Cluster name: judge0-production

Everything inside this cluster:
  EC2 instances (the buildings)
  Running containers (the staff)
  Services (the departments)
  Tasks (individual workers)

Clusters are free. You pay for the EC2 instances inside them.
```

### Component 2 — EC2 Instance with ECS Agent (The Building + Building Manager)

Each EC2 instance (c5.4xlarge) runs Ubuntu 20.04 with one extra piece of software: the **ECS Agent** — a small daemon that talks to AWS's ECS control plane.

```
EC2 Instance (c5.4xlarge)
├── Ubuntu 20.04
├── Docker Engine
├── ECS Agent (small Go binary, ~50MB RAM)
│     → Constantly reports to AWS: "I have 14 vCPU free, 28GB RAM free"
│     → Receives instructions: "Run this container on your machine"
│     → Reports container health back to AWS
│     → If this agent stops: AWS marks the instance as DRAINING
│
└── Running containers (assigned by ECS scheduler)
```

The ECS Agent is what turns a plain EC2 instance into a worker that ECS can schedule containers onto.

### Component 3 — Task Definition (The Recipe Card)

A Task Definition is ECS's equivalent of one service block in `docker-compose.yml`. It is a **JSON document** that describes everything about how a container should run.

```json
{
  "family": "judge0-worker",
  "containerDefinitions": [
    {
      "name": "worker",
      "image": "judge0/judge0:1.13.0",
      "command": ["./scripts/workers"],
      "privileged": true,
      "memory": 6144,
      "cpu": 2048,
      "ulimits": [
        { "name": "nofile", "softLimit": 65536, "hardLimit": 65536 },
        { "name": "nproc",  "softLimit": 65536, "hardLimit": 65536 }
      ],
      "environment": [
        { "name": "REDIS_HOST",   "value": "redis.judge0.local" },
        { "name": "MAX_RUNNERS",  "value": "2" }
      ],
      "secrets": [
        { "name": "REDIS_PASSWORD",    "valueFrom": "arn:aws:ssm:...redis_password" },
        { "name": "POSTGRES_PASSWORD", "valueFrom": "arn:aws:ssm:...db_password" }
      ],
      "logConfiguration": {
        "logDriver": "awslogs",
        "options": {
          "awslogs-group": "/judge0/workers",
          "awslogs-region": "us-east-1"
        }
      }
    }
  ]
}
```

Notice what is gone compared to `docker-compose.yml`:
- No file volume mounts for `judge0.conf` — secrets come from AWS Parameter Store
- No explicit IP addresses — service discovery via DNS
- Logs go to CloudWatch automatically — no manual log driver setup

### Component 4 — ECS Task (One Running Container)

A **Task** is one running instance of a Task Definition. Like a single container.

```
Task Definition: judge0-worker  (the recipe)
Running Task #1: judge0-worker  (one container, on worker node 3)
Running Task #2: judge0-worker  (one container, on worker node 7)
...
Running Task #54: judge0-worker (one container, on worker node 9)
```

54 tasks all running from the same Task Definition. If Task #23 crashes, ECS automatically starts Task #55 as a replacement.

### Component 5 — ECS Service (The Department Manager)

A Service says: **"I want N tasks of this type running at all times."**

```
Service: judge0-workers
  Task Definition: judge0-worker
  Desired Count: 54
  Minimum Healthy: 27  (50% — during rolling updates)
  Maximum Count: 108   (200% — auto-scaling ceiling)

ECS constantly checks:
  Running tasks: 54? → do nothing
  Running tasks: 51? → start 3 new tasks (a node died)
  Running tasks: 54, queue depth > 200? → scale to 72 tasks
  Exam ended, queue empty? → scale back to 6 tasks (minimum)
```

### Component 6 — Application Load Balancer (The Maître D')

The ALB sits in front of the Judge0 API and distributes incoming submissions.

```
Internet
   ↓
ALB (judge0-alb)
  Listener: port 443 (HTTPS)
  Certificate: ACM certificate for judge0.edwisely.com
  Target Group: judge0-server tasks on port 2358
  Health Check: GET /system_info → 200 OK
   ↓
judge0-server task (Computer 1)
  Port 2358
```

Students never hit the EC2 IP directly. They hit the ALB DNS name. If the server task moves to a different IP, the ALB automatically updates its routing.

### Component 7 — Auto Scaling (The Staffing Agency)

This is ECS's biggest advantage over Swarm. Based on metrics, ECS automatically starts or stops worker tasks.

```
CloudWatch Alarm: "redis_queue_depth > 100 for 2 consecutive minutes"
  → ECS Application Auto Scaling: add 12 worker tasks (2 per node)
  → Total workers: 54 → 66

CloudWatch Alarm: "redis_queue_depth < 5 for 5 consecutive minutes"
  → ECS Application Auto Scaling: remove 48 worker tasks
  → Total workers: 66 → 6 (minimum idle baseline)

Exam day scenario:
  9:55 AM: 6 workers idle
  10:00 AM: 1,000 students submit → queue depth spikes to 1,000
  10:02 AM: CloudWatch alarm fires → ECS scales to 54 workers
  10:05 AM: queue draining → first results back
  12:00 PM: exam ends → queue empty → ECS scales back to 6 workers
  
You pay for 54 workers for ~2 hours, not all day.
```

### Component 8 — AWS Cloud Map / Service Discovery (The Internal Phone Directory)

Worker containers on node 7 need to reach Redis on the server node. With ECS, you don't hardcode IPs. AWS Cloud Map gives each service a DNS name:

```
redis.judge0.local   → resolves to the Redis container's private IP
server.judge0.local  → resolves to the Judge0 API container's private IP

Worker container connects to redis.judge0.local:6379
If Redis moves to a new IP (container restart), DNS updates automatically.
```

### Component 9 — AWS Parameter Store / Secrets Manager (The Safe)

Your `judge0.conf` passwords are no longer in a file on disk. They live in AWS Parameter Store:

```
/judge0/production/POSTGRES_PASSWORD  = "prod_secure_password_xyz"
/judge0/production/REDIS_PASSWORD     = "prod_redis_password_abc"
/judge0/production/SECRET_KEY_BASE    = "64char_hex_..."

ECS injects these as environment variables when starting each container.
Nobody needs SSH access to read them.
Rotating a password = update Parameter Store → restart service → done.
```

### Component 10 — CloudWatch Container Insights (The CCTV System)

```
Every container automatically sends:
  CPU usage per task
  Memory usage per task
  Network in/out per task
  Container restart count

Every EC2 instance automatically sends:
  Disk usage
  CPU steal time
  Memory pressure

You get dashboards like:
  "Worker task #34 is using 95% memory — OOM risk"
  "3 worker tasks restarted in last 10 minutes — check logs"
  "Redis queue depth: 847 — auto-scaling triggered"
```

```
              │  │  └──────────┘  └──────────┘   │   │
              │  │       ...9 instances total...  │   │
              │  └───────────────────────────────┘   │
              │                                      │
              │  ┌───────────────────────────────┐   │
              │  │  Auto Scaling Group            │   │
              │  │  Min instances: 2              │   │
              │  │  Max instances: 12             │   │
              │  │  Scale on: redis_queue_depth   │   │
              │  └───────────────────────────────┘   │
              └──────────────────────────────────────┘
```

## PART 4 — What Changes in Student Code Flow with ECS

The flow is almost identical to the Docker Swarm flow — the student experience does not change. What changes is **who manages** each hop.

### What ECS Manages Automatically (vs You Managing It in Swarm)

```
TASK                          DOCKER SWARM (you)    ECS (AWS)
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Worker container crash         You notice, restart   Auto-restart in <30s
Node failure                   Manual re-provision   ASG launches new EC2
Scale workers for exam         SSH + docker scale    CloudWatch alarm fires
Rolling image update           docker service update aws ecs update-service
Centralized logs               Manual ELK setup      CloudWatch auto
Health check routing           Manual               ALB integration
SSL certificate renewal        Manual / certbot      ACM auto-renews
Database failover              None (single DB)      RDS Multi-AZ auto
Redis failover                 None                  ElastiCache auto
```

### The One Flow Difference: RDS + ElastiCache

With self-managed PostgreSQL and Redis (Docker Compose / Swarm), they run as containers. With ECS production setup, you use managed services:

```
Self-managed PostgreSQL container:
  If container crashes → submissions lost for ~30s while restarting
  No automatic backup
  No standby replica
  You manage upgrades

RDS PostgreSQL (managed):
  Multi-AZ: standby replica in different availability zone
  Automatic failover in ~60s if primary dies
  Automated daily backups, 7-day retention
  AWS patches the database engine
  Cost: ~$200/month for db.r5.large Multi-AZ

Self-managed Redis container:
  If container crashes → entire job queue lost
  noeviction protects but container death loses everything in memory

ElastiCache Redis (managed):
  Replication group: primary + 1 replica
  AOF persistence (like your current config)
  Automatic failover
  Cost: ~$100/month for cache.r6g.large
```

---

## PART 5 — New Scenarios ECS Introduces

### Scenario 1 — Workers Need Privileged Mode in ECS

Judge0 workers require `privileged: true` because isolate creates Linux namespaces and mounts cgroup controllers.

**ECS Fargate does NOT support privileged containers.**

This means you are locked into **ECS EC2 launch type** for workers. The server (Judge0 API) can run on Fargate, but workers cannot.

```
judge0-server service  → ECS Fargate ✓ (no privileged needed)
judge0-workers service → ECS EC2     ✓ (privileged needed)
```

### Scenario 2 — Custom CloudWatch Metric for Redis Queue Depth

ECS auto-scaling only triggers on CloudWatch metrics. Redis queue depth is not a native CloudWatch metric. You must push it yourself.

You need a small Lambda function or a CloudWatch agent that runs every 60 seconds:

```python
# Lambda function (runs every 60 seconds via EventBridge)
import boto3
import redis

def lambda_handler(event, context):
    r = redis.Redis(host='redis.judge0.local', port=6379,
                    password=os.environ['REDIS_PASSWORD'])
    queue_depth = r.llen('judge0:queue')

    cloudwatch = boto3.client('cloudwatch')
    cloudwatch.put_metric_data(
        Namespace='Judge0',
        MetricData=[{
            'MetricName': 'RedisQueueDepth',
            'Value': queue_depth,
            'Unit': 'Count'
        }]
    )
```

Then your auto-scaling policy uses this metric:

```
Scale OUT: RedisQueueDepth > 100 for 2 minutes → add 12 worker tasks
Scale IN:  RedisQueueDepth < 5  for 5 minutes → remove 12 worker tasks
```

### Scenario 3 — Task Definitions Replace judge0.conf

There is no `judge0.conf` file in ECS. All configuration is in the Task Definition as environment variables and secret references:

```json
"environment": [
    { "name": "REDIS_HOST",              "value": "redis.judge0.local" },
    { "name": "POSTGRES_HOST",           "value": "judge0-db.xxxx.us-east-1.rds.amazonaws.com" },
    { "name": "MAX_RUNNERS",             "value": "2" },
    { "name": "MAX_QUEUE_SIZE",          "value": "10000" },
    { "name": "MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER","value": "1100" },
    { "name": "SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT",  "value": "524288" },
    { "name": "RAILS_ENV",               "value": "production" }
],
"secrets": [
    { "name": "REDIS_PASSWORD",    "valueFrom": "/judge0/prod/redis_password" },
    { "name": "POSTGRES_PASSWORD", "valueFrom": "/judge0/prod/postgres_password" },
    { "name": "SECRET_KEY_BASE",   "valueFrom": "/judge0/prod/secret_key_base" }
]
```

Changing `MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER` from 1100 to 1200 means:
1. Update Task Definition → create new revision (v2)
2. Update Service to use Task Definition v2
3. ECS rolling deploys new tasks — old tasks finish current jobs, new tasks start with new value
4. Zero downtime

### Scenario 4 — Spot Capacity Providers (Cheapest ECS Option)

ECS supports **Capacity Providers** — a way to mix on-demand and spot instances automatically.

```
Capacity Provider Strategy for judge0-workers:
  Base: 6 tasks on ON-DEMAND instances (always available, never interrupted)
  Burst: remaining tasks on SPOT instances (70% cheaper, can be interrupted)

ECS handles:
  If spot instance gets 2-minute interruption warning:
    → ECS drains the tasks on that instance (stops accepting new jobs)
    → Current jobs finish (7-28 seconds — well within 2 minutes)
    → Instance terminates cleanly
    → ECS launches replacement task on another instance
```

```
Cost with Spot Capacity Provider (us-east-1):
  6 on-demand tasks  × c5.4xlarge on-demand rate
  48 spot tasks      × c5.4xlarge spot rate (~$0.22/hr per instance)

Monthly cost during exams (2 hours/day, 20 days/month):
  On-demand baseline (always on): 2 instances × $0.68 × 730hr = $993/month
  Spot burst (exam hours only):   7 instances × $0.22 × 40hr  = $62/month
  RDS + ElastiCache:                                           = $300/month
  ALB + CloudWatch:                                            = $50/month
  Total:                                                       ~$1,405/month
```

### Scenario 5 — ECS Exec (Replacing SSH for Debugging)

With EC2 + Docker Compose, you SSH into the machine and run `docker exec -it container bash`.

With ECS, you never SSH. You use ECS Exec:

```bash
# Debug a specific running worker task
aws ecs execute-command \
  --cluster judge0-production \
  --task 7b3e9c2a-f841-4d12-b093-a1234567890 \
  --container worker \
  --interactive \
  --command "/bin/bash"

# Now you're inside the container on whatever EC2 instance it's running on
# You never needed to know which EC2 instance it was on
```

## PART 6 — ECS Cost Breakdown

### Architecture

```
ALB:                    1 × ALB
Server:                 1 × c5.2xlarge EC2 (on-demand, always on)
Workers (exam hours):   9 × c5.4xlarge EC2 (spot, ~40 hrs/month)
Workers (baseline):     2 × c5.4xlarge EC2 (on-demand, always on)
Database:               RDS db.r5.large Multi-AZ
Cache:                  ElastiCache cache.r6g.large
```

### Monthly Cost

| Component | Type | $/month |
|---|---|---|
| ALB | Managed | $20 |
| Server node c5.2xlarge | On-demand 24/7 | $248 |
| 2 baseline worker nodes c5.4xlarge | On-demand 24/7 | $993 |
| 7 burst worker nodes c5.4xlarge | Spot, 40hrs/month | $62 |
| RDS PostgreSQL db.r5.large Multi-AZ | Managed | $248 |
| ElastiCache Redis cache.r6g.large | Managed | $122 |
| CloudWatch + Lambda metric pusher | Managed | $30 |
| ECR image storage | Managed | $5 |
| Parameter Store | Managed | $5 |
| Data transfer | — | $20 |
| **Total** | | **~$1,753/month** |

With 1-year reserved for always-on instances:

| Component | $/month |
|---|---|
| Server node (1yr reserved) | $154 |
| 2 baseline workers (1yr reserved) | $631 |
| 7 burst workers (spot) | $62 |
| RDS 1yr reserved | $162 |
| ElastiCache 1yr reserved | $79 |
| Everything else | $80 |
| **Total** | **~$1,168/month** |

```
║ Auto-scaling     ║ No           ║ Manual        ║ Fully automatic           ║

```

## PART 8 — Recommendation Based on Your Context

```
╔══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╗
║  You run timed coding exams.                                 ║
║  1,000 students submit at 10:00 AM sharp.                    ║
║  By 10:15 AM the queue is empty.                             ║
║  You pay nothing for idle workers the rest of the day.       ║
╠══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╣
║  This is EXACTLY the workload ECS was designed for.          ║
║                                                              ║
║  With ECS:                                                   ║
║    9:59 AM: 6 workers idle, costing ~$0.68/hr               ║
║    10:00 AM: 1,000 submissions → queue depth = 1,000        ║
║    10:02 AM: ECS scales to 54 workers automatically         ║
║    10:20 AM: queue drained → ECS scales back to 6           ║
║    Cost for the burst: ~2 hours × 9 spot instances          ║
║             = 2 × 9 × $0.22 = $3.96                        ║
║                                                              ║
║  With Single Node:                                           ║
║    47 workers running 24/7 = $4.08/hr = $2,978/month        ║
║    You pay for 47 workers at 3 AM when nobody is submitting ║
╚══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╝
```

**Start with**: Single node c5.metal for your first real exam (fastest to set up, validate the system works).

**Migrate to**: ECS within 3 months once you know your exam schedule and submission patterns — the cost savings are substantial and the auto-scaling matches your exam-day burst pattern perfectly.

Do you want me to proceed with implementing the production configuration for any of these three approaches?

=== USER ===
For 1000 concurrent submissions how many workers would i need.

=== ASSISTANT ===
Good question. The answer is not a single number — it depends on one thing you need to decide first: **how long can the last student wait for their result?**

Let me show the math.

---

## The Formula

```
Every submission takes T seconds to execute in one sandbox.
You have (workers × MAX_RUNNERS) = (N × 2) sandboxes running in parallel.

Time for ALL 1,000 to finish:
              1,000 × T
  drain time = ─────────
                 N × 2
```

Two values of T depending on whether F-19 (batching) is applied:

```
Without F-19  (1,000 TCs run in parallel):  T = 7s   (2s TC limit + 5s overhead)
With F-19     (250 TCs × 4 rounds):         T = 28s  (4 × 7s)
```

---

## The Numbers

### Without F-19 (T = 7s)

| Workers (N) | Sandboxes | Drain time | All 1,000 done in |
|---|---|---|---|
| 6 | 12 | 583s | **9.7 minutes** |
| 12 | 24 | 292s | **4.9 minutes** |
| 30 | 60 | 117s | **2 minutes** |
| 59 | 118 | 59s | **~1 minute** |
| 119 | 238 | 29s | **30 seconds** |
| 500 | 1,000 | 7s | **7 seconds (real-time)** |

### With F-19 (T = 28s)

| Workers (N) | Sandboxes | Drain time | All 1,000 done in |
|---|---|---|---|
| 6 | 12 | 2,333s | **38.9 minutes** |
| 12 | 24 | 1,167s | **19.4 minutes** |
| 47 | 94 | 298s | **~5 minutes** |
| 117 | 234 | 120s | **2 minutes** |
| 234 | 468 | 60s | **1 minute** |
| 500 | 1,000 | 28s | **28 seconds** |

---

## The Hidden Constraint: Memory

Here is why you cannot just pick "59 workers, T=7s, done in 1 minute" without thinking about RAM.

```
Without F-19 — 1,000 forks per sandbox:
  Per sandbox RAM: ~3GB
  59 workers × 2 sandboxes = 118 sandboxes
  118 × 3GB = 354GB RAM needed

With F-19 — 250 forks per round:
  Per sandbox RAM: ~762MB
  47 workers × 2 sandboxes = 94 sandboxes
  94 × 762MB = ~72GB RAM needed
```

This determines what instances you can use:

| Setup | RAM needed | Fits on |
|---|---|---|
| 12 workers, no F-19 | 12×2×3GB = 72GB | 1× c5.18xlarge (144GB) |
| 59 workers, no F-19 | 354GB | 3× c5.18xlarge |
| 12 workers, F-19 | 12×2×762MB = ~18GB | 2× c5.4xlarge (32GB) |
| 47 workers, F-19 | ~72GB | 3× c5.4xlarge (32GB each) |
| 117 workers, F-19 | ~179GB | 6× c5.4xlarge |

---

## Decision Table

Pick your target. This is the answer:

```
╔══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╗
║  TARGET: All 1,000 results in 5 minutes (300s) — exam-friendly          ║
╠══════════════╦═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╣
║  F-19 OFF    ║  12 workers  → 292s drain                                ║
║              ║  Memory: 72GB → 1× c5.18xlarge ($2,200/month)           ║
╠══════════════╬═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╣
║  F-19 ON     ║  47 workers  → 298s drain                                ║
║              ║  Memory: 72GB → 3× c5.4xlarge ($1,000/month spot)       ║
╚══════════════╩═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╝

╔══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╗
║  TARGET: All 1,000 results in 2 minutes (120s) — fast feedback          ║
╠══════════════╦═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╣
║  F-19 OFF    ║  30 workers  → 117s drain                                ║
║              ║  Memory: 180GB → 2× c5.18xlarge ($4,400/month)          ║
╠══════════════╬═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╣
║  F-19 ON     ║  117 workers → 120s drain                                ║
║              ║  Memory: 179GB → 6× c5.4xlarge ($2,400/month spot)      ║
╚══════════════╩═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╝

╔══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╗
║  TARGET: All 1,000 results in 1 minute (60s) — near real-time           ║
╠══════════════╦═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╣
║  F-19 OFF    ║  59 workers  → 59s drain                                 ║
║              ║  Memory: 354GB → 3× c5.18xlarge ($6,600/month)          ║
╠══════════════╬═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╣
║  F-19 ON     ║  234 workers → 60s drain                                 ║
║              ║  Memory: ~357GB → 12× c5.4xlarge ($4,700/month spot)    ║
╚══════════════╩═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╝
```

---

## The Practical Recommendation

```
For a coding exam where 1,000 students submit simultaneously:

Students are WRITING CODE during the exam.
After submitting, they go back to reading the problem.
They do not stare at the result screen for 5 minutes.
A 5-minute result time is completely acceptable.

RECOMMENDED: 47 workers with F-19 ON
  → All 1,000 results in ~5 minutes
  → Memory: 72GB (3× c5.4xlarge, 32GB each)
  → Cost: ~$1,000/month (spot instances)
  → Simple: fits on 3 worker nodes + 1 server node
  → Workers per node: 16 vCPU / 2 = 8 replicas × 2 runners = 16 sandboxes
```

---

## One More Reality Check

The formula assumes all 1,000 submissions arrive at **exactly t=0**. In practice, even if an exam "opens" at 10:00 AM, submissions spread over 3–5 minutes as students read the problem, write code, and submit at different times.

```
Real exam submission pattern:
  10:00–10:03:  ~200 submissions (fast students)
  10:03–10:10:  ~500 submissions (main wave)
  10:10–10:30:  ~300 submissions (slower students)

Peak concurrent queue: ~500 (not 1,000)

With 47 workers at peak 500:
  drain time = (500 × 28) / (47 × 2) = 149s = 2.5 minutes
  → First submissions return results before the wave even peaks
```

**47 workers handles your load comfortably in the real world.** Scale to 117 workers only if you need guaranteed sub-2-minute results for a simultaneous burst.

now explain the scenario based on load and no load with load balancers.

=== ASSISTANT ===
# Load Balancers: No Load vs Full Load

---

## PART 1 — What a Load Balancer Actually Is

In restaurant terms, a load balancer is the **Maître D' standing at the front door** of a restaurant chain with multiple identical counters.

When one customer arrives — he points them to Counter 1 (free).
When 1,000 customers arrive simultaneously — he distributes them: 100 to Counter 1, 100 to Counter 2, 100 to Counter 3... and so on.

He also watches each counter constantly. If Counter 4's staff collapses, he stops sending customers there and redirects to other counters. Customers never know Counter 4 exists.

```
Without load balancer:
  1,000 students → all hit the SAME server → server collapses

With load balancer:
  1,000 students → Maître D' → distributed across multiple servers
                               each server gets a manageable share
```

---

## PART 2 — Where Load Balancers Sit in Your System

Your system has **two load balancers** and one **queue-based distributor**. Each handles a different layer.

```
STUDENT BROWSER
      │
      │ HTTPS
      ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────┐
│  ALB #1 — Platform Load Balancer│  ← distributes student HTTP requests
│  your-platform.edwisely.com     │    to your Node.js/Rails platform servers
└─────────────┬───────────────────┘
              │ (your platform processes submission,
              │  calls autograder, which calls Judge0)
              ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────┐
│  ALB #2 — Judge0 API LB         │  ← distributes POST /submissions
│  judge0-api.internal            │    to Judge0 Puma server instances
└─────────────┬───────────────────┘
              │ writes token to Redis
              ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────┐
│  REDIS QUEUE                    │  ← this IS the load balancer for workers
│  judge0:queue                   │    workers PULL jobs (no LB needed)
└─────────────┬───────────────────┘
              │ BLPOP (workers compete for jobs)
              ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────┐
│  WORKERS (47 replicas)          │  ← no load balancer here
│  each pulls its own job         │    Redis distributes naturally
└─────────────────────────────────┘
```

**Critical insight**: Workers do not need a load balancer. Redis acts as a natural work distributor. Workers compete to pull jobs — whoever is free grabs the next token. This is called a **pull-based queue** and it is self-balancing by design.

---

## PART 3 — The Full Architecture With Both Load Balancers

```
                     INTERNET
                        │
                        │ HTTPS 443
                        ▼
            ┌───────────────────────┐
            │   ALB #1 (Platform)   │
            │   edwisely.com        │
            │   2 target servers    │
            └───────────┬───────────┘
                        │ Round-robin
              ┌─────────┴─────────┐
              ▼                   ▼
        ┌──────────┐        ┌──────────┐
        │Platform  │        │Platform  │
        │Server A  │        │Server B  │
        │Node.js   │        │Node.js   │
        └────┬─────┘        └────┬─────┘
             │                   │
             └─────────┬─────────┘
                       │ POST /submissions
                       ▼
            ┌──────────────────────┐
            │   ALB #2 (Judge0)    │
            │   judge0.internal    │
            │   1-2 target servers │
            └──────────┬───────────┘
                       │
              ┌────────┴────────┐
              ▼                 ▼
        ┌──────────┐      ┌──────────┐
        │Judge0    │      │Judge0    │
        │Server A  │      │Server B  │
        │Puma      │      │Puma      │
        │(primary) │      │(standby) │
        └────┬─────┘      └──────────┘
             │
             ├── writes to PostgreSQL (RDS)
             │
             └── RPUSH to Redis ──────────────────────────────┐
                                                               │
                                           ┌───────────────────▼──────────────────┐
                                           │          REDIS QUEUE                  │
                                           │   "judge0:queue": [token1, token2...] │
                                           └───┬───────┬───────┬──────────────────┘
                                               │ BLPOP │ BLPOP │ BLPOP   (47 workers competing)
                                        ┌──────▼─┐ ┌───▼──┐ ┌──▼─────┐
                                        │Worker 1│ │Wrkr 2│ │Wrkr 47 │
                                        │node-1  │ │node-1│ │node-9  │
                                        └────────┘ └──────┘ └────────┘
```

## PART 4 — NO LOAD SCENARIO

### The Restaurant at 3 AM — Nobody Is Here

It is 3:00 AM. No exam is running. Students are sleeping. Here is the exact state of every component:

### ALB #1 (Platform Load Balancer)

```
ALB #1 state at 3 AM:
  Incoming connections:     ~0 per minute
  Active target servers:    2 (Platform Server A, Platform Server B)
  Health check status:      Both HEALTHY
  Health check runs:        Every 30 seconds (GET /health → 200 OK)
  Cost running:             $0.0168/hr (base fee, always on)
  
  Target Group status:
    Platform Server A  10.0.1.10:3000  ← HEALTHY ✓
    Platform Server B  10.0.1.11:3000  ← HEALTHY ✓
```

The ALB is fully running but handling near-zero traffic. It sends a health check ping to each server every 30 seconds just to confirm they are alive. No real traffic.

### ALB #2 (Judge0 API Load Balancer)

```
ALB #2 state at 3 AM:
  Incoming connections:     0 per minute
  Active target servers:    1 (Judge0 Server A — primary only)
  Health check:             GET /system_info → 200 OK every 30s
  
  Target Group:
    Judge0 Server A  10.0.2.10:2358  ← HEALTHY ✓
    Judge0 Server B  10.0.2.11:2358  ← STANDBY (registered but weight=0)
```

### Redis Queue

```
Redis state at 3 AM:
  Queue depth:    0  (empty)
  Memory used:   ~50MB (base Redis overhead)
  Connected clients: 6 (one per idle worker)
  
  Workers all blocked on BLPOP with 5s timeout:
    BLPOP "judge0:queue" 5
    → waits 5 seconds
    → nothing in queue
    → retries BLPOP
    → waits 5 seconds
    → nothing
    → ... (repeating forever, using ~0 CPU)
```

### Workers (Auto Scaling — Minimum)

```
Workers at 3 AM:
  Running worker tasks:   6  (minimum baseline, ECS never goes below this)
  Distribution:
    Node 1: 2 worker containers  (using ~200MB RAM total, ~0% CPU)
    Node 2: 2 worker containers
    Node 3: 2 worker containers
  
  Each worker container:
    Status: RUNNING
    CPU:    ~0.1%  (just sitting, waiting on BLPOP)
    RAM:    ~200MB (Judge0 worker process overhead)
    Action: BLPOP on Redis, timeout, BLPOP again
```

### CloudWatch Alarms

```
Alarm: redis_queue_depth_high
  Metric:    RedisQueueDepth = 0
  Threshold: > 100 for 2 minutes
  State:     OK  (not triggered)

Alarm: redis_queue_depth_low
  Metric:    RedisQueueDepth = 0
  Threshold: < 5 for 5 minutes
  State:     ALARM  (triggered — scale IN already at minimum)
  Action:    None (already at minimum 6 workers)
```

### Cost at 3 AM (No Load)

```
ALB #1 + #2:                    $0.034/hr  ($25/month)
Platform Server A + B:           $0.340/hr  (2× c5.xlarge)
Judge0 Server A:                 $0.170/hr  (c5.xlarge)
3 worker nodes (baseline only):  $0.204/hr  (3× c5.xlarge spot)
RDS PostgreSQL:                  $0.240/hr
ElastiCache Redis:               $0.130/hr
─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Total at 3 AM:                   $1.118/hr  (~$26/day idle)
```

## PART 5 — HIGH LOAD SCENARIO

### Exam Day — 10:00 AM Sharp

The exam opens. 1,000 students click Submit within the first 60 seconds.

### What Happens in the First 10 Seconds

```
t=0s:   Exam opens. First student submits.
t=1s:   47 more students submit.
t=2s:   89 more students submit.
...
t=10s:  ~200 submissions received so far.
```

### ALB #1 — Absorbing the Student Wave

```
t=0 to t=60s:

Incoming connections per second:  ~16 (1,000 students / 60s spread)
Peak simultaneous connections:    ~200

ALB #1 behavior:
  Student A → Platform Server A  (least connections routing)
  Student B → Platform Server B
  Student C → Platform Server A  (A now has fewer active connections)
  Student D → Platform Server B
  ... round-robin with connection count awareness

Each Platform Server:
  Receives ~100 req/sec at peak
  Node.js event loop handles this fine (async, non-blocking)
  Calls autograder.grade_async() for each submission
  Returns immediately (async — not waiting for Judge0)
```

### ALB #2 — The Submission Wave Hits Judge0

```
t=0 to t=60s:

Platform servers are calling Judge0 API for each submission:
  1,000 POST /submissions in 60 seconds = ~16 POST/sec average
  Peak burst: ~50 POST/sec in the first 10 seconds

ALB #2 routes:
  POST #1   → Judge0 Server A  (Puma thread #1 picks up)
  POST #2   → Judge0 Server A  (Puma thread #2 picks up)
  ...
  POST #100 → Judge0 Server A  (100 Puma threads all busy)
  POST #101 → Judge0 Server B  (ALB detects Server A is saturated)
              ↑ This is the KEY moment — ALB #2 starts using standby

ALB #2 routing decision is based on:
  Active connections on Server A: 100 (all Puma threads busy)
  Active connections on Server B: 0
  Decision: route to Server B
```

### Redis Queue Depth Explodes

```
t=0s:    Queue depth = 0
t=10s:   Queue depth = 47   (50 submissions arrived, 3 already picked up)
t=30s:   Queue depth = 189
t=60s:   Queue depth = 947  ← 1,000 submitted, 53 already being processed
t=62s:   Queue depth crosses 100 threshold
```

### CloudWatch Alarm Fires

```
t=62s:
  Lambda function runs (every 60s): measures queue depth = 189
  Pushes metric to CloudWatch: RedisQueueDepth = 189

  CloudWatch Alarm: redis_queue_depth_high
    Threshold: > 100 for 2 consecutive data points (2 minutes)
    Current:   189 > 100  ← first breach

t=122s:
  Lambda runs again: queue depth = 841 (still growing)
  CloudWatch: RedisQueueDepth = 841

  Alarm state: ALARM  ← 2 consecutive breaches confirmed
  Action: notify ECS Auto Scaling

t=122s + 15s:
  ECS Application Auto Scaling receives trigger
  Current desired count: 6 workers
  Scaling policy: add 12 workers per alarm breach
  New desired count: 18 workers
  ECS starts launching 12 new worker tasks
```

### ECS Scales Workers Up

```
t=137s:
  ECS scheduler: "I need 12 new worker tasks"
  
  Checks available capacity on existing nodes:
    Node 1: 16 vCPU, using 4 (2 workers × 2 vCPU) → 12 vCPU free → can fit 6 more workers
    Node 2: 16 vCPU, using 4 → can fit 6 more
  
  Starts 6 tasks on Node 1, 6 tasks on Node 2.
  
  Each new task: Docker pull (image already cached → fast), start container, BLPOP

t=155s:
  12 new workers are RUNNING.
  Total workers: 6 (old) + 12 (new) = 18 workers
  Sandboxes available: 18 × 2 = 36
  
  Alarm fires again (queue still at 700):
  New desired count: 18 + 12 = 30 workers
  ECS needs 3 new EC2 nodes (Node 4, 5, 6)
  
  Auto Scaling Group: launches 3 new c5.4xlarge EC2 instances
    → EC2 boot time: ~90 seconds (Ubuntu + ECS Agent startup)
    → Docker pull judge0 image: ~30 seconds (first time on new node)
    → Worker containers start: ~10 seconds
```

### The Full Scale-Up Timeline

```
t=0s      Exam opens, submissions start
t=62s     CloudWatch first breach (queue > 100)
t=122s    CloudWatch alarm confirmed → ECS scaling triggered
t=155s    First 12 new workers online (from existing nodes)
t=180s    Second 12 new workers triggered (queue still high)
t=270s    New EC2 nodes booted, workers online → total 54 workers
t=300s    Queue draining rapidly (54 workers × 2 runners = 108 sandboxes)
t=598s    Queue empty (47 workers drain 1,000 submissions in ~5 min)
```

### During Peak Load — What Each Component Does

```
ALB #1 (Platform LB):
  Handling: ~16 req/sec sustained
  Distribution: 50/50 between Platform Server A and B
  Health checks: continuous (every 30s)
  No scaling needed (Node.js handles this load easily)

ALB #2 (Judge0 LB):
  Handling: 1,000 POST /submissions over 60s = ~16 POST/sec
  Distribution: split between Judge0 Server A (primary) and B (overflow)
  New connections after submission: callbacks returning results
  Deregistration delay: 30s (lets in-flight requests complete)

Redis Queue:
  Peak depth:    ~950 tokens
  Memory used:   ~50MB + (950 tokens × ~2KB) = ~52MB
  maxmemory:     2GB → safe
  noeviction:    ON → rejects new if full (but 950 << 10,000 max)
  Write rate:    ~16 RPUSH/sec (submissions arriving)
  Read rate:     108 BLPOP/sec (108 sandboxes consuming)

Workers (54 active):
  Each worker container:
    CPU:   60-80% (sandboxes actively forking/executing)
    RAM:   ~762MB (2 sandboxes × 250 TCs × ~1.5MB per sandbox)
    State: BLPOP → get token → fetch from DB → run isolate → write result → BLPOP

RDS PostgreSQL:
  Write rate:    ~16 INSERTs/sec (new submissions)
               + 108 UPDATEs/sec (workers writing results)
               = ~124 writes/sec  
  Read rate:     ~108 SELECTs/sec (workers fetching submission details)
  Connection count: 100 (Puma) + 54×5 (workers) + 20 headroom = 390
  max_connections=500 → safe ✓
```

---

## PART 6 — HEALTH CHECKS: The Quality Inspector

Load balancers constantly inspect their targets. This is the most important feature for reliability.

### How ALB Health Checks Work

```
ALB #2 health check configuration:
  Path:                GET /system_info
  Port:                2358
  Protocol:            HTTP
  Interval:            30 seconds  (check every 30s)
  Timeout:             5 seconds   (if no response in 5s → fail)
  Healthy threshold:   2           (2 consecutive passes → HEALTHY)
  Unhealthy threshold: 3           (3 consecutive fails → UNHEALTHY)
```

### Scenario: Judge0 Server A Crashes Under Load

```
t=0s:    Server A is HEALTHY, handling 50% of submissions

t=30s:   ALB sends health check: GET /system_info to Server A
         Server A: no response (process crashed)
         ALB records: FAIL #1

t=60s:   ALB sends health check again
         Server A: still no response
         ALB records: FAIL #2

t=90s:   ALB sends health check again
         Server A: still no response
         ALB records: FAIL #3 → UNHEALTHY threshold reached

t=90s:   ALB action:
           Server A status → UNHEALTHY
           Remove Server A from target group
           ALL new connections → Server B only
           In-flight requests on Server A: drained (30s deregistration delay)
           
t=91s:   Students never notice. Server B absorbs all traffic.
         100 Puma threads on Server B now handling full load.
```

### What "Deregistration Delay" Means

```
When a target becomes UNHEALTHY or you scale in:

WITHOUT deregistration delay:
  ALB stops sending new connections → existing connections cut immediately
  Student mid-submission gets: Connection Reset ✗

WITH deregistration delay (30 seconds):
  ALB stops sending NEW connections to unhealthy target
  EXISTING connections allowed to complete for 30 seconds
  After 30 seconds: all connections force-closed
  
For Judge0 submissions (POST takes ~8ms):
  30 seconds is MORE than enough for all in-flight POSTs to complete ✓
```

---

## PART 7 — SCALE DOWN: The Restaurant Closing Up

```
t=598s:  Queue empty. All 1,000 results delivered.
t=600s:  Lambda measures queue depth = 0
t=660s:  Lambda measures queue depth = 0 again
         
         CloudWatch Alarm: redis_queue_depth_low
           Threshold: < 5 for 5 consecutive minutes (300s)
           State: needs 5 more minutes of low queue before scaling in

t=900s:  5 minutes of empty queue confirmed
         ECS Auto Scaling: scale IN
         Current workers: 54
         Target: 6 (minimum baseline)
         Remove 48 worker tasks
         
         ECS scale-in process:
           ① Mark 48 worker tasks for termination
           ② Each marked worker: finish current job (if any), then stop
           ③ BLPOP returns nothing → worker gracefully stops
           ④ Container exits cleanly
           ⑤ ECS deregisters the task
           
t=910s:  48 workers terminated
         6 workers remain (minimum baseline)
         
         Auto Scaling Group: terminate 6 EC2 nodes
           ① ASG marks nodes for termination  
           ② Waits for ECS to drain tasks (connection draining)
           ③ EC2 instances terminate
           ④ You stop paying for them
           
t=1,000s: Back to idle state
          6 workers running
          3 EC2 nodes (down from 9)
          Cost: back to $1.118/hr
```

## PART 9 — What the Load Balancer Cannot Fix

```
PROBLEM                          LB HANDLES?   WHAT ACTUALLY HANDLES IT
─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Server A crashes                 YES ✓         ALB routes to Server B
Worker node dies                 N/A           ECS reschedules on other nodes
Queue overflows (> 10,000)       NO ✗          Redis noeviction rejects
Bad student code hangs sandbox   NO ✗          SIGALRM + Judge0 wall_time_limit
1 submission takes 33 min (F-03) NO ✗          Fix F-03 (RLIMIT_NPROC)
select() corrupts > 1,024 TCs    NO ✗          Fix F-01 (poll)
Callback URL unreachable         NO ✗          autograder timeout + retry
Database goes down               NO ✗          RDS Multi-AZ auto-failover
```

**The load balancer handles traffic distribution and server failure. It does not fix code bugs or queue depth. Those are the F-01 through F-19 fixes.**

=== USER ===
is it good to serve 1000submissions at once if not how can the approach should be.

=== USER ===
This session is being continued from a previous conversation that ran out of context. The summary below covers the earlier portion of the conversation.

Summary:
1. Primary Request and Intent:
The user is building an online coding assessment platform using Judge0 for sandboxed code execution. The primary goal has been a comprehensive failure analysis and production architecture design for handling 1,000 concurrent student submissions × 1,000 test cases each. The conversation covers failure analysis, restaurant-style explanations of every technical concept, AWS production architecture options (single node, Docker Swarm, ECS), cost analysis, worker sizing, and load balancer behavior. The most recent question is whether serving all 1,000 submissions simultaneously is a good approach, and what the alternative strategy should be.

2. Key Technical Concepts:
- **Judge0** — Docker-based code execution engine with isolate sandbox
- **Single harness model** — 1 submission = 1 Judge0 job (all TCs parallel inside one container), NOT 1,000,000 jobs
- **RLIMIT_NPROC** — per-UID Linux process limit (NOT per-container, NOT student ID)
- **select() vs poll()** — FD_SETSIZE=1024 hard limit in select() breaks C/C++ harnesses with >511 TCs
- **MAX_RUNNERS** — simultaneous isolate sandboxes per worker replica (keep at 2)
- **MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER** — isolate's per-sandbox process cap (default 64 → need 1100)
- **SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT** — Judge0 harness size cap (default 64KB → need 512KB)
- **F-19 batching** — MAX_PARALLEL_TCS=250, reduces T from 7s to 28s but reduces memory from 3GB to 762MB per sandbox
- **Drain time formula** — (1,000 × T) / (N × 2) where N=worker replicas
- **ALB** — Application Load Balancer for HTTP traffic distribution
- **Redis as pull-based queue** — natural load distributor for workers (no LB needed for workers)
- **ECS Auto Scaling** — scale workers based on CloudWatch custom metric (Redis queue depth)
- **cgroup v1 vs v2** — Ubuntu 20.04 has native v1 (remove cgroupv1-init), Ubuntu 22.04 needs it
- **Docker Swarm vs ECS** — Swarm = minimal migration from compose; ECS = fully managed, auto-scaling
- **CallbackServer** — webhook-based result delivery (vs polling) eliminates poll storm
- **Deregistration delay** — 30s window for in-flight requests to complete before target removal
- **UID** — Linux User ID (completely separate from application student ID)
- **isolate** — Judge0's sandbox using Linux namespaces + cgroups

3. Files and Code Sections:
- **autograder.py** — Main entry point. `Autograder.grade()` orchestrates: HarnessBuilder → SecurityChecker → sanitize → Judge0Client.submit_and_wait() → parse_judge0_response(). `Submission` dataclass holds student_id, language, student_code, test_cases, per_tc_limit_s=2, memory_limit_mb=256. `GradingResult` holds parsed results + judge0_raw.

- **core/judge0_client.py** — `Judge0Client.submit_and_wait()`: calculates global_limit_s = per_tc_limit_s + 5 (parallel, not sequential), base64 encodes harness, POST with timeout=120s, returns token. `CallbackServer` uses ThreadingHTTPServer (needs aiohttp replacement for 1,000 concurrent webhooks). `_poll()` checks status BEFORE sleeping (FIX-7). `_get_with_retry()` uses exponential backoff 0.5/1/2s on 5xx/timeout. `Judge0Config`: poll_interval_s=0.5, max_polls=60.

- **core/harness_builder.py** — `HarnessBuilder.__init__()`: generates session_id (12-char hex UUID), delim="@@TC_RESULT__{session_id}__". `_build_python()`: uses sentinel \x00STUDENT_SOURCE_RAW\x00 to avoid .format() brace issues. `_build_c_parallel_runner()` / `_build_cpp_parallel_runner()`: currently generates select() code — BROKEN for >511 TCs. `_build_java_parallel_runner()`: uses Thread.join(Math.max(1, remaining)) — FIX-3.

- **core/output_parser.py** — `OutputParser.parse()`: checks for DONE marker (absence → global_tle=True), regex extracts TC blocks, marks missing TCs as TLE (if global_tle) or MISSING. `parse_judge0_response()`: handles Judge0-level failures first (TLE → all TLE, CompileError → all ERROR).

- **security/security.py** — `SecurityChecker.check()`: checks delimiter injection, Python AST analysis (blocks 20+ modules, 8 builtins, dunders), C/C++/Java regex patterns. MAX_CODE_LENGTH=10,000 chars.

- **docker-compose.yml** — Current config: cgroupv1-init service (remove on Ubuntu 20.04), server with WEB_CONCURRENCY=4/RAILS_MAX_THREADS=20, workers (no ulimits set — needs nofile/nproc=65536), db postgres max_connections=200, redis maxmemory=512mb. Missing: ulimits, mem_limit, SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT, MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER.

- **judge0.conf** — Current: MAX_QUEUE_SIZE=10000, MAX_RUNNERS=2. Missing: SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT=524288, MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER=1100, ALLOW_ENABLE_NETWORK=false, STDIN_SIZE_LIMIT=10485760.

4. Errors and Fixes:
- **F-01 (P0)**: select() FD_SETSIZE=1024 breaks C/C++ harnesses with >511 TCs. Fix: replace select() with poll() in harness_builder.py _build_c_parallel_runner() and _build_cpp_parallel_runner().
- **F-02 (P0)**: SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT not set → 64KB default truncates 1,000-TC harnesses. Fix: add SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT=524288 to judge0.conf + pre-flight size check in judge0_client.py.
- **F-03 (P0)**: isolate default MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER=64 triggers sequential fallback (33 minutes). Fix: MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER=1100 in judge0.conf + ulimits nproc=65536 in docker-compose.yml.
- **F-04 (P0)**: 6 sandboxes drain 1,000 submissions in 19.4 minutes. Fix: scale to 47+ worker replicas.
- **F-05 (P1)**: No ulimits nofile set → default 1,024 FD limit → pipe() fails at TC 512. Fix: ulimits nofile=65536.
- **F-06 (P1)**: No mem_limit on workers → OOM killer kills entire worker process. Fix: mem_limit=4g per worker container.
- **F-09 (P1)**: ThreadingHTTPServer creates 1 thread per webhook → 1,000 threads = 1GB stack RAM. Fix: replace with aiohttp async server.
- **F-10 (P1)**: Polling mode creates 2,000 req/sec against 80-connection Puma. Fix: use callback mode.
- **_can_fork_n() bug**: Only counts current process threads, not all UID processes across containers → incorrect available slot count. Fix: scan /proc for all UID-owned processes.

5. Problem Solving:
- **Single harness insight**: The architecture already uses 1 Judge0 job per submission (not 1M), dramatically reducing queue depth and DB load. Analysis adjusted accordingly.
- **Drain time formula**: (1,000 × T) / (N × 2) — used to calculate exact worker counts for different latency targets.
- **F-19 tradeoff**: Batching 250 TCs reduces memory from 3GB to 762MB per sandbox but increases T from 7s to 28s. For 5-min target: 12 workers (no F-19, 72GB RAM needed) vs 47 workers (F-19, 72GB RAM) — similar memory, different worker counts.
- **Multi-node requires orchestration**: docker-compose up --scale only works on single host. Multi-node needs Docker Swarm (minimal migration) or ECS (fully managed, auto-scaling, higher complexity).
- **ECS for exam workloads**: Exam-day burst pattern (idle most of day, spike at exam open) is exactly the ECS auto-scaling use case. Cost: ~$1,168/month vs $2,978/month for single always-on node.
