## Should You Serve 1,000 Submissions Simultaneously?

**Short answer: No. And here's exactly why — and what to do instead.**

---

## The Restaurant Analogy First

Imagine a restaurant that seats 50 people. At 9:00 AM an exam opens, and **1,000 students all click "Submit" within the first 60 seconds.**

**Option A — Accept all 1,000 instantly:**
- Host says "yes!" to everyone
- 950 people are standing in the lobby with no chairs
- Kitchen has no idea how many plates to cook
- People wait 45 minutes with no feedback
- Half of them leave (refresh, re-submit, duplicate jobs)

**Option B — Controlled acceptance:**
- Host says "we have 50 seats, next 50 come in, everyone else gets a numbered ticket"
- Queue position shown on screen: "You are #312, estimated wait: 8 minutes"
- Kitchen pre-warmed before doors open
- Everyone gets served; no chaos

---

## What Actually Happens If You Accept 1,000 At Once

### Phase 1: The Submission Spike (T=0 to T+10s)

```
1,000 HTTP POST /grade arrive simultaneously
         ↓
Your API server (FastAPI/Flask/Django)
  → 1,000 tasks queued in Redis instantly
  → Redis queue depth: 1,000
         ↓
ECS Auto Scaling reads CloudWatch metric
  → "queue depth > threshold, scale up workers"
  → ECS issues RunTask for 45 new worker containers
         ↓
           ← 2-3 MINUTE COLD START LAG →
```

### Phase 2: The Uneven Experience

```
T=0:    1,000 submissions queued
T=7s:   First 6 sandboxes (3 existing workers × 2) start processing
T=0→2m: 6 sandboxes × 7s each = ~84 submissions done  ← fast students
T=2m:   New workers spin up, 47 sandboxes now running
T=2→9m: Remaining 916 submissions processed             ← slow students
```

**Student 1 gets result in 8 seconds.**
**Student 800 waits 9 minutes.**
**Same exam. Same submission. Massively different experience.**

### Phase 3: The Duplicate Submission Bomb

```
Students waiting 3+ minutes with no feedback:
  → 30% hit refresh or re-submit
  → Queue grows from 1,000 → 1,300
  → Your system is now grading the same code twice
  → Results come back inconsistent
  → Students and professors confused
```

---

## The Recommended Approach: Controlled Admission with Backpressure

### Architecture Overview

```
Student Browser
     │ POST /submit
     ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                 API Gateway / ALB                    │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
     │
     ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│              Submission Controller                   │
│                                                      │
│  1. Check queue depth (Redis LLEN)                   │
│  2. If depth < MAX_QUEUE → accept, return ticket_id  │
│  3. If depth ≥ MAX_QUEUE → return 429 + retry_after  │
│  4. Deduplicate: reject if same student_id already   │
│     has a pending job for this exam_id               │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
     │ accepted                    │ rejected (429)
     ▼                             ▼
Redis Queue                  Student sees:
  LPUSH jobs                 "Server busy. Your
  SET ticket:xyz pending     submission will be
                             retried in 15s."
     │
     ▼
Workers pull jobs
(BLPOP — blocking pull)
     │
     ▼
Judge0 → Result
     │
     ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│           Result Store (Redis/DB)                    │
│  SET result:{ticket_id} → parsed JSON               │
│  SET status:{student_id}:{exam_id} → DONE           │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
     │
     ▼
Student polls GET /result/{ticket_id}
  → Pending: returns position + ETA
  → Done:    returns score + per-TC breakdown
```

---

## Strategy 1: Queue Depth Gate (Most Important)

```python
MAX_QUEUE_DEPTH = 500  # never let Redis hold more than this

def accept_submission(student_id, exam_id, code):
    
    # Dedup: already submitted?
    if redis.exists(f"pending:{student_id}:{exam_id}"):
        ticket_id = redis.get(f"pending:{student_id}:{exam_id}")
        return {"status": "already_queued", "ticket_id": ticket_id}
    
    # Backpressure gate
    queue_depth = redis.llen("judge0:jobs")
    if queue_depth >= MAX_QUEUE_DEPTH:
        wait_seconds = estimate_wait(queue_depth)
        return {
            "status":      "busy",
            "retry_after": wait_seconds,
            "message":     f"Queue full. Try again in {wait_seconds}s"
        }
    
    # Accept
    ticket_id = uuid4().hex
    redis.lpush("judge0:jobs", json.dumps({
        "ticket_id":  ticket_id,
        "student_id": student_id,
        "exam_id":    exam_id,
        "code":       code,
        "submitted_at": time.time()
    }))
    redis.setex(f"pending:{student_id}:{exam_id}", 600, ticket_id)
    
    return {"status": "queued", "ticket_id": ticket_id}


def estimate_wait(queue_depth):
    # 47 sandboxes × 7s per job = 329 jobs/min throughput
    # queue_depth jobs ahead / 329 jobs/min
    throughput_per_second = 47 * 2 / 7   # ~13.4 jobs/sec
    return int(queue_depth / throughput_per_second)
```

---

## Strategy 2: Client-Side Retry with Exponential Backoff

The student's browser should NOT spam /submit on 429. Instead:

```javascript
async function submitWithRetry(code, maxRetries = 5) {
    for (let attempt = 0; attempt < maxRetries; attempt++) {
        const response = await fetch('/api/submit', {
            method: 'POST',
            body: JSON.stringify({ code })
        });
        
        if (response.status === 200) {
            const { ticket_id } = await response.json();
            showStatus("Submitted! Checking results...");
            return pollForResult(ticket_id);
        }
        
        if (response.status === 429) {
            const { retry_after } = await response.json();
            showStatus(`Server busy. Retrying in ${retry_after}s... (attempt ${attempt+1})`);
            await sleep(retry_after * 1000);
            continue;
        }
        
        throw new Error(`Submission failed: ${response.status}`);
    }
    showStatus("Unable to submit. Please try again.");
}
```

---

## Strategy 3: Pre-Warming Workers Before Exam Opens

This is the **single biggest lever** you have. Cold start lag is eliminated entirely.

```
Exam scheduled for 10:00 AM
          │
          ▼
9:55 AM: Exam admin clicks "Arm exam"
          │
          ▼
Your system calls ECS:
  ecs.update_service(
    cluster='judge0-prod',
    service='judge0-workers',
    desired_count=47   # full exam capacity
  )
          │
     2-3 min scale-up
          │
          ▼
9:58 AM: All 47 workers running, idle, waiting for jobs
          │
          ▼
10:00 AM: Exam opens
  → First submission hits a warm worker in 0.3s
  → No scale-up lag
  → First 94 submissions processed in 7s
```

```python
# In your exam management API
def arm_exam(exam_id: str, expected_students: int):
    # Calculate needed workers
    # T=7s, MAX_RUNNERS=2, target drain < 5min
    needed_workers = math.ceil(
        (expected_students * 7) / (5 * 60 * 2)
    )  # = 29 for 1,000 students at 5-min target
    
    ecs_client.update_service(
        cluster='judge0-prod',
        service='judge0-workers',
        desiredCount=needed_workers
    )
    
    redis.set(f"exam:{exam_id}:armed", "true", ex=3600)
    return {"workers_started": needed_workers, "ready_in": "2-3 minutes"}
```

---

## Strategy 4: Per-Student Submission Throttle

Even with all of the above, you want a hard limit: one pending job per student per exam.

```python
def check_student_throttle(student_id, exam_id):
    key = f"submitted:{student_id}:{exam_id}"
    
    # SET NX = only set if not exists (atomic)
    acquired = redis.set(key, "1", nx=True, ex=600)
    if not acquired:
        return False, "You already have a submission in progress for this exam"
    return True, None
```

This prevents:
- Students spamming Submit button 10 times
- Refresh creating duplicate jobs
- Network retry creating duplicate jobs

---

## The Full Flow: 1,000 Students, Exam Opens at 10:00 AM

```
9:55 AM  → Admin arms exam: 47 workers start
9:58 AM  → All workers warm and idle
10:00 AM → Exam opens

10:00:00 → 200 fastest students submit
           → 200 jobs in queue
           → 94 sandboxes start immediately (47 workers × 2)
           → Queue depth: 106

10:00:07 → First 94 results done
           → 94 more start
           → Results page shows scores for first students

10:00:10 → Next 300 students submit
           → Queue depth: ~250
           → Students see: "Position 180, ETA ~20s"

10:00:30 → Next 300 submit
           → Queue depth ~400
           → Throttle gate stays open (< 500 limit)

10:01:00 → Final 200 submit
           → Queue depth ~350 (drain keeping up)
           → All submissions accepted

10:04:30 → All 1,000 results delivered
           → Peak queue depth never exceeded 450
           → No duplicate jobs
           → All students had ETA feedback throughout
```

## Summary: What To Build

| Component | What It Does | Priority |
|-----------|-------------|----------|
| Queue depth gate (MAX=500) | Rejects with 429 when overloaded | P0 |
| Per-student dedup lock | Prevents duplicate jobs | P0 |
| Pre-warm API | Starts workers 5min before exam | P0 |
| Client retry with backoff | Handles 429 gracefully in browser | P1 |
| Position + ETA endpoint | Reduces student anxiety during wait | P1 |
| ECS scale-down after exam | Shuts idle workers down to save cost | P2 |

The pre-warm + queue gate combination means students never see "server busy" during a normal exam, and workers are never idle during the wait. That's the target state.

=== ASSISTANT ===
## Docker vs Kubernetes for Judge0 — The Full Picture

---

## Restaurant Analogy First

**Docker Compose** = You own one restaurant, you manage everything manually.

**Docker Swarm** = You open 3 branches of the same restaurant. Same recipe book, same training manual. You manage all 3 yourself.

**Kubernetes** = You hire a **restaurant management company**. They handle all your branches, auto-open new ones when the queue gets long, auto-close them at night, monitor every chef. But you pay their management fee, and **their rulebook is 800 pages thick.**

**ECS** = Amazon runs the management company for you. Fewer features than Kubernetes, but they handle the infrastructure. Rulebook is 200 pages.

---

## The Critical Problem: Judge0 Needs Privileged Containers

This is the **most important technical constraint** — and it changes everything.

Judge0's `isolate` sandbox works by:
1. Creating Linux namespaces (`unshare`)
2. Setting up cgroups (`/sys/fs/cgroup`)
3. Running as root inside the sandbox
4. Using `mount`, `chroot`, `setrlimit` syscalls

```
isolate --box-id=0 --mem=262144 --processes=64 --run -- /usr/bin/python3 solution.py
        ↑
   Needs SYS_ADMIN + CAP_NET_ADMIN + cgroup access
   = "--privileged" in Docker terms
```

### What This Means For Each Platform:

```
Docker Compose:  docker run --privileged         ✅ Works perfectly
Docker Swarm:    --cap-add SYS_ADMIN in compose  ✅ Works
ECS (EC2):       task definition + privileged:true ✅ Works on EC2 launch type
ECS (Fargate):   ❌ BLOCKED — Fargate forbids privileged containers entirely
EKS (EC2 nodes): securityContext.privileged:true  ⚠️  Works but complex
EKS (Fargate):   ❌ BLOCKED — same as ECS Fargate
GKE Autopilot:   ❌ BLOCKED
GKE Standard:    ⚠️  Works but requires node pool config
```

**If you use Kubernetes Fargate nodes or any serverless K8s: Judge0 breaks completely.**

---

## Side-by-Side Comparison

| Feature | Docker Compose | Docker Swarm | ECS (EC2) | Kubernetes (EKS) |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Single-node only | ✅ Yes | ❌ Multi-node | ❌ Multi-node | ❌ Multi-node |
| Judge0 compatibility | ✅ Perfect | ✅ Good | ✅ Good | ⚠️ Requires extra config |
| Auto-scaling workers | ❌ Manual | ❌ Manual | ✅ Target tracking | ✅ KEDA (excellent) |
| Pre-warm before exam | ❌ Manual SSH | ❌ CLI command | ✅ API call | ✅ kubectl/API |
| Control plane cost | $0 | $0 | $0 | **+$73/month (EKS)** |
| Learning curve | Low | Medium | Medium | **High** |
| Self-healing (crashed worker) | ❌ No | ✅ Basic | ✅ Yes | ✅ Yes |
| Rolling deploys | ❌ Downtime | ✅ Basic | ✅ Yes | ✅ Best in class |
| Operational complexity | 1/10 | 4/10 | 6/10 | **9/10** |
| Team size needed | 1 devops | 1-2 devops | 1-2 devops | 2-3 devops min |

---

## What Kubernetes Gives You (That ECS Doesn't)

### KEDA — Kubernetes Event-Driven Autoscaling
This is genuinely excellent for your exam workload:

```yaml
# Scale workers based on Redis queue depth
apiVersion: keda.sh/v1alpha1
kind: ScaledObject
metadata:
  name: judge0-worker-scaler
spec:
  scaleTargetRef:
    name: judge0-workers
  minReplicaCount: 2       # always-warm baseline
  maxReplicaCount: 50      # burst capacity
  triggers:
  - type: redis
    metadata:
      address: redis:6379
      listName: judge0:jobs
      listLength: "10"     # scale up when queue > 10 per pod
```

**Result:** Redis queue grows → KEDA automatically adds workers → queue drains → workers scale back down. Fully automatic, no CloudWatch metrics to configure.

### Everything Else Kubernetes Gives You
- **Namespace isolation** — run staging + prod on same cluster
- **Helm charts** — package your entire Judge0 stack as one deployable unit
- **NetworkPolicy** — restrict which pods can talk to each other
- **Pod Disruption Budgets** — guarantee N workers always running during node maintenance
- **Service mesh (Istio)** — mTLS between services, distributed tracing

**But for your current use case (Judge0 autograder):** you don't need 80% of this.

---

## What Kubernetes Costs You

### Complexity Tax on Judge0 Specifically

This is the docker-compose config for worker ulimits:
```yaml
# docker-compose.yml — simple
workers:
  ulimits:
    nofile: 65536
    nproc:  65536
```

This is the Kubernetes equivalent:
```yaml
# k8s — much more involved
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: DaemonSet          # needs DaemonSet to modify node-level ulimits
metadata:
  name: ulimit-init
spec:
  template:
    spec:
      initContainers:
      - name: set-ulimits
        image: busybox
        command: ['sh', '-c', 'ulimit -n 65536 && ulimit -u 65536']
        securityContext:
          privileged: true
      hostPID: true
---
# ALSO need this in worker pod spec:
securityContext:
  privileged: true        # for isolate
  capabilities:
    add: ["SYS_ADMIN", "SYS_PTRACE", "NET_ADMIN"]
```

### The cgroup Mount (Judge0 Specific Problem)
```yaml
# Every worker pod needs this volume mount
volumes:
- name: cgroup
  hostPath:
    path: /sys/fs/cgroup
    type: Directory
volumeMounts:
- name: cgroup
  mountPath: /sys/fs/cgroup
```

If you're on Ubuntu 22.04 (cgroup v2), you also need a DaemonSet to enable cgroup v1 compatibility on every node before Judge0 starts. This is the same cgroupv1-init problem you already solved in docker-compose — but now it's a DaemonSet that must run before any worker pod.

## The Honest Recommendation

### Your Situation
- **Team:** Likely 1-3 engineers
- **Primary service:** Judge0 autograder (not 50 microservices)
- **Traffic pattern:** Burst on exam days, idle rest of time
- **Current stack:** Docker Compose, works correctly

### Decision Tree

```
Do you already have Kubernetes expertise on your team?
├── YES → EKS with EC2 node groups. Worth the complexity.
│         Use KEDA for Redis-based worker scaling.
│         Budget $73/month control plane + node costs.
│
└── NO → Do you need multi-node (>1 server)?
         ├── YES → ECS with EC2 launch type
         │         No control plane cost
         │         Auto-scaling via CloudWatch
         │         Privileged containers work fine
         │
         └── NO → Single powerful node (c5.4xlarge/c5.9xlarge)
                  Docker Compose + manual pre-warm
                  Simplest possible, zero orchestration cost
                  Fine up to ~500 concurrent submissions
```

### My Recommendation: **ECS over Kubernetes for your use case**

| Reason | Detail |
|--------|--------|
| No K8s expertise tax | ECS has ~20% the learning curve |
| No control plane cost | ECS = $0/month overhead. EKS = +$73/month |
| Judge0 privileged containers | Both work on EC2; neither works on Fargate |
| Auto-scaling | ECS target tracking handles exam burst well |
| Pre-warm API | `ecs.update_service(desiredCount=47)` — one API call |
| Migration path | Docker Compose → ECS is straightforward (ECS Compose CLI exists) |

**Go Kubernetes if:** You plan to run 10+ other services on the same cluster and already have K8s people. The operational overhead amortizes across many services.

**Stick with ECS if:** Judge0 is your primary or only container workload.

## If You Do Choose Kubernetes — The Exact Config

```yaml
# judge0-worker-deployment.yaml
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: judge0-workers
spec:
  replicas: 2    # baseline; KEDA scales this up to 50
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: judge0-worker
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: judge0-worker
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: worker
        image: judge0/judge0:latest
        env:
        - name: RAILS_ENV
          value: production
        - name: MAX_RUNNERS
          value: "2"
        - name: MAX_PROCESSES_PER_WORKER
          value: "1100"
        - name: SOURCE_CODE_SIZE_LIMIT
          value: "524288"
        securityContext:
          privileged: true       # required for isolate
        resources:
          requests:
            memory: "4Gi"
            cpu: "1000m"
          limits:
            memory: "4Gi"
            cpu: "2000m"
        volumeMounts:
        - name: cgroup
          mountPath: /sys/fs/cgroup
      volumes:
      - name: cgroup
        hostPath:
          path: /sys/fs/cgroup
      # Node must be EC2 (not Fargate)
      nodeSelector:
        eks.amazonaws.com/compute-type: ec2
```

```yaml
# keda-scaler.yaml
apiVersion: keda.sh/v1alpha1
kind: ScaledObject
metadata:
  name: judge0-worker-scaler
spec:
  scaleTargetRef:
    name: judge0-workers
  minReplicaCount: 2
  maxReplicaCount: 50
  cooldownPeriod: 300    # wait 5 min before scaling down (exam might still be running)
  triggers:
  - type: redis
    metadata:
      address: redis-service:6379
      listName: judge0:submissions
      listLength: "5"    # 1 new pod per 5 queued jobs
```

---

## Bottom Line

| If you want... | Choose |
|---|---|
| Simplest possible setup, single server | Docker Compose |
| Multi-node, AWS managed, no K8s expertise needed | **ECS (recommended)** |
| Multi-node, already have K8s team, 10+ services | Kubernetes (EKS) |
| Fargate (serverless containers) | ❌ Not compatible with Judge0 |

The privileged container requirement means Judge0 can never run on serverless container platforms. You're always dealing with real EC2 nodes, which makes ECS and Kubernetes roughly equivalent in infrastructure terms — the difference is purely in the management layer complexity.

Want me to write the production ECS task definitions + auto-scaling config, or the complete Kubernetes manifests with KEDA?

=== USER ===
F-01 (P0) — The Head Chef's Old Clipboard Can't Watch 1,000 Hatches
Setup the scene
When the Head Chef receives an order with 1,000 dishes, he hires 1,000 Junior Chefs (child processes via fork()). Each junior chef gets their own private pass-through hatch (a pipe) to send results back to the head chef.


Head Chef
│
├── Hatch 1  → Junior Chef 1  (cooking Dish 1)
├── Hatch 2  → Junior Chef 2  (cooking Dish 2)
├── Hatch 3  → Junior Chef 3  (cooking Dish 3)
...
└── Hatch 1000 → Junior Chef 1000  (cooking Dish 1000)
Each hatch has two ends — a push side (write) and a receive side (read). Opening 1,000 hatches = 2,000 ticket numbers used.

The head chef now waits. He uses his Old Clipboard (select()) to watch all hatches at once. When a junior chef finishes, they ring a bell through their hatch. The head chef checks the clipboard, sees which hatch rang, and collects the result.

Why it fails
The Old Clipboard was designed in 1983. It physically has only 1,024 checkbox slots. The clipboard cannot track ticket numbers above 1,023.


Old Clipboard:
☐ Hatch 1   (ticket 3)
☐ Hatch 2   (ticket 5)
...
☐ Hatch 511 (ticket 1023)   ← CLIPBOARD FULL. NO MORE SPACE.

Hatch 512 (ticket 1024) → HEAD CHEF SCRIBBLES IN THE MARGIN
Hatch 513 (ticket 1025) → SCRIBBLES OVER EXISTING NOTES
Hatch 514 (ticket 1026) → CORRUPTS HIS ENTIRE NOTEPAD
The scribbling over existing notes is memory corruption. The kitchen doesn't catch fire immediately — instead, random dishes get wrong results, or the head chef stands frozen waiting for hatches he can no longer see.

What the customer sees
"Time Limit Exceeded" for all 1,000 dishes. Or 511 dishes have results and dishes 512–1,000 are silently MISSING. The student has no idea why half their test cases disappeared.

The fix
Throw away the Old Clipboard. Give the Head Chef a Modern Tablet (poll()). The tablet has no slot limit — it can watch 1 hatch or 1,000,000 hatches with equal ease.


Modern Tablet:
☐ Hatch 1    (ticket 3)
☐ Hatch 2    (ticket 5)
...
☐ Hatch 1000 (ticket 2001)   ✓ No problem. Unlimited slots.


In this issue i want to know if we replace it with poll what may be the issues.

Now I have the exact code. The select() is in `_build_c_parallel_runner()` (line 286–342) and `_build_cpp_parallel_runner()` (line 454–506). Here are every real issue poll() introduces — all grounded in the actual generated code.

## If We Replace select() with poll() — Every Issue That Arises

---

## Quick Recap of the Switch

```c
// OLD — select()
fd_set _rfds;
FD_ZERO(&_rfds);
int _maxfd = -1;
for (int _i = 0; _i < N; _i++) {
    FD_SET(_fds[_i], &_rfds);          // ← crashes when fd >= 1024
    if (_fds[_i] > _maxfd) _maxfd = _fds[_i];
}
int _sel = select(_maxfd + 1, &_rfds, NULL, NULL, &_tv);

// NEW — poll()
struct pollfd _pfds[N];
for (int _i = 0; _i < N; _i++) {
    _pfds[_i].fd     = _fds[_i];
    _pfds[_i].events = POLLIN;
}
int _ret = poll(_pfds, N, timeout_ms);
```

Looks simple. But there are 6 places where this can silently misbehave.

---

## Issue 1 — EINTR: The Waiter Gets Tapped on the Shoulder Mid-Count

### What happens
Your harness already calls `alarm(ps + 2)` on line 287 (C) and line 456 (C++). When that alarm fires, the OS sends `SIGALRM` to the parent process. Both `select()` and `poll()` are **interruptible system calls** — any signal delivery makes them return immediately with:
```
return value = -1
errno = EINTR
```

### The current code's reaction (line 304 / line 472):
```c
int _sel = select(...);
if (_sel <= 0) break;   // -1 from EINTR → treated as timeout → breaks loop
```

This happens to work for select() because the alarm firing means we *should* stop. But with poll(), if a SIGCHLD arrives (child exits), poll() also returns EINTR — and the current break would abandon remaining children that may have already finished writing their results.

### The correct poll() handling:
```c
int _ret;
do {
    _ret = poll(_pfds, N, timeout_ms);
} while (_ret == -1 && errno == EINTR && !_global_tle);

if (_ret <= 0) break;   // real timeout (0) or _global_tle set by SIGALRM handler
```

---

## Issue 2 — POLLHUP Without POLLIN: Hatch Slammed Shut, Bell Never Rang

### What happens
`poll()` returns two different events on a pipe read-end:

| Event | Meaning |
|---|---|
| `POLLIN` | Data is available to read |
| `POLLHUP` | Write-end was closed (child exited), pipe is dead |

When a child **crashes before writing** (SIGSEGV, SIGKILL before the write() call), the pipe write-end closes. The kernel delivers `POLLHUP` — but **no POLLIN**, because there is no data.

### What your current poll() naive replacement would do:
```c
if (_pfds[_i].revents & POLLIN) {   // ← only checks POLLIN
    ssize_t _n = read(...);
    ...
}
// POLLHUP-only: _pfds[_i].revents = POLLHUP, not POLLIN
// → this if-block never fires
// → _done[_i] stays 0
// → _pending never decrements
// → loop hangs until alarm fires
// → TC reported as TLE instead of SEGV/ERROR
```

A crash becomes a fake TLE. The student sees the wrong status.

### The correct check:
```c
if (_pfds[_i].revents & (POLLIN | POLLHUP)) {
    ssize_t _n = read(_pfds[_i].fd, &_results[_i], sizeof(TCResult));
    // If _n <= 0: child died without writing — handle via WIFSIGNALED()
    // If _n == sizeof(TCResult): normal result
    // If 0 < _n < sizeof(TCResult): partial write (very rare but handle it)
    ...
}
```

---

## Issue 3 — POLLNVAL Infinite Spin: Pointing to a Demolished Hatch

### What happens
After reading a result from child `_i`, your code does:
```c
ssize_t _n = read(_fds[_i], &_results[_i], sizeof(TCResult));
close(_fds[_i]);          // ← fd is now invalid/closed
_done[_i] = 1;
```

With `select()`, this is safe — you rebuild the fd_set from scratch each iteration using `_done[_i]` as a guard. Closed fds never enter the next fd_set.

With `poll()`, the `_pfds` array is **static across iterations**. `_pfds[_i].fd` still holds the old (now closed) fd number. On the next `poll()` call:
```
poll() sees pfds[i].fd = 7 (closed)
→ returns immediately with pfds[i].revents = POLLNVAL
→ your loop processes it again
→ read() on a closed fd returns -1
→ loop tries again next iteration
→ infinite spin at CPU 100% until alarm fires
```

### The fix — one line after close():
```c
close(_pfds[_i].fd);
_pfds[_i].fd = -1;    // poll() contract: fd=-1 means "skip this slot"
_done[_i] = 1;
```

`poll()` guarantees: if `pfds[i].fd < 0`, it writes 0 to `pfds[i].revents` and ignores that slot. `-1` is the conventional sentinel.

---

## Issue 4 — Missing `#include <poll.h>`: The Tablet Was Never Ordered

### What happens
`select()` is declared via headers the harness already includes:
```c
#include <sys/time.h>   // struct timeval
#include <sys/select.h> // select(), fd_set, FD_SET, FD_ZERO, FD_ISSET
```

`poll()` needs its own header:
```c
#include <poll.h>       // poll(), struct pollfd, POLLIN, POLLHUP, POLLNVAL
```

Without it, the harness compiles on some systems (Linux glibc exposes poll via `<unistd.h>` as a side effect) and silently breaks on others. Judge0's sandbox uses a minimal Alpine or Debian image where this side-effect may not hold.

The fix is to add `#include <poll.h>` to `harnesses/c_harness.c` and `harnesses/cpp_harness.cpp`.

---

## Issue 5 — nfds Is Not max_fd+1: A Conceptual Difference That Kills You If You Mix Them

### What happens
`select()` and `poll()` interpret their "count" argument completely differently:

```c
// select() — pass the highest fd NUMBER + 1
// (internal: kernel scans fd 0 through max_fd)
select(max_fd + 1, &_rfds, NULL, NULL, &_tv);
//     ↑ THIS IS A FD NUMBER RANGE, NOT AN ARRAY SIZE

// poll() — pass the NUMBER OF ENTRIES in the pollfd array
// (internal: kernel processes exactly nfds structs)
poll(_pfds, N, timeout_ms);
//           ↑ THIS IS AN ARRAY SIZE
```

If someone porting this confuses the two and writes:
```c
poll(_pfds, max_fd + 1, timeout_ms);  // WRONG
```

And `max_fd` happened to be 1500 (with 1,000 TCs), `poll()` reads 500 entries past the end of `_pfds[N]` — **stack buffer overread**. The OS may deliver `EFAULT` or silently read garbage memory, producing POLLNVAL on phantom fds and causing random hangs or wrong results.

The correct call never changes — always pass `N` (the actual array size):
```c
poll(_pfds, {n}, ({ps} + 2) * 1000);
```

---

## Issue 6 — Timeout Unit: Seconds vs Milliseconds

### What happens
`select()` takes a `struct timeval` with seconds and microseconds:
```c
struct timeval _tv = { ps + 2, 0 };   // ps+2 seconds, 0 microseconds
select(..., &_tv);
```

`poll()` takes a **single integer in milliseconds**:
```c
poll(_pfds, N, (ps + 2) * 1000);   // MUST multiply by 1000
```

If ported as:
```c
poll(_pfds, N, ps + 2);   // WRONG — ps=2 → timeout = 2 MILLISECONDS
```

Every TC times out in 2ms. All 1,000 TCs get TLE. Student code is never actually run.

---

## Summary Table

| Issue | Symptom if not fixed | Severity |
|---|---|---|
| EINTR not retried | Some TCs marked TLE when they finished correctly | P1 |
| POLLHUP-only not handled | Crashes become fake TLE, wrong status | P1 |
| fd not set to -1 after close | Infinite CPU spin, all remaining TCs timeout | **P0** |
| Missing `#include <poll.h>` | Compile error on Alpine/Debian sandbox | **P0** |
| nfds confusion (max_fd+1 used) | Stack overread, random crashes/hangs | P1 |
| Timeout in seconds not ms | All TCs get TLE in 2ms | **P0** |

---

## What the Correct poll() Skeleton Looks Like

```c
/* Setup — once before the while loop */
struct pollfd _pfds[{n}];
for (int _i = 0; _i < {n}; _i++) {
    _pfds[_i].fd      = _done[_i] ? -1 : _fds[_i];  // -1 if pipe() failed
    _pfds[_i].events  = POLLIN;
    _pfds[_i].revents = 0;
}

/* Collection loop */
int _pending = {n};
while (_pending > 0 && !_global_tle) {
    int _ret;
    do {
        _ret = poll(_pfds, {n}, ({ps} + 2) * 1000);
    } while (_ret == -1 && errno == EINTR && !_global_tle);

    if (_ret <= 0) break;   // 0 = timeout, -1 = SIGALRM set _global_tle

    for (int _i = 0; _i < {n}; _i++) {
        if (_pfds[_i].revents & (POLLIN | POLLHUP)) {
            ssize_t _n = read(_pfds[_i].fd, &_results[_i], sizeof(TCResult));
            close(_pfds[_i].fd);
            _pfds[_i].fd = -1;                // ← Issue 3 fix
            int _st; waitpid(_pids[_i], &_st, 0);
            _done[_i] = 1;
            _pending--;

            if (_n != (ssize_t)sizeof(TCResult)) {
                /* POLLHUP-only: child died before writing */
                if (WIFSIGNALED(_st)) { ... handle SEGV/FPE/MLE ... }
                else { strcpy(_results[_i].status, "ERROR"); }
            }
        }
    }
}
```

All 6 issues are addressed here. Want me to implement this now in `harness_builder.py` and the template header files?

=== USER ===
which is better select or poll

=== ASSISTANT ===
**For your use case: `poll()` is better. No contest.**

Here's the complete comparison:

| | `select()` | `poll()` |
|---|---|---|
| Max file descriptors | **1,024 hard limit** (FD_SETSIZE) | **Unlimited** |
| API complexity | Rebuilds fd_set from scratch every loop | Static array, set fd=-1 to skip |
| Performance at 1,000 fds | O(max_fd) scan — checks fd 0 to 1999+ | O(n) — checks exactly your 1,000 entries |
| Timeout unit | struct timeval (seconds + microseconds) | Single int in milliseconds (simpler) |
| POLLHUP detection | Yes, via read returning 0 | Explicit `POLLHUP` event flag (clearer) |
| Portability | Works everywhere including Windows | Linux/Unix only |
| Age | 1983 (BSD) | 1986 (System V) |

**The only reason to use `select()` is if you need to run on Windows.** Judge0 runs inside a Linux sandbox — portability is irrelevant.

Your specific problem is exactly why `poll()` was invented. `select()` was written when 1,024 file descriptors per process was considered generous. `poll()` removed that assumption entirely.

**One level up — if you ever hit 10,000+ fds**, neither is ideal:

```
select()  →  poll()  →  epoll()
  1,024        ∞           ∞
  O(max_fd)  O(n)       O(ready)  ← only wakes on events, no scanning
```

`epoll()` is Linux-only and only wakes when something is actually ready (zero idle CPU). For 1,000 TCs all running in parallel for 2–7 seconds, the difference between `poll()` and `epoll()` is negligible. `poll()` is the right choice here — simpler than `epoll()`, no fd limit unlike `select()`.

Implement `poll()`. That's the fix.

=== USER ===
can you explain me what exactlty is poll() and how it is different from select()

=== ASSISTANT ===
## What is poll() and How is it Different from select()

---

## The Restaurant Setup (Same as Before)

You are the **Head Chef** (parent process).
You have **1,000 Junior Chefs** (child processes), each cooking one dish (one test case).
Each Junior Chef has a **private hatch** (pipe) to send the finished dish back to you.

Your job: **sit at the pass and wait for hatches to open.**

---

## The Old Way — select() (1983)

### How it works

The Head Chef uses an **Old Clipboard** with exactly **1,024 checkboxes**.

```
Old Clipboard (fd_set):

Checkbox 1  → watching Hatch #3
Checkbox 2  → watching Hatch #5
Checkbox 3  → watching Hatch #7
...
Checkbox 1024 → watching Hatch #1023

[ NO MORE SPACE ]
```

Before every wait, the chef:
1. **Erases the entire clipboard** (`FD_ZERO`)
2. **Re-ticks every active hatch** (`FD_SET` for each fd)
3. Hands it to the kitchen manager and says "wake me when anything ticks"
4. Kitchen manager scans **every slot from 1 to max** even if most are empty
5. Chef wakes up, **scans all 1,024 slots himself** to find which ones rang

```c
// Every single loop iteration:
FD_ZERO(&_rfds);                          // Step 1: erase
FD_SET(fd1, &_rfds);                      // Step 2: re-tick fd1
FD_SET(fd2, &_rfds);                      // Step 2: re-tick fd2
// ... repeat for every active fd ...
select(max_fd + 1, &_rfds, NULL, NULL, &timeout);  // Step 3-4: wait
// Step 5: scan all slots to find which rang
if (FD_ISSET(fd1, &_rfds)) { ... }
if (FD_ISSET(fd2, &_rfds)) { ... }
```

### The two fundamental problems

**Problem A — The clipboard only has 1,024 slots**

```
Hatch #1024 arrives.
Chef tries to tick checkbox 1024.
Clipboard only goes to 1023.
Chef scribbles in the margin.
Margin overlaps other notes.
Memory corruption.
```

This is `FD_SETSIZE = 1024`. It is **compiled into the kernel**. You cannot increase it without recompiling glibc. Hatches 512+ (fd ≥ 1024 because each hatch uses 2 fds) silently corrupt memory or get ignored.

**Problem B — Erase and redraw every single time**

```
1,000 hatches active.
TC 1 finishes. Chef wakes up.
Chef erases all 1,000 checkboxes.
Chef re-draws all 999 remaining checkboxes.
TC 2 finishes. Chef wakes up.
Chef erases all 999 checkboxes.
Chef re-draws all 998 remaining checkboxes.
...
```

Every loop iteration does O(n) setup work even if only 1 hatch rang. For 1,000 TCs finishing one by one: 1,000 × 1,000 = **1,000,000 checkbox operations** total just for bookkeeping.

---

## The New Way — poll() (1986)

### The core idea

The Head Chef throws away the clipboard. Instead, he gets a **permanent laminated board** with **one row per hatch**.

```
Laminated Board (struct pollfd[]):

Row 0:  │ Hatch fd=3  │ WATCH: data_ready │ STATUS: (empty) │
Row 1:  │ Hatch fd=5  │ WATCH: data_ready │ STATUS: (empty) │
Row 2:  │ Hatch fd=7  │ WATCH: data_ready │ STATUS: (empty) │
...
Row 999:│ Hatch fd=2001│ WATCH: data_ready │ STATUS: (empty) │
```

Each row is a `struct pollfd`:
```c
struct pollfd {
    int   fd;       // which hatch to watch
    short events;   // what to watch for  (you fill this in once)
    short revents;  // what actually rang  (kernel fills this in on wake-up)
};
```

### How a single poll() call works

**Step 1 — Chef sets up the board once (before the loop)**
```c
struct pollfd _pfds[1000];
for (int i = 0; i < 1000; i++) {
    _pfds[i].fd     = _fds[i];   // which pipe to watch
    _pfds[i].events = POLLIN;    // wake me when data is ready
    // revents = 0, kernel will fill it
}
```

**Step 2 — Chef hands board to kitchen manager and sleeps**
```c
poll(_pfds, 1000, timeout_ms);
// "watch all 1000 rows, wake me when anything happens"
```

**Step 3 — Something rings. Kernel fills in the STATUS column**
```
Row 0:  │ Hatch fd=3   │ WATCH: data_ready │ STATUS: 0        │  ← nothing
Row 1:  │ Hatch fd=5   │ WATCH: data_ready │ STATUS: POLLIN   │  ← RANG
Row 2:  │ Hatch fd=7   │ WATCH: data_ready │ STATUS: 0        │  ← nothing
...
```

**Step 4 — Chef wakes up and only checks the STATUS column**
```c
for (int i = 0; i < 1000; i++) {
    if (_pfds[i].revents & POLLIN) {
        // this hatch rang — collect result
        read(_pfds[i].fd, ...);
        close(_pfds[i].fd);
        _pfds[i].fd = -1;   // "cross out this row" — poll() skips fd=-1
    }
}
```

**Step 5 — Next iteration: board is already set up. No redrawing.**

The board persists. Only `revents` gets reset each call. `fd` and `events` stay exactly as you set them.

---

## Side-by-Side Walkthrough: 3 TCs Finishing in Order

### select() — 3 iterations

```
ITERATION 1:
  FD_ZERO (erase 3 slots)
  FD_SET(fd0), FD_SET(fd1), FD_SET(fd2)
  select(max_fd+1, ...)   ← kernel wakes when TC1 finishes
  FD_ISSET(fd0)? No
  FD_ISSET(fd1)? YES → collect TC1 result
  FD_ISSET(fd2)? No

ITERATION 2:
  FD_ZERO (erase again)
  FD_SET(fd0), FD_SET(fd2)    ← must re-add fd0 and fd2 from scratch
  select(max_fd+1, ...)   ← kernel wakes when TC0 finishes
  FD_ISSET(fd0)? YES → collect TC0 result
  FD_ISSET(fd2)? No

ITERATION 3:
  FD_ZERO (erase again)
  FD_SET(fd2)             ← must re-add fd2 from scratch
  select(max_fd+1, ...)   ← kernel wakes when TC2 finishes
  FD_ISSET(fd2)? YES → collect TC2 result
```

**Total setup operations: 3 + 2 + 1 = 6 FD_SET calls** (for 3 TCs)
**For 1,000 TCs: 1,000 + 999 + 998 + ... + 1 = 500,500 FD_SET calls**

---

### poll() — same 3 iterations

```
SETUP (once, before loop):
  pfds[0] = { fd=fd0, events=POLLIN }
  pfds[1] = { fd=fd1, events=POLLIN }
  pfds[2] = { fd=fd2, events=POLLIN }

ITERATION 1:
  poll(pfds, 3, timeout)   ← wakes when TC1 finishes
  pfds[0].revents? 0 → skip
  pfds[1].revents? POLLIN → collect TC1, set pfds[1].fd = -1
  pfds[2].revents? 0 → skip

ITERATION 2:
  poll(pfds, 3, timeout)   ← wakes when TC0 finishes
  pfds[0].revents? POLLIN → collect TC0, set pfds[0].fd = -1
  pfds[1].revents? 0 (fd=-1, skipped)
  pfds[2].revents? 0 → skip

ITERATION 3:
  poll(pfds, 3, timeout)   ← wakes when TC2 finishes
  pfds[0].revents? 0 (fd=-1, skipped)
  pfds[1].revents? 0 (fd=-1, skipped)
  pfds[2].revents? POLLIN → collect TC2, set pfds[2].fd = -1
```

**Total setup operations: 3 (done once before the loop)**
**For 1,000 TCs: always 1,000 setup calls, done once.**

---

## The events and revents Fields Explained

```c
struct pollfd {
    int   fd;        // "which hatch"
    short events;    // "what I want to know about" — YOU set this
    short revents;   // "what actually happened"    — KERNEL sets this
};
```

### events — what you ask to watch for

| Flag | Meaning |
|---|---|
| `POLLIN` | Data is available to read (normal case — child wrote result) |
| `POLLHUP` | Write-end closed (child exited, pipe is dead) — always reported even if not in events |
| `POLLOUT` | Ready to write (not needed here) |
| `POLLERR` | Error on fd (always reported even if not in events) |

### revents — what the kernel tells you happened

| Value | What it means in your harness |
|---|---|
| `POLLIN` | Child wrote its TCResult — call read() |
| `POLLHUP` | Child exited without writing (crash before write()) |
| `POLLIN \| POLLHUP` | Child wrote AND then exited — read() will get data, then EOF |
| `POLLNVAL` | fd is -1 or already closed — you set fd=-1 correctly, or you have a bug |
| `0` | Nothing happened on this row — skip it |

---

## The One Diagram That Shows Everything

```
select()                              poll()
────────────────────────────────      ──────────────────────────────────
Every iteration:                      Setup once:
  Erase clipboard                       Build pfds[] array
  Re-draw all active fds
  Call select()                       Every iteration:
  Scan all slots for FD_ISSET           Call poll()
                                        Scan only revents column
Hard limit: 1,024 fds                 No limit: 1, 1,000, 1,000,000 fds

Max fd must be tracked (max_fd+1)     Just pass array size (N)

struct timeval { sec, usec }          Single int in milliseconds

No "skip this slot" mechanism         fd = -1 skips the slot cleanly

Events and results mixed in           events (yours) and revents (kernel's)
  same fd_set — rebuilt every time      are separate fields — persistent
```

---

## One-Line Summary

> **`select()` hands a fresh notepad to the kitchen manager every single time. `poll()` gives the kitchen manager a permanent board and just asks him to mark which rows changed.**

`poll()` is `select()` without the 1,024 limit and without the erase-redraw overhead. For your harness with 1,000 test cases, it is the correct replacement.

=== USER ===
are we checking all 1000 test cases when status is being chaged for any test case in poll() are we again and again asking for the status of the test cases?

=== ASSISTANT ===
Great question. Yes — and here is exactly what happens.

---

## What poll() Does Every Single Iteration

```c
while (_pending > 0) {
    poll(_pfds, 1000, timeout_ms);   // ← passes ALL 1000 rows every time
    
    for (int i = 0; i < 1000; i++) { // ← scans ALL 1000 rows every time
        if (_pfds[i].revents & POLLIN) { ... }
    }
}
```

**Every iteration — yes, all 1000 entries go to the kernel. Every iteration — yes, your for-loop checks all 1000 rows.**

---

## But Here Is What Actually Happens Inside

### Iteration 1 — 3 TCs finished out of 1000

```
poll() passes 1000 rows to kernel.
Kernel walks through all 1000:
  Row 0:   fd=3,   fd is valid → watch it      → nothing ready → revents=0
  Row 1:   fd=5,   fd is valid → watch it      → READY!        → revents=POLLIN
  Row 2:   fd=7,   fd is valid → watch it      → nothing ready → revents=0
  Row 3:   fd=9,   fd is valid → watch it      → READY!        → revents=POLLIN
  ...
  Row 997: fd=1997, fd valid   → watch it      → READY!        → revents=POLLIN
  ...
  Row 999: fd=2001, fd valid   → watch it      → nothing ready → revents=0

poll() wakes up. Returns 3 (number of ready rows).

Your for-loop:
  Row 0:   revents=0      → skip (1 integer check)
  Row 1:   revents=POLLIN → collect result, close fd, set pfds[1].fd = -1
  Row 2:   revents=0      → skip
  Row 3:   revents=POLLIN → collect result, close fd, set pfds[3].fd = -1
  ...
  Row 997: revents=POLLIN → collect result, close fd, set pfds[997].fd = -1
  ...
  Row 999: revents=0      → skip
```

### Iteration 2 — those 3 TCs now have fd = -1

```
poll() passes same 1000 rows to kernel.
Kernel walks through all 1000:
  Row 0:   fd=3,   valid  → watch it
  Row 1:   fd=-1   ← SKIP. Kernel does nothing for this row. revents stays 0.
  Row 2:   fd=7,   valid  → watch it
  Row 3:   fd=-1   ← SKIP.
  ...
  Row 997: fd=-1   ← SKIP.
  ...
  Row 999: fd=2001, valid → watch it

Your for-loop:
  Row 1:   fd=-1 → revents=0 → skip (same 1 integer check as before)
  Row 3:   fd=-1 → revents=0 → skip
  Row 997: fd=-1 → revents=0 → skip
```

---

## So What Is the Real Cost?

```
1000 iterations (worst case: 1 TC finishes per wakeup):

  Kernel side:
    Iteration 1:   scans 1000 fds (all valid)
    Iteration 2:   scans 999 fds  (1 skipped)
    Iteration 3:   scans 998 fds  (2 skipped)
    ...
    Iteration 1000: scans 1 fd

    Total kernel scans = 1000 + 999 + 998 + ... + 1 = 500,500

  Userspace side (your for-loop):
    Every iteration: checks all 1000 rows
    But checking fd=-1 or revents=0 = single integer comparison = ~1ns
    1000 iterations × 1000 rows = 1,000,000 integer checks
    At 1ns each = 1 millisecond total
```

**1 millisecond of overhead for 1000 test cases running for 2–7 seconds each.**

Completely negligible.

---

## The Real Time Is Spent Here

```
Timeline for one poll() iteration:

T=0ms     poll() called, your process goes to sleep
          Kernel is watching active fds

T=0ms to T=2000ms   ← YOUR PROCESS IS SLEEPING
                      Zero CPU used
                      Children are running their code

T=2000ms  Child finishes, writes to pipe
          Kernel wakes your process
          poll() returns

T=2000.001ms  Your for-loop scans 1000 rows (takes ~1μs)
T=2000.002ms  Back to sleep in next poll() call
```

The 1000-row scan takes **microseconds**. The children's code runs for **seconds**. The ratio is 1:2,000,000.

---

## How Would You Eliminate Even That Scan? — epoll()

If you truly never wanted to scan all 1000 rows, the answer is `epoll()`:

```
poll()  → "here are 1000 rows, tell me which ones are ready"
          kernel scans all 1000
          you scan all 1000 to find ready ones

epoll() → "I registered 1000 fds, wake me and HAND ME ONLY the ready ones"
          kernel maintains internal list
          you only touch the fds that are actually ready
```

```c
// epoll() — only ready fds come back
struct epoll_event ready_events[1000];
int n_ready = epoll_wait(epfd, ready_events, 1000, timeout_ms);

for (int i = 0; i < n_ready; i++) {   // ← only loops over READY ones
    int fd = ready_events[i].data.fd;
    read(fd, ...);
}
```

```
If 3 TCs finish: n_ready=3 → your loop runs 3 times, not 1000.
If 1 TC finishes: n_ready=1 → your loop runs 1 time, not 1000.
```

---

## The Three in One Table

| | select() | poll() | epoll() |
|---|---|---|---|
| Setup cost per iteration | O(n) rebuild fd_set | O(1) — board already set | O(1) — kernel maintains list |
| Kernel scan per wakeup | O(max_fd) | O(active fds) | O(1) |
| Userspace scan per wakeup | O(n) FD_ISSET checks | O(n) revents checks | O(ready) — only ready fds |
| Max fds | 1,024 | Unlimited | Unlimited |
| Good for your 1000 TCs | No (fd limit breaks it) | Yes (overhead ~1ms) | Yes (zero scan overhead) |
| Complexity | Simple | Simple | More setup code |

---

## Bottom Line

**Yes, poll() checks all 1000 rows every iteration.** The kernel skips `fd=-1` entries quickly. Your for-loop checks all 1000 `revents` values — but each check is a single integer comparison taking ~1 nanosecond.

For your workload (children running for 2 seconds, 1000 TCs), the scanning overhead is **1 millisecond total across the entire harness run**. poll() is the right tool. epoll() would save that 1 millisecond but adds significant code complexity — not worth it here.

It's hidden because it starts with a dot. macOS hides all dotfiles by default in Finder.

**Show hidden files in Finder — two ways:**

**Option 1 — Keyboard shortcut (instant, toggle):**
Open Finder, navigate to your home folder, then press:
```
Cmd + Shift + .
```
All hidden files/folders (including `.claude`) will appear in grey. Press the same shortcut again to hide them.

**Option 2 — Terminal (permanent):**
```bash
defaults write com.apple.finder AppleShowAllFiles true
killall Finder
```

To hide them again:
```bash
defaults write com.apple.finder AppleShowAllFiles false
killall Finder
```
